 I paced my steps out the front door, across the crushed stone in the driveway and into my 1947 Chevy. The sun started going down as I pulled into the muddy driveway of Mr. Michek's farm. The two youngest girls came running out.
 "Hi, Vance!" they hollered. "Come in the house. Mama almost has dinner ready."
 The two story farm house had a big front porch. The faded white paint had started to peel. A swing hung on one end and children's toys lay scattered everywhere.
 Entering the front door, I smelled the aroma of roast beef. Mr. Michek greeted me inside and offered a seat next to an old radio. With a solemn look on his face, he listened to the world news out of New York.
 "Do you listen to the news on radio, Vance?"
 "No. But I listen to music. Stuff from Elvis, Gene Pitney, Roy Orbison."
 "I understand. I listen to Benny Goodman and recordings of the Glen Miller Band. But you should listen to the news too. Every day something happens in the world that might affect you."
 "I'll do that, Mr. Michek."
 He smiled. A big amiable grin and suggested, "Why don't you call me Kooba."
 "OK, Mr., uh, Kooba."
 From another room, Mrs. Michek yelled, "Dinner is on, everybody!"
 I followed Kooba into a large dining room. The walls, painted flat white, supported a tall ceiling. Their large oval table, covered with a frilly white tablecloth and surrounded by six brown chairs, dominated the room.
 Hot dishes of roast beef, boiled potatoes, green beans, cut loaves of bread and glasses of red wine adorned every square inch of table surface.
 "You can sit next to me," Kooba instructed, pointing to a chair next to the head of the table.
 As we gathered around, I noticed Anna coming down the stairs. She wore a black skirt with white blouse and around her neck hung two thin gold necklaces. A slim girl, her jet black hair fell straight to her shoulders. As she reached the bottom of the stairs, she stared at her mother.
 Mrs. Michek looked at Anna with a frown, "I hope you had a chance to think about your attitude. Go over there, next to your sisters and pass the bread."
 As she reached the far end of the table, we all sat down.
 Without warning, everyone bowed their heads and Kooba prayed, "I want to thank the Lord for this food and the company of our guest. Amen."
 Uncomfortable, I felt my face blush as I squirmed in my chair. Pop never gave thanks for anything. Also, I noticed that Kooba didn't give the sign of the cross at the end. Whatever his religion, it didn't appear Catholic.
 I felt relieved when Kooba said, "Let's eat."
 Kooba and Mrs. Michek started passing food around the table. The two youngest girls stared at me and giggled. Anna appeared to tune out the activity around her by picking at the food on her plate.
 I felt shocked when, without warning, she tried to have a polite conversation, "So, Vance, how do you like school so far?"
 "I don't seem to have enough time. Otherwise, I enjoy it."
 "Do you like working in a mine too?"
 "Well, sure. It takes a lot of hard work but I enjoy bootlegging."
 Mrs. Michek inquired, "Do you plan to work after high school?"
 "No. My Pop wants me to attend Penn State but?.it seems so far off."
 "Well, I hope you succeed at whatever you choose, Vance."
 "Thanks ma'am."
 The rest of dinner, we filled the air with light conversation about the farmers and trades-people we both knew in Lewistown Valley.
 As we finished desert, Anna fired a question at me with squinted eyes, "Did you know that my father has a crushed vertebrae in his back?"
 "Well, I, uh. No."
 Before I could say anything further, Kooba interrupted, "Stop it. Enough, Anna. Vance didn't come here to discuss my physical problems."
 Yelling, Anna shot back, "You promised, daddy! After the accident, you agreed not to go back there!"
 Anna bolted from the table and ran upstairs. Kooba excused himself and followed her.
 Silence overpowered the room, while I stared at the remains of what had been a nice dinner.
 After several moments, Mrs. Michek ordered, "Girls, start taking the dishes out to the kitchen."
 She looked at me with a kind but painful expression, "I apologize for her outburst. Anna has always been a sensitive girl. She adores her father and seems to feel a need to protect him ever since the accident."
 "I didn't know Kooba had a problem with his back."
 "He suffered extensive injuries in the North Tamaqua mine about six years ago. A shelf of coal broke loose and fell onto the loader he operated. Kooba couldn't work for almost a year. He doesn't like to talk about it."
 "Well, I never would have asked Kooba to go bootlegging, knowing his injuries."
 "My husband follows his own mind. No one can tell him what to do, not even me.
 "In any case, I'm glad you came tonight. We don't have guests very often."
 "Thank you for dinner. I should be getting home. It'll be a long day tomorrow."
 Mrs. Michek walked me to the door. As I stepped out on the front porch, she confided, "When our son married and moved to Philadelphia, Kooba struggled with depression. Since you started helping him, he has improved so much. He enjoys working again."
 After bounding down the steps, I countered, "Please tell Kooba I'll see him at the mine by daybreak tomorrow. Goodnight Ma'am."

Chapter 11
 Arriving home, I set my alarm clock for five. The next morning, I took care not to wake anyone, put on my work clothes and drove over to St. Mary's Mountain just as the sun started to rise. Tool box in hand, I started making repairs. A few minutes later, I heard Kooba's truck rattle up the bumpy trail.
 As he approached, I complained, "Kooba, this rusted equipment can't last much longer. God, some of this stuff has to be ten or twenty years old."
 "It'll last. Those steel couplings and cable are three inches thick. If we have to pay cash for something new, we'll cut copper wire from the telephone poles over at the abandoned rail spur in Middleport. Once we burn off the insulation, the Hometown salvage yard will buy it for thirty five cents a pound.
 We spent most of the day checking support beams, rigging, and cable equipment. Kooba lowered a pigeon down the shaft to check for toxic gases. I changed plugs, oil and most of all, installed a replacement drive shaft on the Ford. Kooba ran the ore bucket down the mine several times. Everything checked out fine. We decided to start mining the following weekend.
 Crisp fall weather signaled the turning color of leaves and their eventual flight to the ground. On the mountain, white birch trees stood out and gave the terrain a salt and pepper look.
 Bundled up with coats and gloves, we worked fast to stay warm and keep up with the increased demand for coal. Now, our customers demanded anthracite, not only for firing water boilers but also for heating homes.
 We always gassed up his truck at the station in Mary-D. As Kooba started the pump, a pugnacious looking teenager got out of an old Plymouth and walked my way.
 The boy, dressed in shabby clothes, growled, "You Vance Remke?"
 "Yeah, who wants to know?"
 He sneered, pulling back one side of his mouth to reveal yellow teeth.
 "You stole the driveshaft out of the old Hollister wreck."
 "I didn't steal anything. That's an abandoned car on public land."
 "Well, you're a thief and I want it back."
 "Forget it." I dismissed, walking back to the truck.
 Kooba yelled, "Look out!"
 I felt a burning sensation in my back then fell to my knees for a moment, trying to regain my senses. Curling my right hand into the hardest fist I could make, I sprang up and reeled around, hitting him solid in the face. He tipped over backward, hitting his head as he landed onto the cement.
 "I'll kick your ass!" he screamed while struggling to get up.
 Before I could react, my mentor smashed a fist into his stomach and the teenager doubled over in pain.
 Kooba leaned over him with an open hand pulled back, ready to unleash a painful smack to his face.
 "Listen, punk. You bastards from Tuscarora don't own everything in sight. Understand me? You just opened a bad peck of trouble, attacking my friend here."
 "Screw you!" the teenager shouted while he limped back to his car.
 "I'll be back with seven guys," he rasped, as he pulled out onto Route 209.
 Kooba roared back, "Oh yeah! Well, we won't be here! We don't have time to waste on the likes of you!"
 "You all right, Vance?"
 "Yeah," I groaned, rubbing my back. "Who the hell made him king of old auto parts?"
 "Nobody. We don't need to be concerned about him. Just a fool with bad attitude and not much else."
 Kooba finished filling the truck. After clinking the nozzle back on the pump, he pulled five dollars from his front pocket.
 "I'll settle up for the gas and we can get out of here before the dolt returns."
 We headed back to Tamaqua with a conviction neither of us had before. We worked hard and no one would deny us our success.
 In November, we checked the weather report every day. Waiting for the first major snow fall. The balmy days allowed us to work late into the year but only as long as the remote access road remained open.
 By Christmas, twelve inches of snow covered the area. We couldn't work over the holiday so I invited Reese to go skiing. I picked her up early for the drive to Promised Land Mountain in the Poconos.
 We parked at the base and unloaded our equipment. I took off my sunglasses and stared at the natural beauty of the mountain, untouched by the coal pirates that vandalized Tamaqua.
 With skis perched on our shoulders, we trudged through the drifts of Culver Pass. Half way up, Reese and I put on our equipment and wedge stepped the rest of the way to the start of the trail. We positioned ourselves at the rim and checked our gear.
 Reese observed, "This path seems wide open. Don't forget, we'll have to slow down at the base to get through the tree line."
 We pushed off with our ski poles and launched ourselves down the broad uncluttered white surface before us. With bent knees, I leaned forward, feeling the rush of air against my cheeks.
  Looking behind me, I saw Reese pushing her poles with unbounded intensity.
 We whooshed down the precipitous incline, gaining speed on the clean, fresh snow. Time, people and places seemed irrelevant. All thoughts disappeared as I absorbed the exhilaration of careening down the mountain at brisk speed.
 Our skis drew endless lazy Ss in the untouched white powder.
 Jolted out of my serenity, I saw the wide green line on the horizon. Another few minutes and we would reach the tree line near the base. We skidded to the left and right, trying to decrease our speed before we encountered the forty foot pine trees and uneven terrain.
 We bobbed and weaved back and forth, skis bouncing and twisting, trying to remain upright. My left ski must have hit a rock buried under the snow. I flipped high in the air, cart wheeling over and over. Snow dashed upward in a wide arc and settled in a light dusting, all over me.
 Reese must have spotted my blue jacket as I tried to sit up. She came straight toward me and then skidded to a complete stop.
 Pulling off her sunglasses and staring down at me, she stifled a chuckle, "What happened?"
 Moaning, I mumbled, "Damn rocks."
 Breaking out in laughter, she held out her hand and helped me get up.
 I checked out my arms and legs. Everything seemed to work okay.
 Offering a sheepish grin, I surrendered, "How about we head for the coffee shop?"
 Looking full of the devil, she challenged, "Race you there."
 We rounded up my skis and poles and whizzed down the rest of the mountain to the resort below.
 After stacking our skis on the car rack, we tromped up the rustic log steps to the restaurant.
 Pushing our way past the throng of holiday skiers, we found an open booth near the front window overlooking the immense mountain range.
 We ordered coffee and donuts, listened to Ricky Nelson on the juke box and enjoyed each other's company.
 Reese sounded concerned, "You took quite a spill."
 "Yeah, guess I came down the mountain a little too fast."
 "It's just like you, Vance. Always pushing the edge.
 "Anyway, thanks for bringing me up here. I haven't seen much of you lately."
 "Yeah, I've been busy, Reese. School and the mine."
 "I worry about you. Two people died in those mountains last summer. The Stoiber boy fell into an open strip mine and a cave-in crushed one of the Teklers. Look, the bakery still hires extra students for the holidays. The work doesn't pay much but no one dies there. Please consider it."
 "Don't fret. I enjoy being my own boss and accept the risk. Only a fool would take mining for granted. We check and double check everything. Besides, Kooba and I have a deal.
 Reese sat there for a moment, staring at me. She reached out and touched my cheek. Sadness replaced her smile, as I watched her eyes start to water.
 "I can't tell you what to do. You have to pursue whatever you think is right. But I see danger and for some reason, you don't. Please, just be careful."
 "Hey. Hey. I promise. I will."
 I kissed her hand as we looked at each other. Just Reese and me, as the world around us faded away.
                                                            ****
 My life seemed to have reached a plateau of normal pursuits. I busied myself with day to day rituals. Pop worked some long hours so we almost never saw him. Reese and I put our differences behind us.
 The holidays came and went. Winter passed and yielded to the first warm days of spring. But my existence had all the earmarks of shifting sand, reluctant to let my complacency last for long.
 After school, on a warm afternoon in May, I pulled my car up the driveway and strolled into our house. Silence thickened the air. No television. No radio. I could hear my own breathing.
 "Mom? I yelled, over and over."
 I hurried up the stairs and checked the bedrooms, nothing. I checked the garage, bathroom and laundry room, same thing. Panicking, I ran across the yard to the old house which Pop built in 1938. It used to be a rental but now doubled as a storage area. No sign of Mom.
 Although she would never visit the next door neighbors, I called them anyway. No one had seen her.
 My heart stopped pounding when I saw Pop's 1957 Chevy pull in the driveway. He would know what to do.
 I ran out the front door and met him in the driveway. Trying to control my fear, I yapped, "Mom's gone, I can't find her anywhere."
 "God damn it. Where the hell did she go this time?"
 "Should we call the police?"
 "Nah. We'll have to wait twenty four hours for that. I'll make some calls first."
 Pop and I headed for the phone in our living room. He pulled out his address book and started calling people in the valley. No one had seen her.
 Around five, we decided to take a short break and get something to eat. While we sat at the kitchen table, eating left over roast beef and potatoes, the phone rang. Pop answered it.
 "Hello? Effie? Okay. We'll be there in thirty minutes."
 Pop turned to me, "Your mother walked to Tamaqua. She doesn't want to leave Effie and Yeller's place."
 We put the leftovers away and drove into town. Yeller's place sat at the bottom of Poe Street. Nerves frazzled, we walked up to his front door and rang the buzzer. Effie greeted us with a look of concern then led Pop and me to the living room. Mom sat on an overstuffed, black couch with a small suitcase beside her.
 As we entered, she proclaimed, "I won't go home."

Chapter 12
 Embarrassed, my cheeks flushed red. I considered the problems in our family a private matter. Now, Mom involved Effie, Yeller's wife.
 Effie's living room looked beautiful. The walls, brilliant white and adorned with all sorts of elegant knick knacks. Horses, poodles and other assorted animals, all painted a glossy black. She backed up, as if not wanting to be in the middle of Mom and Pop's confrontation, about to start.
 Pop walked on thick black carpeting past their luxurious furniture, colored in what appeared to be a flat black lacquer. He stopped at a large coffee-table covered with numerous pictures, framed in gold.
 I thought of how different our living room appeared. Small, austere, with old, worn furniture on a cheap tile floor. 
 I walked to the couch and sat down beside Mom.
 "Please come home", I pleaded.
 She frowned, eyebrows arched, arms folded in front of her, "That depends on your father."
 Pop wore a poker face. Not exhibiting any emotion at all.
 Ignoring her statement, he demanded, "You've bothered Effie enough. Let's go."
 "No. You've got to do an about face on our life before I'll go anywhere."
 "I won't stand here and argue with you. We can settle this at home."
 She looked up at him for a moment and then rose to her feet, "Settle? You and I have wasted years trying to lead an orderly life. Instead, we cling to this fantasy, yet accepting less than happiness, less than respect.
 "You can't even admit to yourself that I need help. You let me sneak away to Maryland and see a psychiatrist. But you won't let me see anyone here. You fear what the neighbors will say. Poor Mrs. Remke. She went crazy, you know. Each day, I feel myself slipping away a little more. If you can't help me, let me go."
 Pop, unresponsive, just stood there. Rigid, like a stone wall. Silence flooded the room. I sensed that Mom and Pop reached an impasse.
 Effie interrupted, "Bernice can stay the night. You both need time to think. Walter, why don't you and Vance go home, get some sleep, and come back in the morning."
 Pop looked at Effie and shrugged his shoulders, "I apologize for you getting in the middle of this. I can put her up at the Majestic Hotel for the night."
 "No. I insist. You and Bernice have been our friends for a long time. I couldn't let her stay alone in a hotel at a time like this."
 Mom, looking less defiant, offered, "Thank you, Effie, for your kindness."
 I wanted to say something, anything. But this involved Mom and Pop, not me. Unlike before, she couldn't pick me up from school and leave for Schnecksville. As a teenager, I had my own life now. Besides, Mom no longer had access to the family savings account.
 As we paused at the front door, Effie warned, "Walter, she needs your support and understanding. Bernice seems adamant about leaving. I'll do what I can but the rest is up to you. Perhaps with a fresh start in the morning, you two can sort it out."
 I understood why Mom fled to the Swento's and felt helpless to do anything about it. My mother made me promise not to say anything about her years of turmoil.
 As we drove home, Pop cursed in a low voice, "Damn her. She'll find out what it's like to be on the street soon enough."
 Hearing him talk about her that way made every muscle in my body ripple with anger. I felt like a volcano, pressure building higher and higher, ready to blast the top off with a massive fury. I fought an internal battle with my conscience and pure animal rage. I couldn't hold it inside any longer.
 With madness shattering around me, I roared, "Why don't you leave Mom alone? You've made her suffer enough. "
 Pop recoiled and shot back, "What the hell do you know. Nothing. Less than zero. So shut up."
 "I know what you've done to her. She told me. Mom should have left you years ago."
 We careened down the road, yelling back and forth. Minutes later, Pop squealed into the driveway and mashed hard on the brakes.
 As we got out of the car, he ignored me and walked straight to the front door and unlocked it.
 "Admit it. You don't love Mom. If you did, she wouldn't want to leave. You failed her. She needs your help, damn it. Do something."
 With a stone cold voice, he grated, "You've had your say, boy. Now, I'll have mine. I'm not letting you in here tonight. You can sleep in the street for all I care."
 Before I could react, he walked into the house and locked the door behind him. My mind reeled with everything that happened, not only the last few hours, but my whole life. Visions of all the physical and mental abuse welled up like a massive tidal wave.
 Driven by raw emotion, I slammed my body against the door. Bits of wood and splinters exploded everywhere as the door-frame gave way. When I entered the house, Pop came toward me, a pipe clenched in his hand. With a vicious look on his face, he lifted it high in the air. Before he could strike, I ripped the weapon from him. For the first time in my life, I saw fear in his eyes. At that moment, I understood that I could never be like him and let the pipe drop to the floor.
 High on adrenaline, I rasped, "If you don't call the cops, I won't either."
 Without waiting for an answer, I turned to the stairs and walked up the fifteen varnished pine steps to the top. I looked down at Pop as he stared into space.
 I strode into my bedroom, turned on a light and closed the door behind me. I felt comforted, seeing things so familiar to me. My Tamaqua High School pennant, pinned to the wall. A portrait picture of Reese sat on an end table. Assorted black and white pictures of friends from high school, stuck everywhere at random angles. Magazine pages with photos of James Dean and Marilyn Monroe, ripped out and added to the collage.
 Without warning, I heard what sounded like someone pounding and cutting wood. Yes. Yes, of course. It had to be Pop repairing the door. My father, the perfectionist. He couldn't wait until morning.
 I could visualize him trimming out the jagged pieces of wood and fitting a replacement strip in its place. The mating of the door hardware would have to be flawless before he considered the work completed.
 As my emotions calmed, I tried to reflect on what happened in the last several hours. A line had been crossed. A position taken. But my brain just couldn't deal with it all. I started to feel exhausted from this irrational roller coaster ride.
 I sat on my bed, looking at the mementos that I had accumulated for my little world. Several minutes later, I laid down, my clothes still on. Eyes blinking, trying to stay awake. Sleep brought peace for a small amount of time.

Chapter 13
 Slumber dominated my existence until something jabbed my shoulder.
 "Vance. Wake up. Come on, boy. Get up."
 My mouth smacked of dry sand as I tried to focus. Pop stood at the head of my bed and sipped from a large white mug. My nostrils flared as I smelled the aroma of fresh brewed coffee.
 "Come on. Clean up. I called Effie this morning. We can get your mother in an hour."
 "She wants to come home?"
 "Yeah. I know. She wanted to leave this time, for sure. Well, let's face it, boy, she has nowhere to go. Her sister can't take her and her parents died years ago."
 He turned and left the room. I heard him walk downstairs to the kitchen. Rolling out of bed, I pulled off my clothes and stumbled to the door. With clean clothes under my arm, I marched to the shower. I lost sense of time as the steaming water sprayed down on me.
 Pop shouted from the kitchen, "We leave in thirty minutes."
 Annoyed at his deadline, I finished, dried and slipped into fresh blue jeans, white shirt and sneakers.
 Minutes later, I searched the kitchen cupboards and fridge for food. I made a bowl of cereal and poured a cold glass of orange juice. Hands loaded, I sat across from Pop, at the kitchen table.
 He read the morning paper, as if nothing had happened last night. I went about eating my breakfast.
 We sat in silence. What else could be said? I wanted Mom safe at home.
 Before long, Pop instructed, "Don't forget to wash your plate before we leave. No need to have your mother come home to dirty dishes."
 "Sure, I'll clean it, soon as I finish."
 How strange, I thought. Our family, on the verge of collapse and he wants the dishes clean. Yet, I sense a conciliatory tone in his voice. How can I understand this man, my father? Just when I've made up mind that he has no feelings, he bewilders me with his kindness.
 I finished my meal and walked to the white porcelain sink. Setting the dirty dish and spoon inside, I washed, wiped and stowed them in the cabinet.
 As I finished, Pop ordered, "All right, let's get going, boy. No need to keep your mother waiting."
 The muscles in my neck tightened as I prepared for the approaching trip to Effie's place.
 I forced a stiff smile and offered, "I want to bring Mom home, too."
 Pop straightened out the couch pillows as he approached the front door.
 "Scanning the rest of the room, he observed, "Okay, everything looks tidy. We can go now but we'll stop by the florist on the way."
 We closed the repaired door behind us and walked into the bright sunlight of a fresh new day.
 Optimism flooded my heart and mind. I thought of my parents reaching some neutral place, keeping the last few members of our family together.
 As we walked to Pop's station-wagon, I gazed at the world around me. The pine forest on the horizon to the south, the rich green mountain behind me and the valley in between. How could misery exist in such a place of tranquility?
 We climbed in and slammed the doors closed as we rolled down the driveway.
 Quiet dominated as Pop drove past the neighbors' houses and down the winding half mile past the city limits.
 Without taking his eyes off the road, he mumbled, "No need to tell your mother about last night."
 "Yeah. Sure."
 I wanted to put that ugliness behind me. My feelings grew nauseous every time I thought about the events that cast me on that violent path.
 We drove down Main Street, turned left toward the west end of town and pulled up in front of the florist.
 Pop fidgeted for a moment before divulging his feelings, "I blew off some steam last night. Matter of fact, I could never throw your mother out. Vance. I won't abandon this family, like your grandfather deserted me.
 I sat in shock. For sixteen years, I looked for a chink in his armor. Something that showed he loved us. This day had finally come.
 My father opened his car door and beckoned, "Come on, let's take a look inside."
 Pop walked to the counter next to a flower chiller at the far end. He smiled at the young girl busy cutting and arranging flowers.
 He ordered, "I want six roses, red ones, ma'am. Make them with short stems and fancy gold paper."
 The girl stopped her activities and walked to the large cooler. Pulling out six flowers, she snipped the stems to length and bundled them.
 He pulled a wad of bills from his front pocket and peeled off three dollars.
 With the roses tucked under his arm, he commiserated, "I hope these will help smooth last night's disaster. Let's get your mother."
 We headed back to Main Street and turned up Becker Hill toward Swento's place. We arrived several minutes later to find Mrs. Swento sitting on the front porch. Before we could reach the sidewalk, Effie, arms folded in front of her, walked with short, brisk steps to meet us.
 "Walter, before Bernice comes out, we should talk."
 Pop looked puzzled and questioned, "Okay, what has she told you?"
 Effie's face pursed in anger.
 "What happened to you, Walter? Yeller and I have known you for over forty years. But we don't really at all. Yes, Bernice and I talked about how you've been treating her. Now that I know, I'll be talking to Bernice often. She better not have to go through this ever again. For God's sake, help her. Okay, .I've said my piece. I'll let her know you're here."
 Effie walked into the red brick townhouse and returned several minutes later with Mom. She looked tired with dark circles under her eyes.
 I reached for the suitcase clutched under her arm and put it in the trunk.
 Pop opened the passenger door and looked down at his feet as Mom approached.
 With a sheepish grin, he offered the flowers and sighed, "For you."
 She took the flowers and stared at him with glaring eyes, saying nothing as she slid into the front seat.
 Effie walked up to the passenger window and blasted, "You call me anytime, day or night, for anything, you hear."
 Mom nodded and whispered, "Thanks for your kindness and understanding."
 I jumped into the back seat, feeling relieved that Mom decided to come home.
 As we drove away, I looked back at Effie, her face etched with worry, watching us as we disappeared down the street.

Chapter 14
 Mom and I lived a subdued existence as the weeks drifted by. We worked hard to stay out of Pop's way.
 After school, the mine dominated my life. On weekends, Kooba and I sold our bags of coal on every street of the northwest side.
I saw Reese during lunch and on Saturday and Sunday nights. We hung out at the soda shop, took in a movie or went roller skating.
 Early one morning, I met Reese and her girlfriends in the school lobby next to the basketball trophy case. As I approached, she seemed upset.
 "Vance. Have you heard the news about Ron Keler."
 "No. Did he finally take the day off and go to New York City? He's been talking about it for weeks."
 The tears rolling down her face told me something terrible had happened.
 "His parents found him last night on their farm in New Ringold. He drove a tractor into the foothills. They say he turned too sharp on a steep incline. The tractor rolled over and killed him."
 Dazed, my thoughts jumbled, racing. My stomach tied in knots.
 "Ron? No, it can't be. Our road trip to Fort Lauderdale. We have to go there next summer after we graduate."
 I wrapped my arms around Reese and grieved as students walked past us, oblivious to a world without my friend.
 The following day, school posted a notice on the bulletin board. Services for Ron Keler would be held at Zion Lutheran Church, New Ringold at six in the evening.
 Reese and I took off after school and drove through the gentle rolling countryside south of Tamaqua to the white clapboard church set in the middle of rich farmland.
 As we approached, a preacher dressed in black met us on the front steps.
 His brown hair, slicked straight back complemented a thick, bushy moustache and beard.
 He looked at us with compassion, a bible held tight between his hands.
 "Did you know Ron?"
 I responded, "Yes. We considered him a good friend."
 "Thank you for coming. Please take a seat inside."
 We walked past the large wooden doors to austere looking pews. A rustic cross and alter dominated the lectern.
 Other people sat in front, engaged in restrained conversation. Reese guided us to a pew in the back.
 As we sat down, I saw Ron's coffin. At that moment, I realized the finality of it all. I would never see him again.
 I watched as people continued to come in, filling all the seats, leaving only standing room in the rear.
 Several minutes later, the preacher walked up the aisle to the pulpit. The room turned quiet. All eyes turned to him. He opened the bible and gazed at the room filled with the lives that Ron must have touched.
 A broad array of people filled the pews. Men dressed in suits and others in overalls, my guess, having just finished their farm chores. Women in dresses with frilly Dutch embroidery. Students from Tamaqua High School.
 His attention focused on the front row for a few moments as he offered comforting words to Ron's parents.
 I listened with a heavy heart as he eulogized the sum existence of a boy and his pointless death.
 As relatives carried Ron to the waiting undertaker's limousine, we tendered our final goodbye.
 On the drive back to Tamaqua, Reese cuddled up to me. We felt no need to talk, taking comfort in just being there for each other.
                                                            ****
 Over the next two weeks, I prepared for the close of school and the beginning of summer.
 I completed final exams and turned in my books. Free until September.
 Kooba made arrangements to work for the Pennsylvania Power and Light Co. He hated spraying weed killer around high voltage lines. The fumes burned his lungs but they paid a buck seventy five an hour.
 As for me, Pop set up a construction job. A new appliance products factory in Pottsville.
 As we arrived at the job site on a Monday morning, Yeller shouted from the scaffold, "Hey! You sure have grown since last year. I'd guess at least a couple of inches taller and maybe even put on some muscle."
 "Yeah, I imagine so. I've been working part time at a bootleg mine. It keeps me in good shape."
 Pop kept busy with the inventory of people and materials before starting time. Then he headed to the construction shack to meet with the company manager.
 Yeller climbed down from the scaffold and walked toward me.
 "Effie tells me Walter made peace with your mother."
 "Yeah. They haven't killed each other, so far."
 "Good, no one can claim perfection, including your parents."
 "Why do you care so much about them?"
 "Your father and I fought a war together. Terrible things happened. To survive, we watched out for each other. That kind of thing bonds people for life."
 Our conversation ended when I heard shouting and ran to the construction shack.
 A loud noise resonated from the door as Pop slammed it with all his might.
 I felt uneasy as he walked toward us with clenched fists.
 "Those god damn Heims. They hold the contract for this construction job. The sons a bitches think they can bring in scab labor and cheat us out of our union wages. To hell with them, we'll make those bastards pay."
 Yeller chimed in, "Walter, you know those Heim brothers. They have more violations than any other contractor in the county. Don't mess around, let's take it to the union rep this morning.
 Pop rasped, "You damn right. Come on, we have some business at Union Hall."
 We piled into Pop's station-wagon with vengeance on our mind. The ride to downtown Pottsville took about fifteen minutes. Enough time to think about how the Heims won a bid on this factory.
 My father surmised, "Those good-for-nothing cheats. Of course. I should have seen it right away. The new Pennfield Industrial Park. If they bypass the union on this contract, they win every new job in the park by low balling the bids, using scabs. I won't let that happen."

Chapter 15
 We pulled up to the old four story brick building, home to Union Hall. Over the front door, a large bronze plaque with raised lettering proclaimed to the world - Union Local 317. We swaggered into the lobby and met a cute brunette seated behind the reception desk.
 Pop announced, "We need to see Stuey right away."
 "Mr. Mcbride is in a meeting until noon."
 "Look, we don't have time for this," Yeller sighed.
 She tried to stop us but with Pop leading the way, we kept walking all the way to a portal marked - Stuart McBride, Union President.
 Pop opened the large oak door and we barged in to see three men sitting around a table covered with documents, maps and drawings. Two of the men grabbed the papers and scrambled to stuff them into several briefcases.
 A barrel-chested man with a full beard and wrinkled suit, stood up and shouted, "Walter, what the hell! Have you gone mad? I'm in the middle of a meeting!"
 The other two men, dressed in expensive looking suits, stood up and excused themselves.
 My father raged, "Stuey, we need your god damn help. The Heim boys stole the contract on the Colabs plant by low balling the bid. Over half the bricklayers turned up scabs."
 Stuey inquired, "When did this happen?"
 "This morning. The construction super thought he could walk all over me. I almost slugged the bastard," Pop grated with indignation.
 "But you didn't. Good," Stuey retorted with confidence. "Bad publicity won't help us. We need the city council on our side."
Yeller questioned, "What do we do now?"
 Stuey proposed, "Walter, the men respect you. We need to rally all the union bricklayers to a meeting here on Saturday. Can you handle it?"
 Walter affirmed, "You can count on me."
 I felt inspired. This imperfect father had a side I never knew.
 Pop instructed, "Looks like you're going to work a telephone instead of a cement boat."
 Stuey left the meeting room. Several minutes later he returned with several boxes.
 "These files contain all the names of the rank and file members of Union 317.
 Pop grabbed a hand full of folders, sat down and started dialing. Yeller and I followed his lead, taking as many files as we could hold. Soon we immersed ourselves in a non-stop quest to call everyone before the Heim boys found out.
                                                            ****
On Saturday morning, Pop and I prepared to leave for the rally. I didn't want to attend but he insisted.
 "This rally concerns the union. I don't belong there, Pop."
 "Listen, these men will be looking for solidarity. The union wants them to go on strike. We need everyone there. That includes you."
 We drove west through Lewistown Valley past all the farms and thick forest to the south side of Pottsville. Arriving at Union Hall, we found the meeting place overflowing with people. Pop and I worked our way through the noisy crowd to the front. We joined Stuey, Yeller and the rest of the crew from the Colabs job.
 Several minutes later, Stuey cracked a heavy wood gavel on the table in front of him. "All right. Simmer down. Let's get this meeting started."
 I heard the noise level fall from a loud roar to silence. A sense of reverence fell over the hall. Those walls enclosed the best bricklayers, journeymen and apprentices in Schuylkill County.
 "The union appreciates your coming to the meeting this morning. A rogue contractor threatens all our jobs and we need your help. We have to take action. Now, I'll turn this over to Walter Remke."
 "Thank you Stuey. You know the Heim Brothers Construction Company and their reputation for shoddy work and bid rigging. Well, this time they committed the greatest sin. Scabs. They hired scabs for the Colabs project. They work for half the pay, sucking the strength out of everything the union stands for."
 "You want us to go on strike? You know, we have families to feed," yapped someone from the audience.
 "We can support a strike from the union fund for the next six months, if needed," Stuey chimed in.
 Pop asserted, "Look, the Heims and everyone like them threaten our way of life. The union has to take a stand and fight them. We need to strike."
 Without warning, the crowd sprang to their feet, chanting, "Strike! Strike! Strike!"
 A wave of excitement washed over me as I stood up and yelled along with the crowd. I had no idea what tomorrow would bring but looked forward to every minute.
 By morning, as the sun peered low over the horizon, Pop and I joined a throng of strikers in front of the job site. A cement slab, piles of cinder blocks, steel beams, bulldozers and back hoes dominated the lot. We formed a barrier to stop access to the work area.
 Several hours later, a black Lincoln Continental pulled into the driveway and the back door opened. A figure wearing tailored gray slacks, white shirt and aviator style sunglasses stepped out.
 "Well, if it aint old man Heim," Yeller grated with sarcasm.
                                                            ****
 I remembered the elder contractor from five years earlier when Pop and Buck volunteered to lay block on construction of the New England Fire Company. After finishing the building, local firemen held a bean soup supper for all volunteers and their families. Bitter resentment existed even then between Pop and Dobby Heim.
 During the meal, old man Heim walked to our table and belched his caustic bile, "My boys tell me you and Buck lay four hundred blocks on the fire station in one day. How come you never laid that many for me?"
 "Well Dobby, when you start paying union scale, we just might accommodate an equal measure of work."
                                                            ****
 My daydreaming ended when Dobby Heim approached Pop.
 "What the hell have you done, Remke?" the stocky, white haired man snarled, putting his hands on his hips.
 Pop declared, "You best leave now, Heim. The bricklayers' local 317 voted to strike as of yesterday. No one works here until we say so."
 The strikers yelled, jeered and cursed the old man. However, Dobby Heim looked unemotional, detached. He stood there, hands still on his hips, staring at each man on the line.
 "Do you think it ends here, Remke? You better start looking over your shoulder from this day forward. No one gets in my way for long."
 Pop seemed calm as he folded his arms and smiled. Dobby Heim sauntered back to his car and slammed the door. The union held the line. No one dared to cross.
 As the days passed, our efficiency improved. Everyone worked a rotating shift. Women made soup and sandwiches. Men set up picket signs and carried news from Union Hall. Our confidence bolstered by a lack of resistance from the Heims.
 Pop and I drew the afternoon shift. We arrived around four, walked the picket line, ate dinner, walked some more. As each hour passed, the sun descended over the horizon.
 Engaged in conversation, I didn't pay any attention to the old Ford pickup truck parked at the far end of the street. Without warning, it screeched onto the road, speeding past us.
 In the truck bed, two men holding what appeared to be shotguns, took aim. I heard a barrage of loud, short bursts like firecrackers and saw the flashing of gun fire. Deafening, soul piercing screams and a racing engine interspersed as I watched the truck disappear down Cider Road.
 Numerous strikers lay on the ground, moaning for help. I ran to one of the men riddled with buckshot. Tearing off my shirt, I wrapped his wound and applied pressure to stop the bleeding.
 Seconds later, I heard Pop yell, "Vance, beat it over to the gas station on Fifth Street! Call the police and an ambulance! Hurry!"
 My senses floundered as Pop limped toward me, his pant leg covered in blood. He collapsed on the ground, clutching his thigh, a look of intense pain on his face.
 "My God. You've been hit. I'll try to stop the bleeding."
 I removed my belt and started to wrap it around his leg.
 He grabbed my arm and commanded, "I told you to get moving, boy. Yeller can take care of this."
 I stood up, looking at Pop, not believing the catastrophe before my eyes. Blood started to pool on the ground beneath his leg. Part of me wanted to sass, No. I'm going to finish this. But I knew better. He never gave in. Never compromised. It had to be his way.
 So, instead of arguing, I scrambled to the car, parked two blocks away. Arriving, out of breath, my fingers fumbled for the keys.
 Come on. Turn the ignition, get it in gear. Go.
 Minutes later, I squealed into the only gas station for miles. Car still running, drivers door wide open, my feet pounded the pavement toward a phone booth. Shoving my way inside, I grabbed the weathered telephone book inside and flipped through the yellow pages.
 A swirl of violent scenes kept running through my mind as I made the calls. Hanging up, I took a deep breath, paused for a moment, then continued my frenzied pace back to Pop.
 I heard a low wail of sirens in the night as I pulled into Pennfield Industrial Park. People scurried about, talking, animated. I ran toward the throng of strikers.
 I roared, "Pop! Pop!" bobbing and weaving through the crowd. A familiar voice called out, "Vance. Over here."
Pop seemed unconscious, his body motionless, on the ground. Yeller bellowed above the chaos, "I bandaged Walter's leg but he lost a lot of blood. Damn it. We need a meat wagon, now."
 Frantic, I turned and ran to the middle of the street. The sirens continued to wail. They sounded close now.
 "Come on!" I screamed, looking for any sign of the ambulances. Pacing back and forth for what seemed like an eternity, at last they pulled in front of all the turmoil. I ran to one of the paramedics and grabbed him by the arm. "Over here."
 The paramedic checked Pop for vital signs and prepped him for transport to the hospital. They lifted him onto a gurney and rolled it into the ambulance. I climbed in and sat next to him. He looked so vulnerable. Not the man I feared for so many years.
 The siren warbled while a noisy two way radio barked information to the paramedics. I grabbed onto the edge of Pop's gurney as the ambulance pitched back and forth, picking up speed.
 When we approached the emergency entrance, his eyes opened and he tried to sit up. Leaning forward, I put my hand on his shoulder.
 "Don't worry. I'll call Mom once the docs look at you."
 In a raspy voice, he responded, "She doesn't need to know the details. Tell her I've been hurt. No more."
 I nodded in agreement, pushed back on the bench and breathed a deep sigh.

Chapter 16
 I lied to Mom about the bullet wound but not for long. News of the shoot out hit the local papers by next morning. When Mom and I arrived at the hospital, she scolded us like school children. I had to restrain my giddy mood because Pop didn't have the energy to fight back. He could only lay there, passive and compliant.
 Later in the afternoon, I took Mom home for lunch and came back alone. Pop dozed in and out of sleep while I sat in his room, reading a magazine. However, a knock at the door interrupted our comfortable solitude. To my surprise, Stuey's secretary walked in.
 "Hi. Remember me? Mona Banks from Union Hall. I'd like to talk with your father."
 I looked across the room. Pop seemed peaceful, having just taken a pain killer after lunch.
 "Can you come back later?"
 "Please, it can't wait. I have to do this now."
 Sensing urgency in her voice, I tapped Pop on the arm until he opened his eyes.
 "Pop, Stuey's secretary, Ms. Banks, wants to talk with you."
 He wiped his eyes and propped himself up with a pillow. Pop looked puzzled.
 "What can I do for you, Mona?"
 "I have information about Mr. McBride and the Pennfield Industrial Park."
 She sat in the chair next to his bed. After a long pause of silence, Mona pulled a file from her purse and handed it to Pop.
 "Stewart has been involved in the Pennfield Project since last year. The Heim brothers kick back five percent on every project they win.
 Pop looked shocked. He stared at Mona for a moment as if he didn't believe her.
 "He supported the strike. Helped us organize."
 "I believe Stewart gambled that everyone would accept the contract after the strike fund ran out. He never expected strikers to be shot or how determined they would be to win."
 "Why tell me?"
 "The men look up to you and I believe only someone they respect can end it. This whole thing went too far when you and the others could have been killed."
 She leaned over Pop's bed, pulled some papers from her purse and handed them to him.
 "The file should give you everything you need."
 He flipped through the documents, page by page. Pop's forehead wrinkled as he appeared to read with intense focus. Several minutes later, he nodded.
 "This looks bad."
 Ms. Banks questioned, "Will you call the police?"
 "Yes, this afternoon."
 Mona reached over and touched Pop's arm.
 "Good. I come from a family of bricklayers. I want them safe."
 "I'll help anyway I can," Pop assured.
 She added, "Mr. McBride has a meeting with Heim Construction Company tomorrow morning. He'll pull this file from his personal safe before the conference. I'll need to put it back before then."
 "You can have it this evening after we copy the information."
 "Thank you, Mr. Remke and good luck."
 She stood up and walked out the door.
 "I need pen and paper," Pop instructed. "Go down to the gift shop and pick up one of those ruled tablets and a good pen. Not a cheap, foreign throw-away. And before you go, bring that phone over here."
 Pop continued to read the file as I positioned the telephone next to him. Then I hoofed it downstairs to the gift shop. Upon my return, I found him talking on the phone, his demeanor, serious and intense.
 "Yes. I have the file with me. Then you'll send someone over this afternoon? Okay. St. Joseph's Hospital, third floor, ask the nurse for my room. Yes. Okay. Goodbye."
 He hung up, exhaled a long, deep breath then lay back in the bed.
 "The police want to look at the file. You'll have to copy it. I can't. Too exhausted." 
 He handed the brown manila folder to me and closed his eyes. I started copying the pages, detailed with project names, dates and dollar amounts. After several hours, I finished, closed the file and neatly stacked the sheets of lined paper on top.
 Standing up, I stretched, walked the floor for several minutes and later relaxed in the lounge chair next to Pop's bed. Just as my eyes started to close, a tall man tapped on the door-frame. He took several steps into the room and showed a badge.
 "Pottsville Police. I need to talk with Walter Remke," the dark, square jawed, man declared.
 Pointing to Pop, still asleep, I shot back, "He's been expecting you."
 With a commanding voice, he yapped, "Mr. Remke. Mr. Remke. We need to talk."
 As Pop stirred from his afternoon nap, the officer looked at me and in a no-nonsense tone apprised, "You'll have to leave."
 Looking at Pop, I suggested, "I'll be in the visitor's room."
 An hour dragged by as I watched local news and Kate Smith on television. My patience exhausted, I walked back and sat in the aisle just outside Pop's room. A constant mumbling emanated from inside. Anxious, I placed my ear against the door.
 "Do you think Ms. Banks had any involvement?"
 "I don't know. She seemed concerned. My gut says she just wants justice."
 "Well, until we know for sure, one of my men will tail her."
 "What about the file?" Pop questioned.
 "Give it back and tell her you've talked with us. Nothing more. I'll keep the copy as evidence."
 "What else do you need?"
 "I can't think of anything. With the file, Ms. Banks as a witness and your testimony we have probable cause to search Heim's office tomorrow. I'll let you get some rest."
 Silence flooded the air. Sudden anxiety hit me when I realized they had finished. In a panic, I stumbled to a chair just as the officer opened the door. He stopped for a moment and looked straight at me. I felt flustered and hoped it didn't show. Squirming, I tried to look uninvolved.
 In a husky voice, he inquired, "You Walter's boy?"
 "Yes sir, the youngest."
 "Well, take good care of him. He needs rest. You can go in now."
 "Thank you, sir."
 I watched the officer disappear down the hall before walking into Pop's room. He had propped himself up with a pillow and looked alert.
 "They serve dinner in twenty minutes. Do you want to join me or go home?"
 I looked at the clock on the wall. Twenty minutes to five. My curiosity at a high pitch, I wanted to be with Pop when Mona came back. So I opted to stay.
 "The hospital food tastes bad but I'll join you for dinner anyway, Pop."
 "Fine by me. Grab a deck of cards from the end table. We can have a quick game of duce's wild."
 Pop and I played until supper came, ate meat-loaf, mashed potatoes and Jello. Shuffled some more cards. Read magazines. Just before eight, we heard a knock at the door. Pop motioned to Mona as she walked in. She fidgeted, wiping strands of hair behind her ears.
 Mouth pulled tight and eyebrows arched, she questioned, "Did you talk to the police, Mr. Remke?"
 "Yes. This afternoon. I told them everything."
 "Did they say anything about me?"
 "The police want to talk after you return the file. No matter what happens, just tell them the truth. It took a lot of courage to do the right thing."
 Pop reached over to the end table and opened the drawer. He grabbed the file and handed it to her.
 "You best return this right away."
 "I'll put it back early tomorrow. Mr. McBride doesn't come in until nine."
 "Well. You stay safe. This will all be over soon."
 "I hope so. God bless you, Mr. Remke. Goodbye."
 She retraced her steps out the door and into the hall. Pop did all he could. Now, everything depended on her.
 We played another game of cards but my mind drifted elsewhere. Wondering how tomorrow would unfold. Around nine, as the last remnants of sun disappeared, I prepared to leave for the drive home.
 "Do you want me to bring anything back?"
 "Yeah, a fresh change of clothes. I'm going home tomorrow."
 "But you just got here. Did the doctor say you could leave?"
 "You just do what I told you, boy. I'll decide when to leave, not the doctor."
 An old feeling started to seep back into my consciousness. The one where I raise my mental shield. To protect me from him. We had been through so much the last few days. How foolish to think that anything changed.
 "Mom will have something to say."
 "Now boy, you leave her the hell out of this. Just go."
 I held myself back inside. A part of me wanted to say more but experience tempered my response. He had a will of iron that couldn't be moved.
 "Okay. I'll be back tomorrow morning. What time?"
 "The administration people come in at eight. Be here about then."
 The drive home raced by as my mind filled with thoughts of Mona. So many events hinged on her. Lives would be changed. And Pop. Will he ever walk the same again?

Chapter 17
 Arriving home, I found Mom sitting in the living room watching television, a book nestled in her lap. She looked at me with anticipation. I forced a smile, holding back my frustration with Pop.
 "Your father okay?"
 "Yes. He slept at lot. Except when he had visitors."
 "You look tired, dear. Can I fix you something to eat?"
 "Nah. I ate dinner at the hospital."
 She opened the novel and started to read. I plopped onto the couch and tried to watch television. After several minutes, unable to sit still, I confronted Mom with my anxiety.
 "Pop wants to come home and he doesn't care what the doctor says. Can he do that?"
 She stopped reading and stared at me for a moment. A tight lipped smirk on her face.
 "Yes. If he can get out of the bed."
 "I don't know but he appears to believe it."
 "Well, I can't stop him. Wouldn't try."
 Pop didn't follow the rules that most people live by. Mom accepted it and although reluctant, I did too.
 The events of the day drained me. My eyelids felt heavy and could only think about going to bed.
 "He wants me at the hospital by eight. Can you pick out something for him to wear?"
 "Of course. And you don't have to face him alone. I'll go with you."
 "Thanks, Mom."
 "You should get some sleep."
 "Yeah. Wake me at six. Okay?"
 "I'll have the coffee on."
                                                            ****
 The morning sun ascended over Blue Mountain as we arrived at the hospital. The start of another hot day. A few people sat in the lobby near the admissions office. Mom carried a paper bag filled with clean clothes. Stepping off the elevator, we ambled down the hall. Reaching Pop's room, through the open door, we saw him yelling.
 "Look, girly girl, you get my doctor. Right now."
 The nurse responded, "But Mr. Remke, I can't. He doesn't start until nine."
 "Well, I'll be gone by then."
 A thick knot formed in the pit of my stomach. Feet unable to move, I didn't want to enter that room.
 My mother, jaw thrust forward, eyes squinted and the demeanor of an attack dog, marched inside.
 "Go ahead. Beat her. Strike the nurse just like you hit me. See how far you get."
 The nurse looked shocked and bolted for the door, running past me. My body tensed, waiting for Pop to react.
 Mom thrust the paper bag at him and stood by the bed, her posture aggressive.
 "You can't just ram rod your way out of here. Have you even tried to walk?"
 "You stay out of this. I have a strike to settle and it can't wait. Not for you. Not for this hospital. Not for anyone."
Pop yanked his clothes out of the bag, stared at me for a moment, then waved his finger upward. "Close the curtain, boy."
 Mom stood by the door as I encircled his bed with the blue checked drape. Cursing and thrashing sounds emanated from behind the sheet for several minutes. Then a moment of silence followed by an abrupt, "Okay, let's get the hell out of here."
 I pulled the curtain back and found Pop rolling up the sleeves on his khaki shirt. Several minutes later a doctor entered the room. He walked to the clipboard hanging at the foot of the bed and started to read the chart, a serious look on his face.
 "The nurse tells me you want to leave this morning. Mr. Remke, your wound will take at least four weeks to heal and you'll need physical therapy. Without proper care, you risk gangrene or a limp for the rest of your life."
 "Doc, you have a job to do. Me too. The biggest labor scandal this town has ever seen will hit the streets this morning. I'll be with my men on the picket line when it happens."
 My eyes watered as I fought to hold back a tear. Men don't show their feelings. But pride overwhelmed me.
 The doctor, who seemed so sure of himself a moment earlier, now seemed at a loss for words. He looked at my father in awe, nodded his head and scribbled something on the chart.
 "Your bandage must be changed every day. Your worst enemy right now is an infection. I want to see you twice a week for the next four weeks. Can you do that?"
 My father, less defensive, assured him, "Yes. Thank you."
 The doctor and Pop shook hands. A tone of mutual respect permeated the air.
 "The nurse will bring crutches, bandages and some papers to sign. Good luck, Mr. Remke."
 With the efficiency of a drill sergeant, the doctor completed his writing and marched out of the room and down the hall. Mom and I gathered Pop's few belongings. As we waited for the nurse, Yeller dropped by with an update on the strike.
 "Our men held the line for the last two days, Walter. They want revenge, for you and the others. We can win this strike, no matter how long it takes."
 Pop confided, "We have the law on our side, unlike Dobby and his sons. And perhaps we'll see them get what for, today. The cops plan to raid Heim's office. I believe they'll find plenty of evidence to put them away for a long time.
 "I also have bad news. The Heims got to Stewie, damn it. He took their money. Now, he'll go down with those bastards."
 "Not Stewie. All those years. He built the union from nothing," Yeller lamented, shaking his head.
 All my nerves stood on edge, alert and ready for anything. Like waiting for a disaster to happen. I had to stand with Pop when he went back to the strike.
 The nurse brought a wheel chair for his exodus from the hospital and fitted him with crutches. With caution, he propelled himself from the edge of the bed to an upright position on the walking sticks. After several awkward minutes, he became comfortable with his new wooden companions.
 Pop had an entourage joining him as we left the room. Not only Mom, Yeller and me but also several tall, muscular guys, waiting in the hall. Strikers, according to Yeller. As we passed the attendant's station, the doctor and nurses took time out from their duties to see him off. Then down the elevator, through the lobby and out the front door.
 Pop cursed under his breath as he struggled out of the wheelchair. After settling into the passenger's seat, I heard him whisper to Yeller.
 "Would you give Bernice a ride home? Too much going on today."
 "Sure, Walter. I'll meet you at the Colabs building."
 "Thanks, Yeller."
 Pop closed the door and we pulled out of the hospital parking lot. Looking in the rear view mirror, I noticed the brawny strikers following us.
 My curiosity aroused, I inquired, "Do you work with those men?"
 "Don't worry about them, boy. They just want to make sure nothing happens to me."
 The drive through Pottsville seemed uneventful. People went about their lives, not knowing the law would soon punish several bad men.
 Arriving at the Colabs job site, a feeling of excitement coursed through me, seeing all those strikers. More than a hundred men strong, committed to a single purpose. Protecting their way of life.
 Parking near the crowd, I pulled Pop's crutches from the back seat. He opened his door and maneuvered himself in an upright position. Some strikers noticed Pop and flocked around him. A brother. A kindred bricklayer, come home.
 Like a fire running fast through a forest, strikers stopped talking and looked Pop's way. A few started clapping. Then more. Soon everyone. Pop worked his way through the crowd, shaking hands, talking with the men.
 My eyes followed him, absorbing every moment. This man, respected and admired by so many. My father.
 After several minutes, Pop raised his arm.
 "Thank you, thank you."
 He paused, as the din of the crowd died down.
 "Thank you, brave men of local 317. The Schuylkill Haven crew just told me great news. Their two boys should be out of the hospital in the next few days."
 The strikers let out a roar. The intensity so loud, it echoed through the valley.
 "If everything goes as planned, this strike will end soon. The Heim clan will no longer have the power to control new construction in Schuylkill County."
 Before Pop could say anything further, a police car pulled in front. I recognized one of the detectives from the hospital. He stepped out of the vehicle and walked toward us.
 "The hospital told me you'd be here, Mr. Remke."
 "Yeah. They did a good job of patching me up. What about the raid?"
 "We plan on taking McBride and Heim into custody this morning. If we hurry, you can watch them being transported to county jail.
 A broad smile came across Pop's face.
 "I'd like that."
 "Follow me. I'll get you through midtown traffic."
 Without hesitation, Pop hobbled toward my car.
 "Come on boy. We've got to get moving. Follow that police car."
 My heart started to race. Adrenaline pumped. Hands sweaty as I ran to my car. Pop not far behind. Seconds later, I pulled behind the black and white. Without warning, the police car screamed down the road, siren wailing. Groping for first gear, I rammed the gas pedal, struggling to keep control.
 "Stay with him, boy."
 "Yes sir. I'll do my best."
 My fingers curled tight around the steering wheel. Eyes focused only on the black and white. We raced down narrow streets and through red lights. An eerie whizzing sound filled my ears as we passed within inches of cars, parked on the side.
 Reaching the outskirts of town, we pulled onto the freeway. I watched the speedometer quickly climb to 90, 100, 110 miles an hour. I sensed the old car being stressed to its limits. A new kind of fear raced through my mind. At this speed, could we survive an accident?
 Fifteen minutes later, we slowed to enter East Pottsville Office Park. Every muscle in my body unwound as we drove several blocks into the campus. Building after building looked new and expensive. Suddenly, we saw several police cars with lights flashing. The black and white pulled in right behind them. The officer jumped from his vehicle and ran into one of the suites.
 "Pull over right here, boy. We'll walk the rest of the way."
 Pop shuffled from the car to the sidewalk. I paced my steps so Pop could keep up. A small crowd of passers-by formed around the police cars and the entrance to Heim Brothers, Construction Company.
 The minutes dragged by as we waited for someone to emerge from the front door. Finally, several policemen came out. Then, one by one, four men in handcuffs came out, each escorted by a policeman.
 I recognized Mr. McBride, head down, trying to hide his face from the crowd. Then old man Heim, looking angry, tugging against the grip of the policeman.
 Pop forced his way to the edge of the crowd as Heim walked toward the police car.
 "You lose, you son of a bitch. The men of Union 317 get their lives back today," Pop declared, as he grabbed old man Heim's arm.
 Dobby drew back against Pop's grip and could only muster a blank stare as the cop pushed him forward.
 The policeman who visited Pop in the hospital walked behind the last two men, both wearing handcuffs. The cop paused by my father, "These two Heim boys just confessed to the drive by shooting on the picket line. Facing attempted murder charges, they won't be out anytime soon."
 Pop beamed, "Thank you for bringing the Heims to justice."
 "Glad to help. Take care of that leg."
 Pop nodded as the Heim boys entered one of the police vehicles. We stood there until the last car faded from sight.
 My father declared, "Let's head back to the strike, boy. We have good news to deliver."
 The return drive seemed uneventful, until we arrived at the line. A van from the Pottsville television station parked near the picket line.
 Pop observed, "Looks like word about Heim reached the street."
 My curiosity ran high, as we walked toward a cluster of people, animated in conversation. I spotted Yeller talking to a well dressed woman. She held a pad and pencil, writing lickety-split. Yeller waved to us.
 "Come here, Walter. You can fill this young lady in on what happened this morning."
 "How did you uncover this scandal, Mr. Remke?"
 She recorded Pop's every word as the men formed a circle around them. I stood back and watched as this woman documented places, times and events. History would remember Pop for his integrity and commitment. But I saw through the aura enveloping my father. A man both heroic and oppressive. How will I ever reconcile my feelings with the two sides of this man? I can't.
 With our bricklayer's battle over, the men packed up signs, coffee pots, and clothes, getting ready to head home. While the afternoon wore on, Pop, Yeller and a small number of strikers remained, talking of plans for a new union president and protecting the integrity of the membership.
 Walking to my car, I waited for them to finish. As I started to drift into a nap, Pop opened the door and slid into the passenger's seat. He reached into his shirt pocket and pulled out a pack of Chesterfield cigarettes. Snapping a smoke from the pack, he lit up and took a long draw.
 Leaning back in the seat, he enlightened, "Just received word from Mona at the union. Colabs cancelled its contract with Heim Brothers Construction Company today. It goes up for bid tomorrow. With any luck, we'll start work on Monday. We've had one hell of a day. Let's go home, boy."

Chapter 18
 The rest of the week, I talked about the strike to anyone who'd listen including the milk man and postal carrier. Celebrating my short term unemployment, I slept late, washed my car, hung out with friends at the 5 Point Diner.
 Saturday night, I invited Reese to Route 309 Bandstand. As we entered, I noticed girls lined up against the back wall and guys milling about, working up the courage to ask someone to dance. Couples on the dance floor embraced, swaying to a slow ballad. The lights, low and colored, complemented the cool music which resonated throughout the building.
 "Dance with me, Reese."
 "Not right now. Can you bring me a soda?"
 "Sure. I'll be right back."
 As I walked to the pop machine, someone behind me grumbled, "Vance Remke."
 I spun around to see Kooba's daughter, Anna, marching toward me. A hot, damp sweat crept across the back of my neck. She never stopped blaming me for bootlegging with her father.
 I looked for Reese. But she had walked to the other end of the building, engaged in conversation with her girlfriends.
 Anna stopped just short of my nose and looked straight at me through squinted eyes. Her breath hinted of cinnamon, my guess, from the gum she smacked between her teeth.
 "I don't like you, Remke. So I'll only say this once. Just in case you didn't get the message when you came for dinner. Stay away from my father. He doesn't need you pulling him back into one of those damn mines."
 My forehead wrinkled, eyebrows arched high, anger backed up in my throat.
"You don't understand. Or don't want to. He mines coal because he loves it. Not because of me. Accept it or not. I don't care. Now, have you finished?"
 "No. I don't think so. We'll settle this soon enough, one way or another."
 She turned and disappeared into the throng of high schoolers.
 Walking to the Coke machine, I juggled in my pocket for a dime and fed the metal contraption. A cold bottle of pop thumped out the bottom. I snapped off the cap and headed across the dance floor, dodging the bodies swaying back and forth to the music. Still angry, I thrust the bottle in Reese's hand.
 "You look upset. What happened?"
 "Anna Michek. She has a problem, with me. Her concern, not mine."
 "Do you want to talk about it?"
 "Nah. Just something I have to live with."
 She smiled, leaned forward and kissed me on the lips. Not a peck but firm, moist and exciting.
 "In that case, I'd like to dance now."
 "Okay. I can't wait any longer either."
 A slow song saturated the air as I grabbed her hand and walked backward to the dance floor. Reese passed her soda to a girlfriend.
 Sliding into my arms, her smooth skin touched mine and aromatic perfume filled my nostrils. She laid her head against my shoulder. I closed my eyes and absorbed the ecstasy of the moment. Breathing slowed and deepened. Bodies pulsed as one to the harmonic sounds. I wanted it to last forever but too soon, it ended. With reluctance, we left the dance floor and joined her girlfriends.
 We jitterbugged, talked and laughed, savoring every moment of each other's company. As the evening came to a close, we walked to the front door and said goodbye to our friends.
 Out on the sidewalk, stragglers milled about, talking, waiting for rides home. Without warning, the crowd scattered for a shadowy figure, cursing, goose stepping, heels clicking his way toward us. Under the illumination of a street lamp, I recognized Ace Borman.
 This useless example of the human race never graduated from high school. He seemed dense as a plow mule and looked just as ugly. Now in his twenties and rejected by the Army, everyone knew him for an unemployed nothing.
 "Remke. You don't listen so good. Anna told you to back off. Now, I gotta kick your ass."
 My muscles, thick and bulked, tensed like rubber bands and massive fight or flight juices rammed head long through my veins. With my left hand, I forced Reese to one side. The crowd backed off, leaving me face to face with this animal. His breath reeked of sour beer.
 "Since when does Anna tell you what to do?"
 "Shut up! She belongs to me!"
 "Well, my beef is with Anna, not you."
 "Doesn't matter. Gonna happen."
 He lunged at me with his right fist. I pulled back. Not fast enough. Blood dribbled down my chin. Lip numb. Anger. No, rage.
"Aggggggh!"
 Lunging at him full force, left arm deflecting his next blow, I bashed my fist squarely into his face. I heard the squash of flesh hitting flesh. Blood spattered a bystander.
 Borman recoiled out of control but stayed on his feet. Having lost the advantage of surprise, I dodged every swing he took until he lost his head of steam.
 Stepping forward before he could recover, I kicked upward and smashed my foot into his head. He fell to the ground, his chest heaving up and down. I pulled back my fist, ready for his next move but he just lay there, holding an arm over his face.
 "Enough. I've had enough."
 My senses ran wide open like an engine in neutral but screaming at full throttle. I fidgeted, ready to strike at any second. A gentle hand grabbed my arm. I felt Reese's warm body next to me.
 "Let it go, Vance. He can't hurt you."
 My breathing slowed and I started to feel the throbbing in my mouth. Reese dabbed at my lip with a tissue.
 With a disgusted look, she irked, "Let's get out of here."
 The crowd started to disperse as I looked down at Ace Borman, lying in a heap on the sidewalk.
 Not expecting an answer, I scoffed, "Guess we have nothing left to talk about."
 Wrapping my arm around Reese, we walked into the night.

Chapter 19
 Monday morning, Pop and I sat in the kitchen eating scrambled eggs, fried potatoes and scrapple. Mom, wearing a blue house dress with full length apron, busied herself at the grill. She enjoyed cooking and always made more than we could eat.
 "Dig in. I have a heaping plate of eggs coming up."
 The black, 1930's style phone rang with a loud clanging noise, interrupting our morning meal. I jumped up and answered, "Hello, Remke's house."
"Morning. Benny Kipe here. Looking for Walter."
 Cupping my hand over the receiver, I whispered, "For you, Pop. A Mr. Kipe?"
 He propped himself on one crutch and ambled to the phone. Pop smiled as he put the receiver to his ear.
 "Benny, you old rascal. Good to hear from you. Tomorrow? Okay. My youngest boy works for me now. Just this summer. Good. See you then."
 Pop seemed elated as he hung up. I couldn't hold back my curiosity.
 "What happened, Pop? Do we have work?"
 "Yes. Just as I expected. Kipe Construction took over the Colabs contract. Benny wants the whole crew back tomorrow. I want to get there early so be ready to go by six."
 "Okay. But what about you?"
 Pop looked down at his leg, still fragile, not able to bear the full weight of his body.
 "Yeah. I'll be riding shotgun for the straw boss until it heals."
 Mom grumbled, "All right you two. Breakfast will soon be getting cold. Let's eat. You'll need to build up your strength for tomorrow."
 Although she seemed agitated, I knew better. A fragile harmony prevailed as the men in her life returned to work. She thrived on tending a busy house.
 Pop and I arrived at the Colabs site early Tuesday morning. Bricklayers carried their tools to the scaffolds. Bulldozers roared, scurrying back and forth, leveling the terrain. Pop inspected the materials and equipment then headed for the supervisor's shack. I busied myself with the first batch of cement. A sense of order and stability prevailed.
 Several minutes later, Yeller ambled by on his way to the work site, carrying a large canvas bag filled with trowels, hammers, set lines, etc.
 "Good Morning, Vance. You look in good spirits today."
 "Yeah. After the last few weeks, it feels great to be back."
 Setting his tools down, Yeller pondered, "I've seen my share of strikes. But this last one. The worst I've ever experienced. It reminds me of the skirmish at Bellean Woods, France, 1918."
 My forehead wrinkled. I didn't get it. "What does that place have to do with the strike?"
 "A fight for power. Good against evil. World War I raged out of control. After our allies suffered massive losses in human life, the U.S. Army needed young men like your father and me."
 "Did you know Pop back then?"
 "No. I lived in Tamaqua. Your father, forty miles south, in Palm. But we had one thing in common. We finished our apprenticeships and the Army wanted tradesmen."
 "Huh? What did the Army want with bricklayers?"
 "Rebuilding bombed out houses for command centers. Bridge footings. Abutments. Anything that helped the war effort."
 Yeller glanced at his watch then picked up the tool bag. Like my father, he took pride in being early, always.
 "Well, time for work, Vance. It'll be a hot one. Keep the cement extra wet today."
 "Wait. You didn't finish. What exactly happened over there?"
 Our conversation came to an end as the straw boss came out of the supervisor's shack with a clipboard under his arm. Pop followed close behind.
 "Gather round, men. I have something important to say. Kipe Construction holds the contract for this project. We recognize Union 317 as your bargaining agent. In addition, a bonus for every man if we bring the project in ahead of schedule."
 "What about overtime pay?" yelled one of the men.
 "Time and a half for everything over eight hours. If you have any more questions, see Walter at the control shack. He'll be working for me, straight-away. Welcome aboard and work safe."
 The men, looking upbeat, started back to their jobs. Before Yeller could leave, I asked, "Can we talk about the war during lunch?"
 "Sure. But haven't you heard most of this from your father."
 "No. Pop seldom talks about it. Except that he blames the politicians for sending good men to their deaths."
 "Yeah. Sounds like him. Okay. See you at lunch."
 Curiosity provided the energy needed to heave bricks and mortar to the scaffold. My body performed the tasks but my brain immersed itself in a time over forty years ago.
 What happened to my father during that terrible time? Did the war change him? What secrets would be revealed?
 The sun baked everyone, without mercy, by late morning. Sweat saturated my shirt and rolled down my face. When the lunch whistle blew, I ran to grab my bag and looked for Yeller. Across the yard, I saw him headed for the shade of some old oak trees. Catching up to him, I sprawled on the cool green grass and started to devour my bologna sandwich.
 "Yeller. What happened after you and Pop joined the Army?"
 "They shipped us by railroad car to Fort Mead near Greenville, North Carolina. We lived in tents during our basic training. Nothing but an open field in the middle of rolling green countryside. The camp housed over ten thousand men including the commissioned officers."
 "This basic training thing. Did you meet Pop there?"
 "Yeah. During rifle and pistol training. He earned sharpshooter status on his second week. So did I.
 "We didn't like each other, at first. I thought he had an obsessive love affair with the Army. At the time, I didn't know it served as the family he never had. My folks lived in Tamaqua and loved them with all my heart but the Army gave me a chance to get away from the boredom of a small town.
 "We competed for everything. Marksmanship, hand to hand combat, running. We tried our damnedest to outdo each other."
 "When did you get to the war part?"
 "Not soon enough for us. After eight weeks of training, Construction Company Number Ninety craved the excitement of going to Europe.
 "In April, we sailed on the Masitania from New York, crossed the Atlantic and billeted in Blackpool, England, Kensington Attachment.
 "Our orders stationed the company there for the next month, building soldier's barracks. A task that kept us busy but didn't satisfy our desire to be near the front lines.
 "The waiting paid off when we received our first field assignment. Attached to the First Infantry Division, we followed their artillery brigade across the English Channel using a frigate commandeered from the Germans.
 "Disembarking at the French coast line, the war seethed, not more than a hundred miles to the west, through the flatlands of central France. We didn't know that hell waited for us on the other side of Belleau Woods."
 I leaned forward, listening, absorbing every word. The war never seemed real before. I closed my eyes and envisioned Pop dressed in his olive drab uniform. The one that hung in his closet. Riding britches with bumps on the sides, leggings up to his knees, thick wool shirt with insignias sewn on the arms and medals pinned to the left pocket. Topped off with an infantry hat.
 "Did you guys carry guns?"
 "Pistols. Army issue thirty eight caliber with cavalry style holster. They made holes half an inch wide and left no survivors.
 "On a humid June morning, sixty tradesmen, boarded military trucks loaded with construction supplies and headed for Champillon, a supply station, close to the front lines.
 "Your father and I bounced about on dirt roads for hours, passing small, thatch roofed, farm houses, fields of grain, abandoned armament and military vehicles. Although canvas enclosed the truck-bed, dust got into everything. Uniforms, equipment. I breathed that damn red dirt all morning.
 "By midday, tired and hungry, we arrived at a staging area near Champillon. When the truck motors turned off and I set foot on that desolate soil, the air came alive with the crackle of gunfire interspersed with the blast of canon shells exploding just over the horizon. A smell of gunpowder and mustard gas hung heavy over the terrain. Plumes of black smoke drifted toward the sky. We stood at the edge of oblivion."
 Yeller paused. A look of anguish froze across his face. Head lowered, he covered his eyes. I didn't move. We just sat there, in silence.
 "You okay?"
 "That accursed war. Thought I put it behind me years ago. I can't talk about it any more, Vance. Listen, I have to get back to work."
