 He seemed to look beyond just upset, more like angry, sad and shaken to the core. I felt numb and grasped for words but instead offered nothing as he walked back to the construction site. He always laughed and joked with me but not on that day. 
 Pangs of guilt washed over me as I folded my lunch bag and drank the last of my thermos coffee. I had no right to ask Yeller to unearth the past. Yet I had no one else. Pop refused to discuss it.
 That afternoon, work seemed to drag by. Troubled by Yeller and his description of the war, I thought of all those young men in that dangerous place.
 What brought them so close to the front? Did their excitement turn to anxiety? How many met their death?
 The next day I approached Yeller at lunch time, hoping to continue our conversation.
 "I need to know about Champillon. Please tell me more about being at the front?"
 "Let it alone, Vance. Generals soothed their egos and good men died. The story ends there."
 We went about work as if his answer solved everything. But a craving ate at me. An overwhelming need to know about my father in that place.
 Compelled by a strong yearning for the truth, I drove the winding road to Tamaqua on Sunday afternoon. I pulled in front of Yeller's house. The walk to his front door seemed to take forever. I forced myself to knock. Effie looked surprised when she saw me.
 "Vance? What brings you here?"
 "Good afternoon, Mrs. Swento. Can I talk with Yeller?"
 She nodded and gave me a warm smile.
 "Come in. You'll find him watching television in the living room."
 The steps to the second floor, steep and narrow, winded me, as Effie led the way. Entering the living room, I struggled with my thoughts.
 What will I say to Yeller? How dare I ask him to bare painful memories? What if he tells me to leave?
 Hands in my pockets, feeling stupid, I walked across the dusky room. Yeller, laid back, relaxed, in a black, recliner chair. A quart of Yingling beer sat on the end table next to him. He drank from a glass of the yellow liquid then sat up and stared at me.
 I glanced at her and a moment later, Yeller, afraid to begin a conversation.
 Effie suggested, "I'll leave you two alone. I need to finish some work in the kitchen."
 As she left the room, Yeller, with brows furrowed and sad eyes, bummed, "Why can't you leave it alone?"
 "Sorry Yeller. I don't mean to be disrespectful. But has your father ever hugged you?"
 "Well, sure. What does that have to do with anything?"
 "Has he ever told you he loved you?"
 "Not in those words. But I guess so."
 "My Pop never did any of those things. In my whole life. Ever. What had the war done to him? I've got to know. Please."
 Yeller breathed a deep sigh and shook his head.
 "The war's legacy to your father? Death of his comrades and the heartbreak of a lost love."
 "Wait. I understand the war and death part. What do you mean by lost love? Did Pop have a girlfriend?"
 "A thin, brown haired girl named Gwennie. A WASP from the upper class section of London who he almost married. But I don't want to get ahead of myself.
 "On the afternoon we arrived at Champillon, the First Army Mess fed the company a lunch of beef and chips. One of the few hot meals we ate in that place. For the rest of the day, we unloaded the trucks of their railroad ties, cinder blocks and supplies at the mouth of the Cierges River. "Our mission - finish a narrow-gauge railroad over the river and all the way to Bellean Woods. Of course, that depended on the First Artillery Division advancing and securing land across the no-man zone. Land that came at a very high price.
 "We finished unloading our provisions by late afternoon. Dog tired, we didn't rest until every two man team set up tents and stowed their gear. That night, no one slept well except maybe from sheer exhaustion. The sound of war, still in the distance became less intense and drifted to sporadic silence."
 "Yeller. What about my Pop? Did he look scared?"
 "No. He always had a poker face. You never knew what he thought. But I've got to believe he felt as I did. The fear that Germans could over run the no-man zone during the night and kill us all in our sleep.
 "Next morning, our commanding officer organized the company into teams. About fifteen men, all officers, claimed professional skills like architecture and engineering. They did the planning. Fifteen men had backgrounds as stone masons, bricklayers and cement finishers like your Pop and me. The balance, about thirty men, built train tracks and trestles.
 "A surveyor determined the best location for the bridge and an engineer, its construction. Our purpose in that war had begun.
 "Within a month, we finished constructing the base. Seven cement piers across the river, designed to hold the trestle's massive wood frame. Your father and I worked hard on that project. Proud of our achievement, we strutted about with pride for days. But the Germans took it away in minutes."
 "Did they break through the lines like you thought?"
 "No. They ravaged our work from the air. I can still hear the biplane engines screaming as they dived down on us. The bastards threw bombs. Black powder, lead encased grenades from their cockpits. They'll rip a man to shreds.
 "Everyone ran for cover. But the explosives came too fast. I still remember hearing the ear splitting bursts. Water and dirt flying everywhere. Seeing men fall. Feeling my heartbeat pounding out of control. Then as fast as the bastards came, they disappeared."
 "What happened to Pop? How did he survive the bombing?"
 "He had duty on one of the river barges. When the bombing started, he dove into the water. Damn lucky too. Some of those bombs went off in the river.
 "The commanding officer ran back and forth, as if he had gone mad, barking orders at everyone to account for those around them and assemble on the east shore. Waist high in water, I busied myself with the task of helping men from the river. Then I saw him. Your father, about fifty feet out, struggling to swim. He held a fellow soldier in his arms.
 "Swimming with all my strength, I reached them in minutes. We carried the man to shore and tried to revive him. Too late for our friend. Riddled with shrapnel, I figured the soldier died before he hit the water.
 "Though Walter would never admit it, after we carried our dead comrade to the clearing, he cried. For a brief moment. Then just as quick, wiped his face and continued searching for bodies. It never happened again."
 "I've never known that side of him, Yeller. What happened next?"
 "After several agonizing hours of search, we accounted for everyone. Six men wounded. We tended them best as possible. No medics available. Just a couple of medical kits. First Army Battalion had assigned all medical personnel to the front.
 "Three men didn't make it. We wrapped the bodies in blankets and trucked them back to Normandy. The war ended for those heroes. Unlike us, they would be going home.

Chapter 20
 "The following day, marines trucked in heavy artillery. Fifty-caliber machine guns set up on both sides of the river. The damn place crawled with gunners and marksmen. Next time, it wouldn't be so easy for those sons a bitches to kill us.
 "Headquarters sent for replacements from England but they wouldn't arrive for two weeks. In the meantime, the rest of the division, including your father and me, checked for damage. Six piers survived, just some nicks and scrapes. One took a direct hit. Black powder burns all over and chunks of concrete, blown away.
 "I hustled, chiseling at loose stone. Walter fixed the biggest holes with a mix of small pebbles and cement. By nightfall that pier stood in good shape. Battered but ready for the trestle.
 "Our job done, Walter and I carried rails and lumber for the railroad men as they framed the bridge. Slow but sure, it became a hell of a creation designed to carry some pretty hefty equipment like tanks and canon as well as men.
 "As the days passed, we tried to tune out the nagging drone of battle, just beyond the skyline. Reports from the front didn't give much hope for a quick victory.
 "After much waiting, replacements arrived. Progress continued. Rail men laid track about a half mile behind us. By the time they caught up, the trestle stood ready."
 "Did you guys finish the mission across the river into Bellean Woods?"
 Yeller heaved a deep sigh. He reached for the quart of beer at his side, filled his glass and gulped from the brew. I sat motionless, on the black couch across from him, waiting for his next words.
 Tamaqua and 1959 faded to a gray haze. My senses burned raw, experiencing death and destruction through Yeller's eyes. I walked in his shoes. 1918, Bois de Belleau became now and consumed my every thought.
 He finished the beer and lit a cigarette. His mouth pulled tight across a tanned, worn face from too many years outside.
 "Your father and I just wanted to complete the god damn thing. Not everyone got that chance.
 "Our construction company pushed across the bridge and into thick wooded flatlands. Hot weather, mosquitoes and trees became our enemy. Work slowed. But it took our minds off the war. A big mistake.
 "On a sunny day in early June, the world we knew came to an end. It started with breakfast at the mess tent. Then six of us picked up tools and reported for work with the lead crew.
 "We cut a rough path about twenty feet wide for the track bed. Midmorning, while busy clearing brush and trees, I heard a loud gunshot. On my right, one of our guys fell like a sack of lye soap. A bullet ripped through his skull.
 "Krauts. Stinking, rotten Heinies ran from the tree line. Looked like only two of them. My guess, advance scouts.
 "Before I could react, a whole bunch of shots zinged by. A railroad man standing next to me jerked backward. Blood spattered across my uniform. Then he plunged to the ground.
 "Like rabid dogs, they rushed us. Screaming and shooting at the same time.
 "One of our best bricklayers clutched at blood surging from a hole in his chest, then collapsed.
 "Then another fell next to him. The bastards must have thought they could kill us all real fast.
 "As they closed in, your father slung his shovel full force at the closest son of a bitch. It hit him hard in the neck with a loud, hollow thump. A look of disbelief froze on the Heinies face as he gasped for air. I lunged for my dark blue thirty eight and shot him twice. He fell face first in the dirt.
 "Before I could aim at the second Kraut, Walter drew his pistol and fanned off six rounds. The son of a bitch jerked and twitched for the longest time before he toppled like a tree.
 "Then silence, except for ringing in my ears and the smell of gun powder. Walter ran to our men. Their bodies limp like rag dolls, tossed aside without care. Your father checked them for signs of life. All dead.
 "Mad as hell, I rifled through the Kraut's pockets looking for information. I wanted to know how they got through the front lines and if the bastards came alone. Found their wallets but couldn't read a thing. All in German. Understood the pictures though. Family pictures. Wives, children. Felt sorry for the kids. God damn war.
 "Walter and I left the Heinies where they dropped then carried our men to the side of the clearing, in the shade. We expected to walk back to base camp for help. The enemy had different plans.
 "I spied them first. More Krauts, in gray uniforms, maybe infantry regulars this time, coming out of the woods. At least ten or fifteen. They must have heard the shots and came looking for their scouts.
 "We ran like hell to the tree line for cover. Bullets followed us like fleas to a dog. Falling hard on the ground, I checked the ammo belt at my waist, loaded with twenty four rounds.
 "Crouched down on one knee, I laid eyes on the bastards running in my direction. Breathing like a race horse at full gallop, I pulled back the hammer of that thirty eight and steadied my right hand. I drew a bead on them and pulled the trigger, over and over, until the firing pin's dull click, click on empty casings reminded me to reload. Three Krauts dropped. The rest took cover.
 "Walter, about twenty feet to my right, squeezed off several rounds. Two more Heinies fell. Your father dumped the shell casings, reloaded, then yelled to head for the ammo dump about a quarter mile back. The Marines had fire power. All we had to do is make it back alive.
 "Your father and I started running. No time to even think about those poor souls left behind. We bobbed and weaved through a maze of dense brush and trees. The Krauts, no more than two hundred yards back. That forest saved us. Otherwise, the sons a bitches would have shot us in the back. I wanted to make it to the ammo dump so I could kill them all.
 "I heard a bunch of shots going off behind us and men shouting in German. We kept the sun at our back, running toward the Marines. The Krauts advantage of surprise, gone.
 "Darting through the woods, Walter hollered that we should head for the second crew. A cold chill ran up my back. I forgot about them. Railroad men started laying track that morning about a quarter mile behind us. I wondered if they heard the shots. We needed to warn them.
 "I pointed to the left, jabbing the air with my finger. We changed direction and ran as fast as our boots would carry us toward the track bed. After fifteen or twenty minutes, we emerged at the clearing and fell to our knees, out of breath and exhausted.
 "As I reloaded my pistol, your father stood up, looking down the track bed both ways. He ripped off his helmet and threw it to the ground, cursing in whispers so the Krauts wouldn't hear him. No crew in sight. I panicked. I didn't know if we came out behind them or in front of them.
 "I wiped at beads of sweat running down my forehead and sprang to my feet. If we came out behind, they would be dead in minutes. Walter motioned not to make any noise as he strained to hear the slightest sound. A slight breeze through the trees, birds chirping. No gunshots except for a low murmur at the front. Seconds seemed like hours.
 "Nothing. Nothing.
 "Then a sound so low, we almost didn't hear it.
 "The sound of hammers driving spikes into ties. At that moment, we knew the crew worked somewhere just ahead of us.
 "We wanted to let out a cheer but instead ran like hell, bounding over the flat ground, leveled for the wood ties. Several minutes later, the Heinies ran out onto the track bed. By then, we saw the second crew.
 "Walter and I waved our arms, screaming that Germans breached the line. The crew of ten men looked shocked for a moment, stopped work and drew their weapons.
 "As we reached them, a barrage of gunfire erupted. Everyone scampered for the safety of a large stack of ties at the side of the road bed. We returned their fire but couldn't match the Kraut's rifles for accuracy. They advanced, taking cover behind trees at the edge of the woods. I wanted to run toward the ammo dump but out in the open they'd take me down in seconds.
 "Within minutes, the standoff became one sided. Several of our men suffered flesh wounds. I watched your father load his last six rounds. He focused on making every shot count because they would rush us as soon as the ammo ran out.
 "Not expecting to survive, I reached for the identification tags around my neck. At least my parents could claim the body. I pulled my knife, a razor sharp, twelve inch blade and prepared to defend that pile of wood to the end.
 "Sounding like the voices of angels, my ears filled with whoops and hollers coming in behind me. Marines. Damn Marines. At least a hundred of them. They must have heard all the commotion. But I wondered what the hell took them so long. Another thirty minutes and it would have been too late.
 "They ran past us, laying down a volley of rifle fire. Those poor Heinie bastards didn't stand a chance. In the end, about thirty German soldiers of the 461st Regiment lay dead, stacked like cut timber on the railway to Bois de Belleau.
 "After the skirmish, we helped recover our fallen comrades and shipped the wounded back to Champillon for medical treatment.
 "Construction stopped for several weeks until the Marines swept through Belleau Woods. A general even came to congratulate us for our work while taking out a few Heinies in the process.
 "The captain tried to patch our group together until we received new orders. To our surprise, recruits shipped out from the states to replace us. First Army Brigade scheduled Construction Company Number Ninety for rotation back to England.

Chapter 21
 Without warning, the room filled with silence. Pulling me away from the carnage in my mind's eye, jerking my senses back to the present.
 Yeller stared at the smoldering cigarette pinched between his fingers. He flicked the ash and inhaled from the butt. Then mashed it out in the ash tray across from me. As he sat back in the plush chair, our eyes made contact, his face immersed in sadness.
 In school, I dismissed the war as boring. Stuff old people talked about. Now, as my mind reeled from the horror of that time, I struggled to say the words in my heart.
 "Yeller. You and Pop lived through hell. I'll never forget, not today or ever."
 "We just wanted to survive. Call it luck or fate, everyone from Team One died, except your father and me. From that day forward, we became close pals. Forty years later, we still watch out for each other."
 All of a sudden, Yeller stood up and walked to the front window. As he pulled the curtains open, I noticed the sun descending over the mountains to the west. Hours slipped by since we embarked on our journey to the past. I lost all reference of time.
 He rasped in his deep, rough voice, "Listen Vance. About this girl named Gwennie. Whatever happened between Walter and her, it shouldn't come from me. Besides, I feel tired, real tired. You best go on home. Your Mom and Pop must be wondering where you've been all afternoon. I'll see you at work on Monday."
 Reluctant to leave, yet afraid to upset him, I smiled and capitulated, "Okay, thanks for everything, Yeller."
 Stretching, yawning, I stood up and walked toward the steps. To my surprise, Effie emerged from the kitchen and grabbed my arm. She straightened my collar, like Mom did so many times, a concerned look on her face.
 "You've grown up so fast, Vance. It seems like yesterday when I held you in my arms at Saint Emmanuel Church. The day I became your godmother. I can't believe in a few more years you'll be a man."
 I felt the warmth of embarrassment wash across my face. Looking down at the floor, I fidgeted, not knowing what to say.
 "Take care of yourself, Vance, and tell you mother I asked about her."
 "I sure will, and about Mom, thank you for being a good friend to her. She doesn't have anyone else to talk with except maybe for me."
 "Well, she can call me anytime. You tell her that."
 "I will. Thank you, ma'am."
 Effie joined me as I walked down the carpeted stairs to the front door. Saying good night, I stepped off the porch and crossed the dark street to my car.
 Driving through the west end of town, I tried to put together my father's life. Now, thanks to Yeller, I knew about the horrors Pop experienced during the war. For the first time, I hoped to understand him and find a sense of peace from it all.
                                                            ****
 Lost in my thoughts, I turned left onto Owrigsburg Street, driving slow past all the houses, worn and discolored from years of built up coal dust.
 Without warning, red lights flashed and sirens wailed several hundred feet ahead. Cops waved me onto a side street. Stuck in traffic, impatient, I parked on the shoulder, stepped out of the car and walked toward the commotion. When I reached the crowd, mayhem prevailed in front of a white, two-story house. Police cars parked on the front yard with cops everywhere.
 Then I realized, my god, Darryl lived there. Where did I see him last? Oh yeah. At lunch time, on the last day of school. A bunch of us went across the street to the red brick house we called the Folk Store. They didn't have burgers or fries that day, only stuff like Snickers and Coke. Most of us hung out there because we didn't bring a bag lunch.
 Darryl and I bought our sweets and sat on the curb out front, talking about girls and cars, devouring candy bars and soda until the bell rang. Later that afternoon, we finished English and Science classes. At the end of the day, Darryl and I said goodbye. I didn't expect to see him again until after Labor Day.
 Standing on my toes, I tried to see over the cluster of people in front of me. Then someone exclaimed, "He did it. I knew it would happen sooner or later."
 They pointed to a shape lying on the grass, next to the front porch, covered with a green Army type blanket. All my senses bunched up with the thought of a body underneath that spread.
 I pushed to the front of the mob, trying to see inside the house. Silhouettes of heads bobbed about, back and forth, on the shade, as I tried to look through the window. Several minutes later, police emerged at the front door with Darryl leading the way. His hair looked disheveled with puffy, red eyes. Only half his shirt tucked in. He appeared tired, hunched over, as the cops walked him to one of the police cars. Then I noticed the handcuffs.
 Ducking past the barrier, I walked up on the lawn. As I came closer, a cop held up his hand.
 "Hey kid, get back to the sidewalk."
 "Yes Sir. I just want to talk to Darryl."
 "I won't say it again. Turn around. Now."
 I stepped to the curb and yelled, "Darryl, what happened?"
 He tried to look past the police car headlights shining in his eyes.
 "Who wants to know?"
 "Vance Remke."
 He smiled for a brief moment but just as quick, sobbed, out of control, as one of the cops opened the car door.
 Darryl cried out, "I shot the bastard! I killed him and he deserved it!"
 "Who?" I hollered back.
 "My old man."
 Everyone in school knew about the restraining order against Darryl's father. A drunk who had a reputation for stealing, writing bad checks and violence. According to local gossip, he liked to beat up his wife. The last time made the newspaper when he knocked her down a flight of stairs, putting her in the hospital for three weeks. He served a year in prison down near Philadelphia for it.
 Darryl's words triggered my memory to the violence in our family. Yeah, I thought of killing Pop for the times he hit us. But only for a brief second because my father lived on a different plane than most men. He not only provided food, clothing and shelter for Mom and me but commanded respect from everyone who knew him. Did that make it right? No, but neither would revenge. I had no answers. One thing I knew for sure, although Darryl and I both endured troubled families, we walked very separate paths.
 I watched a middle aged woman, my guess, his Mom, run from the front door and wrap her arms around him. One of the cops grabbed Darryl and pushed him toward the squad car. Another drew the woman aside and walked her to a second black and white.
 Darryl yelled, "Vance. You still there?"
 "Yeah. I can hear you."
 "Please tell my girlfriend the cops arrested me."
 "Who? I don't know your girlfriend."
 "Sure you do. Cassie Hanks."
 I felt my eyes widen as the shock of his statement settled in. Did he know the real Cassie? About what she did at the Brine Bar after dark? That the sorry excuse of a mother pimped her to every beer belching, greasy misfit that had twenty bucks in his pocket.
 This had nothing to do with me. I should have walked away, hopped in my car and drove home. And what about Reese? What would she think about me acting as messenger boy for the likes of Cassie Hanks? I didn't like it but I considered him a friend. Someone who would stand by me if something bad happened. Besides, what possible harm could come from dropping by the Brine Bar?
 "Yeah, I know Cassie from school. Don't worry. I'll tell her what happened."
 "Hey Vance. God bless ya, pal. I owe you."
 The police shoved Darryl inside the cop car and closed the door. Minutes later all the squads pulled onto the street and headed toward downtown. An ambulance pulled up and two men dressed in white, moving with quick efficiency, loaded the remains of Darryl's father into the back.
 I wanted to get the hell away from there. His death reeked of overwhelming finality with no second chances. No last plea for understanding or forgiveness between father and son.
 As the rescue squad departed, the crowd dispersed down the sidewalk. Traffic on the street started to move while I climbed into my car.
I drove down the steep incline of Spruce Street to the sleazy side of Tamaqua and pulled into the Brine parking lot. Finding every space filled, I ended out on the street next to the Reading Railroad tracks.
 On Saturday night, the Brine filled with miners and other locals, many I imagined on state aid, spending the last of their checks for the month. I pushed my way inside, past the high pitched vibrations of Peggy Sue on the juke box and bodies gyrating about on the dance floor.
 How different I felt, entering this place filled with the foul stench of alcohol. No longer the nervous, intimidated, teenager of two years ago, I strode down the length of the bar, looking for Cassie. Nowhere in sight.
 Turning to the bartender, I yelled over the noise and music.
 "Have you seen Cassie Hanks tonight?"
 The young blond, dressed in Levis and a revealing t-shirt, pointed to the door behind me.
 "Yeah, back room but she has a friend with her right now."
 "Thanks but this can't wait"
 I shoved through some curtains spanning the doorway to find a dark room and a few tables occupied by couples engaged in conversation. In the corner, I saw Cassie sitting with an old guy, maybe in his fifties. I clenched my fists, chin up and started walking. I stopped in front of her, leaned over and whispered, "Darryl has a message for you."
 The old guy drooled, "Damn it, kid, get the hell out of here."
 Cassie, looking bewildered, snapped, "I'll meet you at the bar."
 I sneered down at the grizzled old slob next to her and backed away without a word. The hell with him. I came here to tell Cassie about her boyfriend and leave, nothing else.
 I swiped the curtains aside, filled with anger, and walked to the bar.
 "Hey, blondie. Give me a Coke. Please."
 She nodded but continued mixing drinks at a frantic pace.
 "Be there in a second."
 I dropped twenty five cents on the counter as the bartender popped the cap, filled a clean glass with effervescent soda and never missed a beat.
 She inquired, "Don't you bootleg coal with Kooba Michek?"
 "Yeah, used to, but I haven't seen him in the last two months. Does he still come in here?"
 "A couple of times a week. He usually gets drunk, bitches about the hot weather and his crummy job."
 "Well, tell Kooba that Vance Remke asked about him."
 "Okay. But I have a poor memory for names. Why don't you leave your handle?"
 She shoved a note pad across the bar. I scribbled a short message along with my phone number and pushed it back to her.
 I felt a tap on my shoulder and spied Cassie standing next to me. She seemed irritated, looking back and forth as if someone would hear.
 "How do you know about Darryl?"
 "I talked with him tonight. He told me about you."
 "What the hell? He wouldn't do that to me."
 "He didn't have any other choice. The cops took Darryl to the police station."
 Cassie's face filled with shock as she reacted to the news.
 "What happened?"
 I shook my head, struggling for the right words.
 "He shot his old man."
 "Oh my god. Did they take him to the hospital?"
 "He died on his front lawn up at Owrigsburg Street."
 Lucy's face distorted in anguish, a low pitched sob emanated from her quivering lips. Tears rolled down her cheeks as she walked toward me. Without thinking, I wrapped my arms around her as she convulsed, out of control.
 How strange, she had feelings like a real person. A real person? Had I discounted her humanity? Perhaps, on a subconscious level. I didn't feel very pleased with myself.
 A few moments later she regained her composure and pushed away from me.
 "Sorry, Vance. I didn't mean to, well, you know."
 "No need to say anything. I understand."
 "Damn, what should I do? I can't just stay here. I have to see Darryl. Can you give me a ride to the police station?"
 "I can't. I, uh, have to go. My father expects me home soon."
 "Ooh, okay. I know how parents can be. I'll ask around. Someone should be going that way."
 I turned into quite a liar. On a Saturday night, a lot of my friends would be out and about. I couldn't let anyone see her in my car. Ever. But what about Darryl? I pondered if he would do the same for me.
 As she started to walk away, I interjected, "Wait. My parents would understand if I took a few extra minutes to run you over to the cops."
 I hoped that I wouldn't regret it. We walked past the dance floor, to the front entrance while a few patrons stared at us. I knew what those bastards thought. Another paying customer. I felt indignant and wanted to punch somebody in the face. Yet Cassie's dilemma touched me and stirred emotions I struggled to understand.
 The side street leading to the railroad tracks and parking lot out back immersed itself in darkness except for a lamp overhead. Unlocking the door for Cassie, I wondered about her and Darryl. I didn't think anyone at school knew about them.
 As she climbed into the car, I questioned, "How did you get together with Darryl?"
 "We met at a beer party just outside Mahanoy City."
 "I heard that town has a tough school."
 "Yeah. He attended there. Every weekend, Darryl guzzled beer up by the water tower till he passed out. He drank to get away from his father. But hell, most of the high school blitzed out on tower hill. Kids parked their cars in a circle and shared quarts of beer.
 "About three years ago, my half brother and I crashed one of the parties. We brought a case of quarts with us so no one cared. I met Darryl back then."
 "Did you guys date for very long?" I asked, while starting the car.
 "Yeah. Darryl and I liked each other from the start. We met on weekends in Mahanoy City for about a year until he transferred to Tamaqua High School. He accepted me and never asked any questions. But when he started senior high, I knew he'd find out."
 Pulling onto Huber Street, I inquired, "Find out what?"
 "Don't insult me. You know. Everyone in town understands how my mother sold me on the street since I turned twelve. When I graduate, she'll see the last of me. I'll head for New York."
 "Sorry. I only heard rumors. Guys talking in the locker room at school. Stuff like that. So, did you tell him?"
 "Yeah. I told him everything. It broke his heart and he left me."
 "Darryl never told anybody at school. Stuff like that gets around," I offered, as I drove through the back streets of town.
 "Last year, his old man beat him so bad, he needed stitches. He didn't want to worry his mother, so he came to me. Old Doc York, down the street, stitched him up. He had no where else to go so I took him in for a couple of days. After that, we got back together."
 I pulled onto Coaldale Street and observed the police station a block ahead. A squad car parked on the street with the motor running. Several officers stood on the sidewalk, smoking cigarettes.
 "You can drop me off by the cops, Vance. Thanks for the ride."
 "Uh, listen. You don't have a ride back. I could hang around for a while."
 "You don't have to do that."
 "Nah. I don't mind."
 Parking behind the black and white, we walked to the police station and entered a dark gray lobby. The room crawled with cops and reporters. I overheard them talking about Darryl and the murder. An overweight police sergeant, maybe in his forties, sat behind a large metal desk in the center of the room. He glanced at us while hunched over, phone to ear, writing on a large yellow pad.
 "You can wait over there," he barked, pointing to several chairs against the wall.
 We sat on the hard seats, our patience wearing thin, looking for any sign of Darryl. I wondered if the cops would let Cassie talk to him. She loved him but that didn't make her family.
 About fifteen minutes later the sergeant inquired, "Okay, people. What can I do for you?"
 Cassie and I walked to the officer. I felt uneasy standing there, waiting for her to ask about Darryl.
 "You arrested Darryl Grainger this evening. When can I see him?" Cassie asked.
 The sergeant, who had considered us a minor distraction, now gave us his full attention.
 "Well, that depends. You got a name?"
 "Yes Sir. Cassie Hanks."
 "Hey? You that Hanks girl over on Wells Street?"
 "Yes. I live there with my mother and brother."
 A twisted smile formed on the cops face as he looked around the room, then at her.
 "Look. Grainger can't see nobody except his lawyer. But I might be able to make an exception. You do something for me and maybe I can work it out. We keep a storage room in back. A real quiet place where, uh, we, heh, heh, won't be interrupted."
 I felt anger welling up inside of me as I grabbed her by the arm.
 "You don't have to do this. Let's leave, right now."
 She turned to me, eyes watering and a single tear rolling down her cheek.
 "Vance, thank you for everything. You've done all you can. Go on home."
 I watched the cop lead her past all those people in the lobby to a doorway in back. My guess, to that grimy little room where she would surrender herself, yet one more time.
 Strong emotions overcame me. I wanted to slug the bastard and send him flying across the room. But then I'd be in a jail cell just like Darryl. A sense of injustice, devoid of hope, set in my stomach like a brick. Now I understood her world and it sickened me.

Chapter 22
 I felt helpless, standing there alone, yet not alone, surrounded by a cacophony of sound from the grown-ups chattering back and forth about my friend. State Police, dressed in tailored brown uniforms, reminded me of the Nazi pictures my brother sent home during World War II. The others, wearing blue uniforms and lacking the spit and polish of the State Troopers, must have been local cops. Journalists, with press badges pinned to their suit lapels, wrote non-stop as the cops gave their details of the murder.
 Cassie, branded a prostitute, paid a heavy price that night. I wondered if she could survive the burden of it all - Darryl's murder, the cops, her mother and this town.
 I tried to understand what had just happened. My promise to Darryl evolved far beyond the simple task of messenger. He exposed me to a disgusting comprehension of the violent and fragile relationships in his life. Under my breath, I whispered, "God help you, Darryl."
 As I walked to the front door, unnoticed and irrelevant, the significance of the last few hours seemed so clear to me. My world of friends, enemies and parents could change without warning. Shifting, moving like the fine coal silt on Saint Mary's Mountain.
 Stepping onto the sidewalk, past the squad cars, into the hot summer night, I thought of Reese. At that moment, I needed her arms around me, to lose myself in the sweet smell of her hair and feel her smooth lips against mine. She gave me refuge, a place to suspend my concerns about the present much less the future. Maybe I could just drop by her place for a couple of minutes.
 I glanced at my watch. Twenty to ten. My god. Time stole my night like a common thief. Damn. Pop will kill me. I just wanted to see Yeller and go home. The night is half over and I have to get up in seven hours.
 Running to my car, I started the motor and shifted to first gear but took care not to squeal out of the parking slot. I came to the end of the block and turned left at the Elks Club, out of sight from the cops. I revved the engine several times then rammed the gas pedal to the floor, peeling rubber all the way to Main Street.
 Guilt rode me for not being able to help Cassie and although the evening drew to a close, I needed to see Reese. At the stop light, I drove up Mauch Chunk Street and pulled in front of her house. It stood dark as I grabbed some pebbles from her front lawn and threw them one by one until the light in her second story window came on.
 Meeting her at the front door, we talked in whispers between kisses. I explained my visit with Yeller and the sad details of Darryl and Cassie.
 "Vance, you already helped more than most people. Go home and get some sleep. You can be a friend but they will have to sort it out for themselves."
 "I suppose you have a point. Guess I'll head to the valley so you can get some rest. Hey, who loves you?"
 "You do, silly."
 She smiled and then kissed me. A long smooch, leaving my heart beating faster. I felt warm and loved. In my mind, the world filled with sunshine, flowers and goodness. Pure happiness in the form of Reese Arkov.

Chapter 23
 The fast back sedan labored, climbing the steep mountain road out of Tamaqua. I looked down on the city lights sparkling below, beautiful yet deceiving. Flickering shards of light hid Cassie's misery and the loss of hope that Darryl must feel tonight.
 This town wrapped its arms around me and stirred my emotions. It pulled me into its whirlpool of humanity, feeling the suffering of others.
 Now, I faced my own quandary, racing to get home, where Pop waited. A tense knot formed at the base of my neck as I followed the weaving blacktop road out to the valley. Pulling into the driveway, light shined from the living room and spilled out onto the front lawn. I took a deep breath and walked in, trying to look as if nothing in my life changed since morning.
 Pop sat in the old wicker chair with his back to me, watching television. As I passed through the living room, he scrutinized me for a moment then growled, "You have supper tonight?"
 "Uh, no," I confided, realizing that I hadn't eaten since lunch.
 "Well, your mother made pot roast tonight. Look in the fridge."
 "Okay. I'll just make a sandwich."
 I mashed chunks of meat between two pieces of bread, poured a glass of milk and sat at the kitchen table.
 As I consumed the snack, Pop walked to the bathroom and went about his nightly ritual, brushed teeth, mouth wash then two Alka-Seltzers for his persistent heart burn. Several minutes later, he sat at the kitchen table across from me and drank the bubbly water. Wiping his mouth and without warning he stared at me and queried, "Where you been so late tonight, boy? Out with that girl, Reese?"
 "Yeah, yeah, just hanging out. Pop, I have to ask you something."
 "What do you want?"
 My mind bristled with anxiety. I wanted to ask about that other woman in his life, before Mom. A voice deep inside me screamed, ask him.
 "Pop, nothing. Nothing important."
 Gwennie can wait. No need to bring her up now. Tomorrow will be a better day.
                                                            ****
 The sounds of pots and pans clanking on the stove downstairs woke me. I stretched and yawned, then pulled the window curtains back. Still dark outside.
 An aroma of bacon wafted into my room and pulled me from the warm sanctuary of my bed. I fought the urge to roll over and with reluctance turned on the lamp behind me.
 I fumbled through the closet for a washed pair of jeans and my cement caked work boots. Then to the dresser for a t-shirt.
 I heard a thumping sound coming from the hallway. Seconds later, Pop stood by my door, balanced on one crutch.
 As usual, he dressed in khaki shirt and pants, matching Fedora, cocked forward on his head and boots like mine, scuffed and stained with cement.
 "We have a busy day ahead, boy. The contract deadline comes up in five weeks so Benny needs everyone to give his best. You'll wish you had those extra hours of sleep by this afternoon."
 "Don't worry about me. I won't let the crew down."
 He nodded, looking at me with a solemn expression on his tanned, leathery face.
 "Okay, I'll see you downstairs."
 I knew what he meant by the tone of his voice. Don't let him down. Not Benny, not the crew. Pop expected me to protect the Remke reputation and I understood why but he shouldn't have been so concerned. In spite of our disordered relationship, just being his son drove me to outdo the other summer help. Yeller nicknamed me Speed last year and no one dared challenge it.
 Grabbing my comb and wallet, I raced downstairs, catching Mom's attention. She hustled about the kitchen for a moment then piped, "I didn't hear you come in last night."
 "Yeah. I got home about eleven."
 "You okay? I mean, a lot has happened since the strike, what with the Heims and Pop's leg."
 "Nothing like that, Mom. A friend from high school got into trouble last night. I tried to help him."
 "You want to talk about it?
 "Nah."
 "Okay. Then how about some breakfast? You hungry?"
 "Sure. I didn't eat much yesterday."
 She scooped fried potatoes, scrambled eggs and bacon on my plate while I grabbed a chair at the kitchen table.
 Soon afterward, I heard Pop in the garage, cursing and the heavy sound of metal crashing against concrete.
 "He sounds angry. Why doesn't he come in for breakfast?"
 "Already ate. I don't think he slept much last night or for the last several weeks. My guess, he relives the shooting every night. He woke me up around four, making noise checking his tools, then loading them into the station-wagon even though he can't use most of them with his leg out."
 After breakfast, Mom asked me to help carry bed sheets and clothes to the laundry room. As I loaded the washing machine, she grabbed my hand.
 "Effie called me last night. She overheard you and Yeller talking about Pop and the war. You did the right thing going to him because I can't help and your father won't ever talk about it."
 The dirty clothes fell from my hands onto the floor in a heap. I wanted to talk with Mom about it, but not today. Now I had no choice.
 "I have something important to say, Mom. Yes, Yeller told me about the war but he also talked about Pop meeting a woman named Gwennie from England. He loved her but for some reason it didn't work out. I wanted to know more but Yeller wouldn't tell me. He suggested I ask Pop."
 A pained expression came across Mom's face. She backed up against an old chair, sat down and exhaled, a long deep breath. My mother's voice quivered as she confided, "I know all about her. The first time your father and I ever argued, he screamed that I could never compare to his Gwennie."
 I knelt by the chair and hugged her.
 "It's okay, Mom. You'll always come first with me."
 She dabbed at her watery eyes with her shirt sleeve and started to get up.
 "You need to get ready for work. And if you talk to your father about that woman, be careful. I don't think he ever got over her. You might stir up a lot of hurt for him and us."
 After we walked back to the kitchen, Mom started to pack lunches. She pulled two brown paper bags from a drawer and filled them with sandwiches and chips then handed them to me.
 "I notice sunlight starting to break over South Mountain. Go check on your father. He wants to leave at seven sharp."
 With caution, I poked my head into the garage and saw Pop shuffling tools back and forth in the station-wagon. Last year, he bought the rust colored 1957 Chevy from the Kovich Chevrolet dealership in town. He didn't believe in credit and paid cash for the vehicle. For my father, I think his being debt free represented power, control and pride.
 "You about ready to go, Pop?"
 He pulled on his watch bob and clicked open the metal face plate.
 "Don't rush me, boy. We can leave in about ten minutes."
 As he slipped the watch back in his pocket, I noticed his hands. Raw and chaffed from years of laying bricks, he had wrapped tape on each finger to keep them from bleeding. For the first time since talking to Yeller, I looked at my father through different eyes.
 "Mom packed lunches. I'll put them in the back seat."
 "Good. Open the garage door on your way."
 When he spoke, his voice radiated crisp authority with a hint of accent, Pennsylvania Dutch style.
 I pulled the door upward, letting early morning sun flood the garage and onto Pop. Bathed in light, I noticed how fit he looked for a man in his sixties. It puzzled me. Why did these things seem different today or had I changed?
 We loaded the last of the equipment and a five gallon decanter of water into the back then closed the hatch door. I climbed behind the wheel while Pop struggled to stow his crutch and hobble into the passenger's seat.
 As I started the engine, Mom scurried into the garage, still in her slippers, up to my side of the car.
 "Enjoy the day, dear, and do your best. No one can expect more of you."
 She patted my shoulder, then walked to the other side of the car and talked to Pop in a low monotone voice.
 "Don't push your son too hard. It'll be over ninety degrees."
 He looked at her with a blank stare.
 "I can't treat him any different than the others."
 "I know. Just show a little tolerance."
 Without responding, Pop looked over at me and motioned to back up.
 "Let's go, boy."
 I shifted the car in reverse and engaged the clutch, while Mom stood at the entrance to the garage with a lost look on her face. As I pulled onto the macadam, I promised myself, nothing will stop me from asking about that woman, Gwennie.
 The drive through Lewistown Valley seemed to take forever. Silence dominated the air inside the station-wagon. Pop lit a Chesterfield cigarette and puffed on it, sometimes flipping ashes out the window. I kept both hands on the wheel and tried to think of what to say. Should I bring her up now or wait? Later. Definitely later. Maybe at lunch? Yeah. I'll ask about Gwennie at lunch.
 Pop took off his hat, revealing his neatly trimmed gray hair, flat-top style. I tried to imagine how he looked in 1918, a young man wearing his military uniform with blond hair, short on the sides and wavy locks on top.
 Yeah. I had changed. I could see through the hardened old man sitting next to me. Beyond the limitations of the eye. From that day forward, part of him would be the courageous soldier that Yeller had described to me.
 We arrived at the job site about seven thirty. Pop hobbled toward the supervisor's shack. I carried our water to the lunch area and then headed toward the long line of scaffolding on the west side of the building. The walls stood about five feet tall with another ten to go.
 Some of the men arrived and started their morning task of preparing for the day's work. Just like Pop, I walked past the stack of cinder blocks, counting the rows, estimating how many might be laid today.
 Yeller showed up minutes later, talking with several of the old timers, then went into the supervisor's shack. At five to eight, he came out and stopped by as I started loading bags of cement into the mixer.
 "Good Morning, Vance."
 "Morning, Yeller. I wanted to thank you for everything last night. It meant a lot to me."
 "No need. I probably would have taken that story with me to the grave. It felt good to get it off my chest. Well, enough said, I best let you get back to work."
 He climbed on top of the scaffold, checking the set line, cleaning the mortar board, getting ready for my first load of mud.
 The late morning air sizzled with heat but didn't slow our pace. Pop and Benny came out later to inspect our progress. By noon, Yeller and the other bricklayers laid three courses of block on the massive structure. We made great progress.
 As we knocked off for lunch, my stomach churned and sweat drenched my palms, thinking about the showdown. The apprehension overwhelmed me even though I stood about an inch taller than Pop.
 Over the last two years, I had developed a muscular body with broad shoulders and narrow waist. In my daydreams, I fancied myself competition for the likes of Steve Atlas.
 I grabbed my bag lunch and headed for the break area. Some of the crew walked to their cars for the comfort of a padded seat and radio. The rest sat under the cool shade of some trees toward the back of the property.
 Sitting on the grass, I started to eat my sandwich. Waiting. Waiting for Pop. Almost ten minutes later, he ambled to an open spot and sat down.
 I downed my bologna with white bread and walked over by him. Opening the decanter, I poured a cup of cold water and slowly sipped, biding my time. Near the end of lunch, most of the guys headed back to the job site. Now. I had to do it now.
 "Pop. I want to ask you something."
 "Yeah? About what?"
 "Someone in your past. A woman named Gwennie? What happened to."
 Before I finished, the sting of his hand slapping my face, surprised me. My head recoiled but moved very little unlike three years ago when he knocked me to the floor. Had I grown so strong in such a short time? I rubbed my cheek and looked at him through narrowed eyes.
 Pop bellowed, "You son of a bitch! You crossed the line, boy. You got no damn business asking about her. Your mother started this, didn't she?"
 "Leave her out of this! If you want to pick on someone, look right here. I asked the question."
 "I knew you'd be trouble from the day she brought you home from the hospital. Always fussing over you. Well, you're going to keep your nose out of my past."
 "Go to hell! You never wanted Mom. That woman came first. Didn't she? You couldn't even hold on to her so you had to settle for us."
 "Shut your mouth, boy. You don't know what the hell you're talking about. I'm done with you."
 As he struggled to get up, I felt angry, expelling heavy breaths like an aggravated bull, but relieved that I demanded an accounting for our lives. Pop headed straight for the supervisor's shack with some of the other workers following behind him. A few laborers remained in the break area, talking between themselves and glancing at me from time to time.
 With minutes left before work, I gulped the last of my water and started to get up. As I looked around, I spied Yeller walking toward me from the tree line. He must have been there all the time.
 "Look, uh, Vance. Why don't you drop by the house on Saturday. I guess we'll finish that conversation after all."
 A feeling of gratitude washed over me, "Thanks, Yeller."
 As we started walking to the job site he advised, "Don't be too hard on your father. His life hasn't been easy."
 I immersed myself in work that afternoon, trying to forget what happened. The drive home filled with bristled tension. At home, Mom tried to keep us apart as much as possible. The week dragged by living under a cloud of unresolved questions. So much for the admiration I felt for him just a few days ago.

Chapter 24
 On Saturday morning, I drove to town filled with anticipation. I went straight to Yeller's place and knocked on the door. Effie met me, smiling, wrapping her arm around me as we went inside.
 "Yeller told me you'd be dropping by. Have you eaten lunch yet?"
 "No, ma'am."
 "Well, come join us then."
 We walked through the living room to a large kitchen area. Yeller sat on one end of a white Formica table. An urn of steaming clam chowder soup sat in the middle and next to it, crackers, neatly circled on a plate. She led me to a chair across from him.
 As Effie sat down, she directed, "Help yourself and don't be afraid to have seconds."
 I ladled the hot soup into a bowl in front of me and started eating as Yeller pushed the plate of crackers toward me.
 We engaged in conversation about school, my girlfriend Reese, what I wanted to do with my life. As we talked, I though about how strange it felt, my enjoying this delightful meal with pleasant discussion. A feeling of harmony.
 After lunch, I helped Effie put dishes and silverware in the sink.
 "I'll finish up here. Why don't you and Yeller go into the living room?"
 We walked into the cozy alcove and sat down. I took the overstuffed arm chair and Yeller, the recliner he seemed to prefer.
 He drew a cigarette from his pack of Marlboros on the end table next to him and tapped it against the hard marble top. Pulling a lighter from his pocket, he lit the smoke and puffed until the tip reached a bright red.
 Yeller leaned back and asked, "Have things simmered down between you and Walter?"
 "We don't talk much. He spends most of his time reading the newspaper or brooding over his bad leg."
 "I feel responsible for the whole damn mess. After all these years, he never got over Gwennie and took his anger out on you."
 "Yeller, I don't blame you. Sooner or later, Pop and I would have had it out anyway. If not Gwennie then something else. Besides, he only gives a damn about the union and his men, not Mom or me.
 "It might seem so but in his own way, he does care about you and your mother. Give him some time. He can't stay mad at you forever."
 "I'll try for Mom's sake."
 "Good."
 "About the war, Yeller. What happened after Construction Company # Ninety went back to England?"
 "We said our good-byes to the Marines, packed our duffel bags and arrived in London two weeks later. Our barracks lay just outside the city in Tiffany Square. The Army gave us two day's leave before we started our next assignment. After Belleau Woods, it felt like a holiday.
 "Wearing our best uniforms, we headed for the glitter of downtown. The streets filled with cars and trolleys. Throngs of people scurried about on the sidewalks. Walter and I took in the sights. Museums, London Tower, changing of the guard. Walked until our feet hurt. Then ate fish and chips from a street vendor. That evening we headed for the Armed Forces dance hall.
 "The building looked old. A large two story structure made of cut stone with twin lions on both sides of the entrance. As we entered the massive room, the air came alive with bouncy, snappy, band sounds. Doughboys, Navy and Marines, wall to wall, dancing with girls. Lots of them.
 "But dancing held no interest for us at that time. The pub at the far end of the room suited our thirst for a tankard of warm ale. We pushed in among the young girls and soldiers lining the long oak bar trimmed with fancy scrolls and cherubs.
 "Without delay, the bartender filled our order for two large mugs of brew. We toasted to the men lost at bois de Belleau, chugged our drinks then ordered another round.
 "In the quest to satisfy our thirst, we paid little notice to the people next to us. Civilians. Two men dressed like dandies. Probably from the affluent north side of London. And a girl. A real beauty. Well dressed in a low cut, black satin dress that enveloped and accentuated her shapely curves. Short, brown hair, framed an oval face with milk white complexion.
 "Your father smiled when he noticed her. And he never looked pleased about anything. Stunned, I watched as he interrupted the three engaged in conversation. With unexpected politeness or at least unnatural for your father, he asked the lady to dance. I held my breath, expecting a fist fight to start any minute. Instead, she agreed and he escorted her to the dance floor. They soon disappeared into the sea of bodies swaying back and forth on the gleaming white marble.
 "I remember feeling homesick at that moment. My girl lived thousands of miles away."
 "Do you mean Mrs. Swento?"
 Yeller forced a grin for the first time since he started talking.
 "Yeah. She waited for me through the whole damn war.
 "While I watched for Walter to return, the dandies struck up a conversation. They called the girl Gwennie. Gwennie Blair. Seems her parents insisted those two fine gentlemen, family friends, chaperon the girl. She attended Cambridge, lived several blocks from the college and liked to come to the dances on weekends.
 "Soon, the music ended and they returned from the dance floor, walking hand in hand as if they'd known each other for years. Your father beamed as he engaged her in conversation.
 "Walter and Gwennie spent the night absorbed with each other, most of the time on the dance floor. The dandies and I talked and drank ale. We stayed until the band stopped playing and the bartender started to sweep the floor.
 "As we walked outside, your father said good night to Gwennie. He kissed her as she climbed into the chaperone's roadster. Seconds later, as they drove off, I hailed a cab back to the base. After that night, we walked different paths.
 "Did you guys still hang out together after that?"
 "Sure but things changed. Over the following weeks, Walter and I settled into our new duty assignment. We started work on an addition to the brigade officer's quarters. During the weekends, I stayed on the base, writing letters to Effie, playing poker or watching silent movies at the square. Gwennie picked up your father in her parent's roadster on Friday evenings and they disappeared until Sunday.
 "He seemed happy. A big change from the serious man that I knew. But the guys in the regiment gossiped about him. It didn't take long to find out Gwennie came from a family of barristers and she intended to follow that line of work. They thought she just wanted to party with a Yank, for fun. But I believe she loved him.
 "Walter and I talked about her several times during the following months. He fell heavy for the girl and wanted to marry her after the war. That day came sooner than both of us expected.
 "We just finished the south wall of the officer's quarters when the news came in. On a sunny afternoon in the fall of 1918, the Krauts surrendered. We jumped up and down, screamed until our lungs gave out. The war. That god damn war, with all its misery, finally came to an end.
 "Everything turned upside down that day. Even though I still had to serve our country, to complete the project, I could only think about being with my parents and most of all Effie. I marked time with the passing of winter then spring and home.
 "How did Pop feel about the war being over?"
 "Your father planned on proposing to Gwennie. I even helped him pick out an engagement ring from one of the jewelers on the Abby. That weekend, he asked her to become his wife. She accepted and took him to meet her parents. Your father told me the Blairs acted cordial enough. They wished the couple well but seemed distant. Walter dismissed the event, saying they just caught her parents by surprise.
 "We wrapped up the officer's quarters in late October, just before the first cold rains. The roofers finished a week later. And except for the brickwork on several fireplaces, your father and I spent the rest of winter helping to build the walls and floors inside.
 "Harsh winds gave way to the gentle breezes of spring. With the new officers quarters completed we worked on constructing U.S. Army barracks next to the Queen's Regiment.
 "Late in April, we received final orders to ship out of Portsmouth Harbor, back to America. I scurried about trying to wrap up loose ends. Surrendered my bricklaying tools to quartermaster, returned some books to the library, said good by to friends.
 "Your father spent little time if any on the base. Walter and Gwennie stayed at her parent's summer cottage in the country planning for the future. He had such high expectations for a fresh start back home.
 "They agreed, Walter would send for her after he arrived at Fort McKellon, New York and started to muster out of the Army. Walter and Gwennie planned to wed in New York and live there or Philadelphia.
 "On a bright morning in early May, we stood in troop formation to say one last goodbye to our English hosts. The commanding officer of Fifteenth Commando Brigade said old Ninety served with integrity, courage and represented the finest men he had ever known. Yep, I remember him saying those exact words like it happened yesterday.
 Yeller mashed his cigarette in an ash tray, wiped his eyes and stood up.
 "I have something you should see. Just relax. I'll be right back."
 He left the room for several minutes and returned with two boxes. One had the words Queen and Country embossed on top with a flip up lid. Yeller lifted it to reveal a medal. It seemed like brand new with a multicolor ribbon and bronze medallion. The other box, with U.S. Army on top, held a decoration with solid blue ribbon and silver colored star.
 "This came from the queen herself. England's highest medal for valor. The other from our President. A silver star for courage above and beyond the call of duty. Your father and I both received commendations that morning."
 He set the medals on my lap as a tidal wave of emotion swept over me, tearing me apart inside.
 "My god, Yeller. Pop never told me. I don't think he ever spoke about receiving medals, to any of us. Why would he do that?"
 "I can only guess but they might serve as a reminder of the war and Gwennie."
 "What happened after you received the medals?"
 "We climbed on flatbed trucks that afternoon and rode for several hours to Portsmouth Harbor. Straining under the weight of our gear, Walter and I boarded the SS Johnson and settled in on the lower deck. One of the aft cabins served as our home for the next several weeks.
 "Early next morning we headed for New York. The transport ship held forty knots with over a thousand men from four divisions on board. Once we made state-side, every soldier would receive fourteen days leave.
 "We played poker, wrote letters and cleaned our gear during the day. At night we went on deck, watched the stars and listened to some of the boys sing and play harmonica.
 "The journey seemed to take forever but on one clear morning, I saw a beautiful sight, New York's skyline. After cheating death in France, I felt damn lucky to have made it back alive.
 "What happened when the ship landed in New York?"
 "An Army band played music, confetti fell from the tops of buildings and we waved at everyone dockside until our arms hurt. Your father and I prepared to disembark as a long line of soldiers entered the gangplank to meet with their families.
 "We finally stepped on dry land and waded through the crowd. Minutes later, out of nowhere, Effie ran up and kissed me. My parents came too. We just stood there and hugged for the longest time.
 "Then I noticed your father, all alone, leaning against one of the military trucks lining the dock. No one came to see him and it showed by the empty look on his face. His old man, Charles, would have come that morning but Walter didn't want him there.
 "Did Pop end up spending his vacation at the base?"
 "Hell no. I knew if he got on one of those trucks, he'd be cooped up in some empty barracks. After two years at war, I'd be damned if he'd waste his leave. I invited him to stay with me at my parent's house in Tamaqua. At first he didn't want to come. Said he'd be in the way. But Effie and I insisted and after much prodding, he accepted.
 "We drove from Staten Island to Tamaqua that afternoon. To my surprise, the old town hadn't changed and my parent's house looked exactly the same with the worn tarpaper shingles and red brick chimney."
 Yeller stood up, walked to the large picture window and pointed to a row of houses across the street.
 "There it sits, second from the end. The new owners fixed it up after my parents died. Damn. Your father and I had a great time there. I introduced Walter to the neighbors on the block. They treated us like royalty. Soldiers, defenders of liberty. I can't put into words how it felt.
 "The family next door held a party for us our first night back. Some single girls hung around your father like dogs in heat, even though they didn't stand a chance. Effie brought some food, kept me company for a few hours and left early in the evening. Then your father, some old friends and I stayed up half the night drinking beer and singing to the music of an old player piano.
 I joined Yeller at the window and looked for what used to be his parent's house as he continued to talk about coming home.
 "We woke up around noon, feeling like we'd been hit by a freight train. I found empty beer bottles and ash trays filled with cigarette butts everywhere. It took several hours to clean up but I didn't care. Walter and I had lived to see a better day.
 "I showed your father around town. Main Street sported a bunch of little shops, restaurants and the local blacksmith. Up on the west end, the fire company served beer every day except Sunday.
 "At night Effie and I went out on the town, dancing, fancy restaurants or taking in a silent movie at the Majestic. We had a lot of making up to do. And my parents kept your father busy playing Bingo at the fire house or Canasta with the neighbors. They adored him and never forgot the courage he displayed at Beleau Woods.
 "Later in the week, we drove out to some of the big coal mines like the Number Six Colliery and met the guys I grew up with. The mine buzzed with activity back then, hauling coal to the foundries as fast as the miners could get it out of the ground.
 "I saved a trip to the new construction sites in South Tamaqua for last. Bricklayers had as much work as they could handle building coal processing plants. Shaker and sieve plants sorted coal by size for the foundries.
 "I told Walter we'd make a great team, laying brick for the big coal companies. He didn't want any part of it though. Gwennie wanted to live near a major city where she could practice law and have access to theater and museums. I never brought it up again until your father contacted me eleven years later."
 As Yeller and I stared out the window, absorbed by the past, Effie entered the room with what smelled like a pot of tea and a tray filled with two cups, sugar, cream and spoons.
 "Sorry. I didn't mean to interrupt. Thought you might like something to drink. Vance, help yourself."
 Without another word, she walked back to the kitchen. We filled our cups and sat on the large couch next to the coffee table.
 "Let's see, Vance. I told you about the trip to South Tamaqua. On our last night, my parents, Walter, Effie and I went out to dinner and ended up at the West End Fire Company. A local band played jazz and blues on weekends.
 "Effie and I danced most of the evening. Your father entertained my parents except when the local girls kept him busy dancing the Bustle.
 "Those two weeks seemed to pass in an instant. Early Saturday morning, Walter and I packed our clothes in anticipation of the trip to Fort McKellon. Your father told my parents how much he enjoyed his stay and thanked them for their kindness. I also said my goodbyes, knowing it would be several months before we received our discharges. Later that morning, Effie drove us back to New York.
 "We settled into the First Army Division as instructors for the Corps of Engineers. Seems the Army thought your father and I knew a thing or two we could teach new recruits about building bridges and railroads.
 "After Walter settled into a routine at the base, he looked for a place to rent. Several weeks later, he found a small, second floor apartment, just outside Yonkers. It sat a short distance from the Hudson River plus the community flourished with an affluent middle class.
 "He seemed happy, filling the place with used furniture from a second hand shop a few blocks away. I helped him move the larger pieces as well as paint walls and lay carpeting. The place looked nice when your father finished.
 "The weather grew hot as July approached and we motored trainees out to the construction projects in Tuckahoe. Every morning, your father worked the Army Privates until they dropped. Had em laying brick, casting cement and clearing track beds. In the afternoon, they received arms training. Walter told me that in the next war, they'd have to be faster, stronger and more weapons savvy, just to survive."
 "What about Gwennie? What happened to her?"
 "I'm getting to that. He wrote to her often and she answered every letter. Sometimes he read her postcards to me, especially the parts about her preparing for the trip to the U.S. She planned to take the Masatania, scheduled to leave England at the end of July and arrive in New York by early August.
 "Your father acted like a school boy, giddy and full of life. He even bought an ice box, of all things. Much too expensive for a soldier to own but he wanted it for Gwennie. Walter also made plans for their wedding at a Protestant church several miles away in Mount Vernon. Not that your father had any commitment to the religion. Hell, he always embraced atheism, but he probably would have become a Protestant to make her happy.
 "I remember the day Walter received word by telegraph that the Masatania had finally left Portsmouth and set course for America. He looked nervous as a mother ready to give birth. I don't think he could concentrate on anything. Your father rambled on about weather patterns over the Atlantic and iceberg sightings near Greenland. Memories of the Titanic, seven years earlier still remained fresh in everyone's minds.
 "Walter bought his first cake of ice for the cooler from a street vendor and stored some milk, meat and cheese. He also seemed to plunge into his work during the day, probably trying to keep his mind busy. At night, we ate dinner at the non-commissioned officers club then sat around swapping war stories and drinking beer until midnight.
 "On the fourth of August, 1919, a Thursday, as I remember, Gwennie's ship docked at New York harbor. We had been out on the shooting range when Walter received the news. After work, your father dressed in his best uniform, borrowed a friend's roadster and drove twelve miles to port. After months of preparation, the day had arrived to claim his future bride."
 Yeller paused, shaking his head, with a look of sadness. He reached out and touched my shoulder for a moment.
 Without a word, I stared back at him, trying to understand the feeling of anguish flooding my senses.
 "In 1919, Walter turned twenty seven and had already seen more than his share of misery. But it seems life had plans for sending a lot more hurt your father's way.
 "That afternoon, I stayed at the barracks and busied myself with getting ready for the next day. Several hours later, as I sat on my bunk, spit polishing boots, I heard someone raising a commotion outside.
 "I swung open the front door to find your father kicking over garbage cans and cursing everyone in sight. His breath reeked of alcohol, mud stains covered his uniform and his tie, pulled out of place, hung like a noose around his neck.
 "I'll never forget the expression on his face. He looked like a man who had been kicked in the stomach. Rage filled his eyes.
 "He screamed at me, asking what he'd done wrong. I didn't know what he meant as I grabbed him by the arm and pulled him inside the barracks. If the Lieutenant saw Walter in that condition, he'd be written up for public drunkenness.
 "He threw up on the floor and stumbled back and forth past the foot lockers, rambling about how he hated Gwennie. After some coaxing, Walter calmed down long enough to fall asleep on my bunk. I pulled the blanket over him and kept watch all night in case he woke up.
 "Around five the next morning, I forced him into the shower. After he regained his bearings, we walked to the mess hall. Over coffee, eggs and toast, we talked about what happened that previous afternoon.
 "Your father told me he arrived at port around five and entered the ship's Provost Station but they wouldn't let him on board. Instead, they handed him a letter from Gwennie. Confused, he ripped open the envelope and read it.
 "The damned note said she couldn't marry him and intended to return on the next ship back to England. Gwennie wouldn't even see him to give a reason why. The hand written letter said she still loved him but he should start a new life without her."
 "Yeller, how could she have be so heartless? Damn her to hell. I hate Gwennie for what she did to him."
 "Whoa. Let me finish before you cast judgment. Yeah, it seems rotten, the way she treated Walter but hear me out.
 "After reading her letter, I got angry, damn angry. Who the hell travels a couple thousand miles only to turn tail and run away? I told your father to get dressed because this time, I planned to board that ship and nobody would stop me from seeing Gwennie.
 "After trading duty with our fellow drill sergeants, we arrived at port about mid-morning and marched straight to the ship's office. Walking up to the Provost at his desk, I leaned over him, almost nose to nose and asked what it would take to see Miss Gwennie Blair.
 "He looked up at me with a blank stare and asked my name. Before he could blink, I ground out the words, Sergeant Yeller Swento, and said that he should make it snappy because it had been a long night and I wouldn't tolerate any flimflam.
 "The bastard must have understood that I meant every word because he picked up the ship's phone and dialed Gwennie's cabin. He talked to her for a moment then hung up. Without hesitation, the Provost spun around and faced me. Sporting a somewhat more cordial tone, he directed, "The lady will see only you."
 "What about Pop? I've never seen my father back off from anything. Why didn't he demand to see her?"
 "Listen, after Walter sobered up, he wanted to crash the gate and ramrod his way onto the ship. I talked him out of it because if the CO got wind of something like that, he would have busted your father to Private.
 "Walter trusted me to find the truth and make sense of it all. At the time, I didn't comprehend just how crazy a young woman's life could turn out. Overwhelming events that fall outside a person's control and render them helpless."
 "I don't understand at all. They loved each other and it should have been enough."
 Yeller shook his head and smiled at me as he lit another cigarette.
 "You can't be more than seventeen, right?"
 "Yeah. So what?"
 "You don't know all the things that can go on between a man and woman. I mean the push and pull of family, friends and meeting each other's needs."
 "No. I guess not."
 I sat back on the couch, exhaled a long deep breath then poured another cup of tea.
 "What happened after the Provost gave you permission to board the ship?"
 "Well, as I remember, I had to find my way to the aft deck, first class cabins. The hallways buzzed with activity as I passed other human beings dressed in fancy clothes, all going somewhere in a hurry.
 "Getting lost didn't help but after walking the corridors for about twenty minutes, I found Gwennie's cabin. I stood there, trying to decide what to do and vowed to get some answers.
 "Ready for a confrontation, I banged on the door and prepared to wade into her. A moment later, Gwennie appeared and gawked at me through red, puffy eyes. Her skin seemed pasty white when contrasted against the black bathrobe that hung loose on her shoulders. The silky dark hair that I remembered from England jutted here and there as if it hadn't been combed in days.
 "Words I had memorized and prepared to use like weapons left me and could only think to ask if she felt okay. She said no, forced a troubled smile and invited me inside.
 "The cabin, dark but roomy, smelled like a rose garden. Several vases with fresh cut flowers decorated the foyer. Gwennie walked to a large drawing area and sat on one of the classy looking parlor chairs positioned around a fancy coffee table.
 "Before I could join her, she picked up the ship's phone and ordered a bottle of scotch for two. As we started to engage in conversation, a knock echoed at the front door. One of the ship's waiters held a sterling silver tray with a pint of McGintey's Reserve, decanter of ice and two crystal glasses with HMS Masatania etched on the side.
 "He popped its cork, poured the glasses half full then departed. She belted down the drink and poured another while I sipped at mine. I felt uncomfortable about the whole damn thing, drinking in the middle of the day with Gwennie while your father waited on the dock.
 "Before long though, I realized she might need the brew to help her explain the note she left for Walter. Gwennie stared at her glass for a moment then confronted me, a solemn look on her face."
 As Yeller continued, I started to feel uncomfortable, like I had become too close to their private lives. A peeping Tom. My senses told me to back off but I couldn't stop now.
 "Yeller, does my Mom know about the note and why you met with Gwennie?"
 "I can't say. Bernice never asked and even so, I figured by all accounts, she didn't care. It happened years before Walter met your mother.
 "Anyway, Gwennie thanked me for coming aboard to see her. In the same breath she finished her second scotch. Then she asked about Walt. How strange, in almost forty years, I've never heard anyone but Gwennie use that nick name - Walt.
 "I told her how he got crocked and risked getting thrown into the stockade, renting the apartment, wedding plans, everything. She didn't take the news well. Her face drew up tight and a multitude of tears found their way down her cheeks.
 "She cried while I sat like a stupid jamoke, not knowing what to do. So, I waited, staying quiet, and shared her sadness. Several minutes later, as Gwennie wiped her eyes, she pulled a piece of paper from her bathrobe pocket, unfolded what looked like a telegram and pushed it toward me.
 "The document looked like it had been crumpled into a ball then smoothed flat. As I struggled to read it, the message chilled my soul. Gwennie's father sent the telegram two days before the ship docked and brought bad news.
 "Her mother lay in the critical care ward of All Saints Hospital in Ireland. Mrs. Blair had traveled to Dublin, serving as barrister to several members of the Irish Republican Brotherhood. These men had been conscripted by the British Army to fight in World War One. They had challenged the right of England to force them into servitude.
 "While Mrs. Blair argued their case, the guerilla faction of the IRB blew up the court house, killing or wounding four judges, nine lawyers and twenty seven civilians.  She suffered massive second degree burns and internal injuries. Gwennie's father asked her to return right away. The worst part, even if Mrs. Blair survived, she might never see again."
 Listening to Yeller, I felt a knot forming in the pit of my stomach. How could I stay unemotional and separated now? Gwennie became more than just a name. I sensed her loss and agony.
 "The news devastated her, Vance. I tried my best to comfort Gwennie but it didn't help.
 "She couldn't bring herself to see Walter, breaking his heart and knowing she had to return home. "She pulled your father's diamond engagement ring off her finger and handed it to me. Gwennie wanted Walter to give it to someone who could be there for him.
 "I put the ring in my shirt pocket and finished the drink. As I stood up to leave, once again, she thanked me for my kindness and understanding. I offered a humble prayer for her mother and wished Gwennie, God speed back to England. I never saw her again, neither did your father. She boarded the US Filbright, bound for Europe, the next day."
 Yeller stopped talking and stared out the large picture window overlooking the mountains. I bowed my head almost in reverence of my new found understanding. No one can predict where life will take them.
 After that day, I felt older and tired, very tired. I searched for truth, knowledge and found it but the burden of knowing weighed on me.  At least my parents held no more secrets or did they?
 At work, Yeller and I talked several more times about when he returned from the Army, but we never mentioned it to my father.
 As for Pop and me, we seemed to reach a compromise in our relationship. We kept to ourselves. He stayed close to the supervisor's shack for the next several weeks until his leg started to heal then tried laying brick for a few hours every day. I tended to mixing cement, carrying brick and wondered what unknown events lay ahead in my life.

Chapter 25
