 About the middle of August, after a hot day at work, Pop and I pulled in the drive way. We grabbed our lunch pails and thermoses and headed for the kitchen. I found a snack in the refrigerator and walked into the living room. My father trudged upstairs to change out of his cement laden clothes.
 I turned on the TV then looked for Mom. Most times, she busied herself working in the kitchen or sewing room.
 I called out, "Mom? You upstairs?"
 Pop yelled down, "I don't see her. Check out back."
 I looked in the shed behind our house, walked to the back yard, behind the small vineyard of Concord grapes. Nothing.
 Out of frustration, I walked part-way up the mountain where the back yard gave way to dense foliage. Again, nothing.
 When I returned to the house, I found Pop talking on the phone. Minutes later he hung up, shaking his head.
 "Nobody saw her, not the Swentos or the neighbors."
 I looked at the wall clock and noticed the time. Five minutes to six.
 "Pop, in three hours it'll be dark. What should we do?"
 "We'll head for town, check out the stores on Main and the park too."
 Pop and I piled into the station-wagon and headed for Tamaqua.
 When we arrived downtown, Pop let me off at the east end by the fire company. I started running from shop to shop, slowing down only long enough to check inside. My heart began to beat faster but not just from running. This time, I sensed something bad happened.
 The last store on Main, Acme Grocery, had just closed its doors for the day. No sign of Mom inside. I ran past the residential area with its blocks upon blocks of row houses, toward the west end of town where Pop searched through Bungalo Park.
 Coming up on the recreational area, I saw Pop's station-wagon in the parking lot. Some children played on a swing set, a few adults walked through manicured grass, appearing to enjoy the evening.
 I walked into the park, past the baseball diamond and swimming pool to the tree line and followed it to the picnic area. In the midst of barbeque pits and wooden tables, Pop sat on one of the benches looking out on the horizon.
 "She can't be downtown, Pop, I looked through all the stores."
 My father seemed lost, overwhelmed as he shook his head.
 "I couldn't find her either. Maybe this time she left for good."
 My body tensed in disbelief because Mom wouldn't have gone away without saying good bye or taking me with her and besides where would she go? Aunt Cleonas? Schnecksville?
 "No. She wouldn't do that to us, not after all we've been through."
                                                            ****
 My memory thought back twelve years to 1947, as a five year old, first grader.
 "Vance Remke, you get over here, right now. Didn't I tell you not to take your father's tools out in the yard? Saws and hammers can hurt you and they'll rust sitting on the grass. I want you to find each one and put them back in the toolbox.
 "If your father saw those tools in the yard, he'd take it out on me, not you."
 "I'll be good, Mama. I promise, I won't do it again."
 "Yes, well, it won't matter if he finds out. I swear, if he touches me one more time, I'll leave and never come back. You hear me, I'll never come back. "
 "Maaamaa, pleassse don't leave me. I'm zzzorrry. I'll be good. Pleassse, pleassse don't leave me."
 As if someone flipped a switch, Mom stopped yelling at me and the expression on her face turned from anger to grief. She dropped to her knees and reached out to hug me.
 "My God, what have I done? Of course, I'll take you with me."
                                                            ****
 My daydream evaporated as I heard Pop trying to get my attention.
 "Boy. Hey, boy. Let's go. We have to check the valley. It might be a long shot but I don't know where else she would go."
 "Yeah, Pop. Also, she could have walked to the Ressie or visited with the Delk family in the hollow."
 We boarded the wagon one more time and headed back up the mountain, past our house and the pine tree forest across from the Johnson family.
 Parking in the New England Valley middle school lot, we ran across the busy road, ducked under the iron-gate and walked down the dirt road to the hollow where the Delks lived. I felt reluctant to bother them but Pop marched up to the front door and knocked until someone answered.
 After a few moments, an old, white haired woman came to the front door and peered at him through the screen door. Pop introduced himself.
 "Good evening Ma'am, Walter Remke here. My wife, Bernice, well, she seems to be missing. I know she visits you from time to time. Have you seen her today?"
 The woman sneered at him and crossed her arms.
 "So you come looking for the Misses? I know about you, Mr. Walter Remke. She told me everything. Like when her brother Gene beat the hell out of you in Cumberland. You hit her for the first time then, didn't you and her brother made you pay for it. But as fate would have it, you moved her to Schnecksville, away from her brother's justice. You seem to be one lucky bastard.
 "You hear me good, Mr. Remke, I don't know where Bernice might be but she deserves better than you. Now get the hell off my porch."
 The door slammed shut with a loud bang. Pop stood there, appearing dazed, looking at the ground and hands in his pockets. He seemed unable to say anything.
 I took several steps backward because knowing Pop, he would launch into a tirade of cuss words aimed at the door where Mrs. Delk had stood only a few moments ago.
 Instead, Pop turned toward me, a disgusted look on his face as he cursed in German.
 "Gott fir dumm seit. Listen to me boy, it never happened except in your mother's mind. One night after she had an argument with Agnes, your mother became quite upset. While in our room, she seemed to lose all control. I tried to calm your mother down but she took her rage out on my face. Your mother decked me and left a pretty good shiner.
 "I called your Uncle Gene to help out and between the both of us we managed to get her to sleep. He stayed overnight and when we left for work the next morning, some of the boarders saw my black and blue eye.
 "Nobody knew the real story, so the gossipers made up their own. Your mother didn't remember anything about what happened that night and I didn't want her to fester over the fact she hit me. To this day, she thinks Uncle Gene gave me the shiner."
 He spit on the ground, wiped his mouth in slow motion and shook his head. Pop continued to curse in German, but this time under his breath, as we walked down Mrs. Delk's driveway.
 I kept my distance because I hadn't seen him this mad in a long time.
 As we headed back toward the road, he grouched, "Let's get the hell out of here, boy."
 "Pop, we need to check the Ressie next. Sometimes, Mom likes to sit by the falls and listen to the roar of water on the rocks below. She might be there."
 We continued to walk the sloping road down to the broad murky reservoir and followed the shore line to the old dam constructed by federal workers in the 1930s.
 I followed the foot path so familiar to me, Pop right behind as we approached the deafening sound of water falling several hundred feet below at the mouth of the dam.
 Pop and I searched everywhere around the edge of the falls. No sign of Mom. I peered into the froth below, holding back the fear that she fell by accident or worse. Nothing.
 After searching for almost an hour, we gave up and walked back to the main road. And like a bad omen, the sky started to darken with black clouds. A sense of foreboding filled me as daylight gave way to evening.
 Without warning, it started to rain as we rushed to the vehicle and drove further out in the valley. First the light pitter, patter of small rain drops fell on the windshield as we passed the Garners place and Shoup's egg farm.
 Several minutes later, a heavy downpour fell, sounding like someone beating on the metal roof. Pop turned the windshield wipers on high as the rubber blades smacked back and forth on the glass.
 We passed Poter's place, Tasler's farm and Ladam's property. Now, I felt scared because I didn't know any of the houses beyond Wanamaker's Woods. Pop and I didn't talk as we stared out the windshield, in a trance-like state, looking for Mom.
 Houses gave way to dense wooded forest on both sides of the road. Bursts of lightning like photo flashes illuminated the rough textured tree tops covering the mountain as we ground mile after agonizing mile further into the valley.
 "She can't be here, Pop. No human being would walk through this place tonight."
 "Just keep looking, boy. We'll go all the way to Pottsville then we can head back home."
 Rain beat against the road like a beast gone mad while I watched Pop struggle with the steering wheel against the violent deluge. With each clap of thunder, my heart seemed to skip a beat.
 On the edge of my seat, I peered through squinted eyes at the shadows, real and imagined, that moved back and forth on the horizon.
 Trees and road, trees and road without end. Please, God, help us find her.
 Minutes ticked by in agonizing procession as we drove through that lonely stretch of road.
 When we climbed a small hill, I strained to see something moving at the top. At first, nothing more than a small speck bobbing up and down on the horizon.
 "Pop, I see something up there, on the right side of the road."
 "Yeah, let's take a look but don't get your hopes up. It could be anyone.
 My anticipation grew as we pulled up behind the sole figure walking through the rain. I looked for something familiar about this entity just ahead of us but darkness obscured everything.
 Pushing my face up to the side window, I strained to see this isolated person on the road. Shocked by the sight before me, I gasped for air.
 "Stop the car! Stop the car!"
 Mom, wearing only a house dress and full length apron, like she had just come from the kitchen, looked back at me then continued walking.
 I pushed the door open with all my strength, reached out and held her, yelling all the while, "Mom! Mom!" We just stood there for a moment as the rain pelted us, our clothes soaking wet.
 She acted strange as I opened the back door and helped her inside. Mom didn't seem to recognize me, the warm, loving look in her eyes, gone. My mother just stared at the torrent of rain coming down outside.
 Pop, looking almost uninvolved, just sat behind the steering wheel, a pained expression on his face.
 "Mom, please look here. It's Vance, your son. Do you know me?"
 A look of acknowledgement came back to her face.
 "I have to go home, dear."
 "We'll head back right now and get you dried off. Right, Pop?"
 He stared at the windshield without saying a word.
 "No. No. Aunt Cleona needs me. She'll make everything right once I get to her house."
 I forced the words, "Mom. Aunt Cleona lives over four hundred miles from here."
 I watched the life drain from her face as she looked past me to the rain outside.
 Pop turned the car around in the middle of the road and started the trek home, our home.
 My worst fear came true. Mom lost ground in the battle for her mind. She could walk away at any time and never return.
 As I struggled to cope with the emotions welling up inside me, I longed for the happy times about thirteen years earlier. I couldn't have been more than four.
                                                            ****
 The sky, powder blue, competed against the lush green of the surrounding mountains on a warm sunny day.
 A gray metal tub stood before me on the dirt driveway that ran from the road up to our small frame house. The receptacle had been filled with cold water from the well for my bath.
 "Vance, take off your underwear and get in."
 "Mommy, people will see me."
 "It's okay, dear, nobody will notice."
 I looked at my mother's warm smile and realized I had no choice. She reached under my arms, lifted me into the tub and washed the total of my physical existence until I met her inspection and approval.
 Mom dried me with a towel then I scurried to the house where she dressed me with clean clothes. We played and talked and enjoyed each other's company.
 I would never feel that closeness again.
                                                            ****
 Pop drove at a tedious rate of about forty miles an hour through the downpour, while Mom and I sat in the back. We salvaged our family for one more day, until the next time.
 When we arrived home, he pulled the station-wagon close to the front door so I could help her into the house.
 My mother walked to the laundry room, pulled a clean towel from the closet and headed upstairs. I followed, not far behind.
 "Mom, if you need anything, just let me know. Okay?"
 She stopped at her room, smiled at me and nodded, then closed the door behind her.
 I headed downstairs while Pop heated a pot of coffee on the stove. He sat at the kitchen table with a deck of cards, shuffling them over and over. I joined him, watching my father distracted in mindless activity.
 "Pop? Pop. We have to do something. There must be a way to help her. She can't go on like this, my mother one minute and no-one the next."
 Our eyes met and I observed a stranger, yet still my father. He seemed beaten, defeated and burdened with every trouble in the world.
 "What the hell do you want me to do? Send her to a shrink at fifty bucks an hour until he drains me dry. Those bastards can't help her. In all my life, I have never known anyone cured by those quacks.
 "And forget about the state institution. If you think she has problems now, just see what a year at Danville would do. I helped build an addition to the place in thirty nine.
 "I laid brick next to the Yard, a fenced-in place where crazies roamed the grounds. Some loonies screamed until they became hoarse, sitting in the sun all day, rocking back and forth with white froth running down their chin. Others just cried or moaned, hitting and slapping themselves until they keeled over.
 "Madness. Madness without end, surrounding her twenty four hours a day. Do you want that for your mother?"
 I felt numbness creeping through my arms and legs up to my face. No answer? No solution? No way to make things normal again? Silence filled the room as I struggled to find an answer.
 "So we do nothing?"
 Pop's eyes watered, as he dealt five cards to me and five to himself.
 "Deuces wild. Your play."
 I never saw my father show emotion before. It scared me because Mom's sickness seemed to go far beyond his control. He appeared to be deadlocked, unable to deal with the reality. In my heart, I knew Mom would only get worse.
 We played poker, hand after hand, as if we could ignore what had just happened. After an hour of losing more than winning, my stomach reminded me that we never had supper.
 "You hungry, Pop?"
 "Yeah. Go ask your mother to come down. We'll have something to eat, the three of us. I'll cook some hot dogs and maybe fry up some taters."
 I heard Pop rummaging through the fridge as I strode upstairs and knocked on Mom's door.
 Waiting for what seemed a long time, the knob turned and the door opened a few inches.
 "Mom? Can you come down? Pop has a mess of frankfurters and spuds cooking on the stove."
 I noticed she had dried off and changed clothes. Mom seemed better, in control and a look of determination on her face. She reached out and touched my cheek in a loving way.
 "In a minute, dear, right now, I need to tell you about the Laing Street house before it leaves me. You should know what happened at our family's boarding house in Maryland. Terrible things."
 "Sure, Mom, we can talk but just for a couple of minutes. Pop will be finished making dinner soon."
 I walked to the bed and sat down next to her. She grabbed my hand and gazed at me, as if struggling to find a place to start.
 "Your father and I met during the summer of twenty one. He stayed at my stepmother's boarding house where I worked as a cook's helper and server.
 "Pop worked for the Federal Construction Company, laying brick at the Corriganville prison. Your father stayed to himself but came to meals on time, had polite manners and drove a fancy black, Model T Ford. In the mornings, he wore denim overalls and after work, a dark blue, pinstripe suit.
 "Unlike a lot of the other boarders, he had a trade. Most of the men worked in the local tire plant as day laborers.
 "Your father seemed different than most folks. Always serious, his eyes, piercing, intimidating.
 "People in Cumberland knew everything about their neighbors but not a soul perceived the slightest clue as to the make-up of this man, Walter. A mystery which made him exciting to be around."
 "Mom, you never mentioned this before. Why now?"
 "Well, some days, I can't remember any of it, the boarding house, stepmother Agnes. Nothing."
 Not surprised, I understood and accepted the reality of Mom's illness. Like some bandit, it ransacked my mother's memories, leaving behind an empty soul.
 "I hated my stepmother. She tormented me every day, from the age of five, after my poor father, Harry, married her. It all started about a year after my mother died of Tuberculosis.
 "He had to wed the harpy for a lot of reasons besides the loss of Mama. He lost his right leg while working for the Baltimore Railroad. From that day forward, he couldn't support me, my sister, Cleona or brother Gene.
 "Harry coupled boxcars for the New York freight run. One day a steam engine lurched backward while my father sat spread eagle across the track, working on a hitch. The wheel cut his leg off just below the knee. No leg, no work.
 "Growing up, Agnes forced me to eat spoiled food, making me sick, sleep without blankets in the middle of winter and a thousand other acts of meanness. I can still hear the shrew's trash, You poor loveless child. I have no love to give you.
 "But I also feared Agnes. Soon after she married my father, the hag had an argument with him. When he turned his back, she hit him on the head with a piece of fire wood and sent him to the hospital.
 "Rumors also had it she stabbed a man in the chest because he demanded money owed him. The police questioned her but without witnesses, they had to release her. Many nights, I lay awake, listening for footsteps into my room, waiting for her to kill me.
 "The abuse didn't end there. She threatened to throw me out on the street unless I quit school and started work in her boarding house. What could I do? At sixteen, I felt trapped in a life of drudgery, until your father came along.
 "One evening, after finishing dinner, Walter stopped by the kitchen to scrape his plate. He asked me if I would join him for dinner and movie on a Saturday night."
 "Mom, did you and Pop, you know, for real, like each other back then?"
 "Yes, and I accepted his offer of a night on the town. Your father took me to one of the best restaurants. We ate thick porterhouse steaks smothered in mushrooms and drank sweet apple cider from champagne glasses. Later we watched Perils of Pauline at the cinema. His attention overwhelmed me.
 "Several days later, when Agnes learned of our date from a nosy boarder, I expected her to lash out at me. However, to my surprise, she seemed pleased. Then a cold sense of anxiety swept over me. Something felt very wrong. Agnes never acted happy about anything.
 "Your father and I dated through the summer, riding horses in the country and picnicking at Pinellas Park. Many times, we rented a row boat on the Potomac River, brought wine to drink and an umbrella to protect us from the midday sun. We stayed there for hours. Those times remain some of my happiest memories.
 "We married in October of twenty one, almost six months after your father and I met. The wedding, held at a justice of the peace on Dixie Street in Cumberland seems so clear to me today. A small reception took place afterward with some family and friends.
 "Agnes wasted no time welcoming my new husband to the family. The hag repulsed me with her sappy sweet talk about being delighted for us. I tried to warn him before the wedding, so many times, about the evil she possessed. Fear ruled my life, afraid of what she might do to me or worse, to the man I loved.
 "I moved from the servant's quarters to your father's room on the second floor the same night we married. However, several days later, my stepmother insisted Walter and I move to chambers on the ground level. She offered the large bedroom with its own bath as a wedding gift. Your father accepted her offer but I wanted to leave, move to another boarding house or a small apartment, anywhere away from Agnes."
 As I sat on Mom's bed, caught up in her turmoil from almost 40 years earlier, a voice interrupted us. Pop's voice.
 "Vance, Vance."
 "We'll talk later, Mom," I offered then walked to the stairs.
 "Yeah, Pop?"
 "We can eat now. I just put vittles on the table."
 "Okay, we'll be right down."
 "Your father cooked supper? This must be a special occasion."
 Curiosity tugged at me like a five year old with a hundred questions. Mom and Pop as lovers? And what about Agnes? Her name didn't exist in our house but during a visit to Maryland, I once heard Aunt Cleona say she earned a long, agonizing demise.
 We walked downstairs to find the table set with plates and silverware in perfect form. A steaming plate of wieners sat in the middle with bread, mustard, ketchup and sliced onions. The aroma of hot butter wafted from a dish of cubed taters.
 Pop, next to the stove, poured coffee into three white, porcelain cups. He set them on the table, then pulled out Mom's chair and waited for her to sit down. He nodded to me and we joined her.
 Silence flooded the room, until I reached out and helped myself to one of the frankfurters and a big helping of spuds. Seconds later, we indulged ourselves with eating and chatting about local gossip as if nothing had happened.
 My curiosity would have to wait. I didn't want to think about anything, not her illness or past. Only to savor the warm glow of a fleeting moment. Mom, Pop and me. A family.

Chapter 26
 Pop woke me early the next morning to get ready for work. I could hear Mom banging around in the kitchen, making breakfast. Before he started downstairs for coffee, I asked him about Agnes.
 "Last night, Mom told me about her stepmother. How she treated Mom so mean and even killed somebody. Did it really happen?"
 An awkward moment of silence prevailed as a look of surprise crossed Pop's face.
 "Your mother hasn't talked about her since we left Maryland and with good reason. Agnes could lie, steal and even kill with no conscience whatsoever. She put a hurt on your mother that will never go away. I don't understand why she would want to scratch open an old wound after all these years."
 "I think Mom wanted to tell me while she still could. She said terrible things happened at the boarding house. What bad stuff took place there?"
 "Agnes would have killed me and a lot of others, if not for your mother. Listen, boy, I've put that time behind me. If she wants to shovel up that heap of old memories, I can't stop her. But heed my words, don't go pushing her for answers if she changes her mind."
 "I won't. She wanted to talk about Agnes so I listened."
 Pop turned and walked downstairs, leaving me to ponder the significance of it all. Did events from so long ago push my mother to the brink of madness or would it have happened anyway?
 I dressed and prepared for another day of mixing cement and hauling bricks. The Colabs plant stood six feet tall with another four to go. The roofers would start delivering lumber and roof tiles soon.
 Before going downstairs, I crossed off yesterday from my calendar. But instead of putting my pencil away, I found myself tracing the X over and over. Then, as if possessed, filled with anger, I stabbed the small square on the calendar, again and again until the pencil point broke off. I wished yesterday had never happened.
 I took a deep breath, pushed it out of my mind and joined Mom and Pop in the kitchen. We ate breakfast, talked about the day ahead and acted like our world had never been altered by last night's events.
 Daily activities seemed the same but in the furthermost reaches of my mind I felt a compelling obligation. An imposed calling to be ever vigilant, watching for the slightest hint of irrational action or senseless rambling. I planned to tell the neighbors and ask for their help. I would assume the role of my mother's watchman.
 After we finished the morning meal, Pop checked our tools and loaded them into the car. We rolled down the driveway, turning west to Pottsville. The ride to work stirred up memories from last night, using the same road we found Mom wandering. What drove her to such lengths? A search for Aunt Cleona? Escape? Peace? I didn't know but I intended to find out.
 Pop and I pulled onto the grass field next to the Colabs plant, about thirty minutes early. A few bricklayers and apprentices arrived soon after and milled about. At seven, we started raising the scaffold for the final courses of cinder block. I hoped the walls would be finished soon, at least before school started.
 My father pointed toward the car, "Get my tools, boy, this block won't lay itself."
 "You sure about the leg, Pop? The doc said you should keep weight off it for another month. Besides, this scaffold sits awful high, you could get hurt if you fell off."
 "Just get the tools, boy. Let me worry about my leg."
 During my walk to the station-wagon, I saw Pop standing on the first rung then in slow motion, climb to the next and the next until he reached the top. My father took charge of organizing the area, lining up cinder blocks and checking scaffold boards.
 I brought his trowels and levels to the top and headed back down. As I started filling the cement mixer, I heard a familiar voice.
 "Hey, Speed. Let's get some mud up here."
 Squinting toward the top of the scaffold, the sun in my eyes, I saw Yeller working next to Pop.
 "How do you want it, Yeller? Firm or wet?"
 "Looks like a hot one today. Make it wet."
 "You got it."
                                                            ****
 The last two weeks in August passed much too soon. On my last day, a hot and humid Friday, one course of block remained. A crane started unloading steel beams from a flatbed truck, ready to set them as soon as the cement cured. I had hoped to be there when the roof went on but the following Monday, I'd be in school, a senior at Tamaqua High.
 At sun-up, I hustled back and forth from cement mixer to scaffold, moving mud and block. Morning gave way to noon and I joined our crew one final time, gathering in the shade of maple trees for lunch and local gossip. Apprentices, only a few years older, ribbed me about leaving a real job.
 The rest of the afternoon, I engrossed myself in labor but my mind raced, thinking about the direction of my life. Why couldn't I quit high school and become an apprentice? The crew respected me and it felt like I belonged. Why? Because Pop would never have allowed it. Yeah. I'd go back to school that year, because I had no choice but after graduation, I'd decide my own future.
 At four, I cleaned out the last of the cement and straightened up. In a few days, a new apprentice would take my place and I'd be sitting in a classroom. I looked around me, absorbing everything about that day, the building almost completed, bright blue sky overhead and lush green on the mountain range. Everything seemed in its proper place, in harmony, except for me. I felt like a transient, without roots, not belonging anywhere.
 Bricklayers and apprentices started coming down off the scaffolds packing up their tools and lunch boxes, getting ready to go home.
 As I waited for Pop, one of the apprentices walked over to me, "Hey Remke, good luck. Most of us wondered if you could tough it out for the summer but your old man said you wouldn't quit. Well, you proved him right and you worked just as hard as the rest of us."
 Before I had time to think about what he said, the apprentice shook my hand and headed toward his car. Then one by one the bricklayers came by and wished me well. Each had words of wisdom about life or surviving twelfth grade.
 As the crowd thinned out and cars started leaving, I saw Yeller coming down the scaffold, then hoist a bag of tools to his shoulder. He walked toward me with the tired gait of an old man.
 He dropped his tools, fidgeted for a moment, then asked, "So. You ready to head back to school?"
 "Yeah, I guess."
 "Well, if you ever want to talk, you know where to find me."
 "Thanks Yeller, that means a lot."
 "Okay, you take care of yourself."
 Yeller started to reach out his hand then gave me a bear hug. Just as quick, he grabbed his tools and walked toward the parking area.
 As I watched him drive off, I fought back tears and whispered to myself, "Goodbye Yeller."
 All the events in my life during the summer of 1959 had a great influence on how I viewed the world. Pop's leg seemed to be healing in spite of his pushing it to the limit. A strong friendship had been forged with Yeller because of our shared secret. Mom? I feared for her sanity and the disturbing story about Agnes upset me but I needed to know the rest. I hoped she'd have the chance to tell me.

Chapter 27
 Saturday morning arrived with the sun peeping over Sharpe Mountain and the smell of coffee waking me from a restless sleep. I struggled to get out of bed and find a clean pair of Levis. After several minutes, I wandered downstairs to find my mother tending the kitchen stove. She had water boiling in a tea kettle and bread toasting over hot coal embers.
 Rubbing my eyes, I mumbled, "Morning Mom."
 "Good morning, dear."
 "Pop out already?"
 "Yeah. He headed to the garden a while ago to tend some plants. Says he has a taste for rhubarb pie."
 I walked through the front door, followed a concrete pathway to the side and gazed out back. Somewhere between tomatoes and corn, Pop knelt on rich black soil, cutting stalks of the red fruit, then loading them into a wicker basket. He cultivated the ground around each plant before moving on to the next. My father seemed engrossed, inside the acre of bounty with everything from asparagus to zucchini. Without making noise, I walked back, careful not to disturb him.
 When I returned, I found Mom sitting near the kitchen table, dunking pieces of toast into a cup of what smelled like brown tea.
 "It looks like Pop will be out back most of the morning. Do you need help with the pies, Mom?"
 "No, but I'll bake one just for you, dear."
 "Thanks. I love fresh rhubarb from the garden."
 I grabbed the old coffee pot with a hot pad, poured a mug of brew and joined her. Strong morning sunlight poured through the kitchen window as I stirred sugar into my coffee and prepared to ask questions only she could answer. A wave of anxiety consumed me when I turned my chair to face her.
 "Mom, do you remember talking about your step-mother several weeks ago?"
 "Huh? No. Why would you say such a thing? I have no step-mother."
 Mom stared at me, appearing puzzled, struggling to respond.
 "My mother, Ida Mae Mosier Ridenbaugh birthed me in the summer of Nineteen Hundred. She gave me life and loved me. Do you understand, she loved me."
 In the time it took my body to recoil from her outburst, Mom pushed away from the table and walked to the garage. I thought she wanted to be alone, away from my prying question.
 Several minutes later, a loud horn sounded from the garage. I knew it had to be Pop's station-wagon. Over and over it blared. I ran to find Mom behind the steering wheel, bashing the horn with her fists.
 I pulled on the driver's door handle only to find it locked then tried the passenger's side, also secured. Panicked, I yelled and waved at Mom to stop. In the midst of my frantic efforts, I felt a hand on my shoulder. Pop walked past me to the front of the car, pulled the hood up and disconnected several wires.
 Silence, except for the rasp of my heavy breathing, saturated the air. My mother sat motionless, staring ahead, her bloodied hands on the wheel. I started to walk toward her side of the vehicle.
 "Just leave her alone. Can't do nothing until she comes out of it. You and I best head back to the kitchen."
 I felt helpless as I glanced at my mother one more time before leaving the garage.
 Pop demanded, "What the hell happened? She never went violent like this before."
 "Mom seemed so, normal. We sat around the table, just talking. Then I asked her about Agnes."
 "God damn it, boy. Didn't I tell you not to push your mother? Why do you think we never talked about Agnes before? She doesn't need you reminding her of the past."
 "I didn't force her. I only, well. Okay, okay, it'll never happen again."
 No explanation of good intentions could have made it right. I thought about Mom and my heart sank to depths I'd never felt before.
 "Listen boy, you be here when she comes out. Ya hear?"
 "What? You can't leave. She needs our help, right now."
 "You heard me. Stay here. I'll finish up in the garden and be back soon as I can."
 "What if Mom doesn't get better?"
 "Don't argue with me boy, she'll be okay."
 My father walked out and with reluctance, I started to clean up the kitchen. Every few minutes, I peered at the door to the garage, wondering how Pop could be so sure she'd recover.
 In an attempt to stay busy, I washed the morning's dishes, swept the floor and put more coal in the stove. Then I sat on the old wicker couch and waited. A constant tick-tock from the wall clock lulled me into a half sleep, half wake dimension where sub-conscious thoughts played tricks on my reality.
 Abrupt noises jarred me from my fuzzy world as I bolted up-right like a gazelle, head flitting back and forth. Mom stood at the sink, running water wide open, washing her hands. I rushed to her side then felt queasy, looking at my mother's raw, swollen knuckles.
 "Mom, Mom. What can I do?"
 She looked haggard with deep set, dark eyes.
 "My hands hurt. Did I fall down?"
 "Uh, yes, yes, you had an accident."
 I retrieved a tube of ointment from the medicine chest and lightly patted it on her hands.
 "I feel so tired, worn out to the bone. Please help me upstairs, dear."
 "Sure. Mom. You can hold on to me."
 She grabbed my arm and we walked up each step to her room. As my mother lay down, I found a quilt in the closet and covered her.
 "If you need me, I'll be downstairs."
 Before I left, she confided, "Tell your father I can't make the pies. Not today."
 "Soon as he comes in, Mom."
 She seemed to force a smile and then closed her eyes.
 I backed up to the door, trying to be quiet as possible and shut it behind me.
 Once downstairs, I looked for Pop in the bathroom and garage. Nowhere to be found. The garden? He still had to be there. I walked out back and saw him busy working the soil.
 "Hey Pop. You said you'd be right back. It's been over an hour. What happened?"
 "Couldn't come in until I sprayed the beans. Damn bugs chewed the hell out of these leaves."
 "You should have been there when Mom came out of the wagon. She seems drained, like the life has been sucked out of her. But this garden must be more important."
 Wiping sweat from his brow, he yapped, "Shut your god damn mouth, boy. I've been married to your mother for over thirty seven years and watched her get worse every year. From a frightened teenage that couldn't be alone to an adult who trusted no one."
 "But you left me, with Mom out-of-it and hurting herself."
 "Look. When we first married, I spent countless nights holding her until she fell asleep and smoothed over arguments with neighbors, who, your mother claimed, plotted against her. After leaving Cumberland, I couldn't take it any more. She'd have a spell and I'd nurse your mother until she recovered. Somewhere along the way, I realized, nothing helped but she always survived."
 "But you said Mom will only get worse. Where does it end?"
 "How the hell do I know? When she completely loses her mind and kills someone?"
 Pop seemed frustrated so I didn't want to ask any more questions. Feeling numb and depressed, I told him I'd be in the house. He went back to gardening. As I followed the path back, my mind raced, thinking about this strange bond that formed between us. And what about school on Monday? None of my friends knew about Mom and they never would.
 But someone did understand. My love, Reese. Once inside the house, I headed for the phone and cranked the handle.
 "Operator, I want the Arkov household on Mauch Chunk Street.
 After several long rings, I heard the sweetness of her voice.
 Reese? Can I come over? I need to see you."

Chapter 28
 Driving down the mountain with Tamaqua spread across the valley far below gave me time to think about Mom's craziness and Pop's inability to deal with it. I wanted to run far away from home, blend in with normal people and know peace. But guilt also consumed me. How could I leave when my family seemed to be festering like an old wound?
 Reese's two-story house sat on top of Mauch Chunk Street about four blocks from St. Joe's Church. I parallel parked on the narrow street in front, walked to the door and knocked. Soon, I heard footsteps and Reese appeared, wearing dungarees, white blouse and sneakers. Shoulder length, brown hair caressed her face. A vision of beauty, from petite nose to bewitching eyes.
 "Vance. Come here, you."
 She wrapped her arms around me and placed a kiss on my lips. I felt a rush of warmth across my face as I stepped back.
 "Reese, promise me you'll never change. You wear your feelings outside and I love you for it. I can't share the mixed up thoughts running through my head with anyone, except you.
 "Remember the first time we met? I'd just started Junior High down town and came to the malt shop for lunch. You hung out there with your girlfriends."
 "Yeah and you oozed of shyness, a farm boy from the valley. If I hadn't decided to pick on you, we never would have met."
 "Well, you reminded me of a spoiled kewpie doll. Always dressed up, hair never out of place and full of yourself. Without mercy, you teased me about being from the country."
 "Yet every noon you showed up at the Dairy Barn, ordered a chocolate milkshake to go with your bag lunch and stared across the counter at me."
 "I came because I knew you'd be there."
 "Did you like being tormented?"
 "No, I had a crush on you, even back then, only I didn't have the courage to say so."
 She reached out and put her hands on my shoulders.
 "You asked me out on a date several weeks later. Remember, farm boy?"
 "Yeah. You floored me, Reese."
 "Well, I can't believe it's been almost three years since we started going together."
 Her smile faded to a look of concern.
 "What happened at your house this morning? You sounded upset over the phone. Did you have another argument with your father?"
 "Well, sort of, but more. Listen, can we talk over lunch at the Five Point?"
 She grabbed my hand and pulled me close.
 "Sure. You know I care about your parents. The first time you brought me out to New England Valley to meet them, your Mom made such a fuss. She showed off her hand crochet doily collection, red tulips in a little flower garden out back and my favorite - your birth certificate, custom framed at Blatt's Framery in town.
 "Your Dad never said much though so I figured he didn't approve of me. When we first arrived, he seemed more interested in reading his newspaper. Things changed when we sat down to dinner though, your father and I started talking about high school and me being on the honor roll. Before we left, he actually smiled, thanked me for coming and hoped I'd visit again. I'll never forget."
 "He likes you, though he'd never say it out loud.
 "Hey, Reese. My watch says quarter to eleven. Let's head down to the diner before the lunch crowd."
 We jumped into my car and I peeled out, row houses whizzing by like smudged paint on a wall. Reese laughed as I indulged my sudden compulsion to speed down the biggest hill in Tamaqua. Adrenaline consumed every nerve ending. I mashed the gas pedal to the floor and watched the traffic light at the bottom turn red. It seemed to race at us like a freight train. Seconds ticked by while we passed the half way point.
 "Whoa. You pegged the speedometer, Vance. Slow down."
 "Just a little longer."
 Feeling free, like a bird, I didn't want to stop but certain destruction awaited us if I failed to make the signal. Hitting the brake hard, the car chattered as I watched the speedometer dive from 100 down to 70.
 Now, only several hundred feet separated us from traffic on the cross road. I grabbed the gear shift, pushed the clutch and rammed it into second. As the transmission engaged, a loud whine screamed all around us. The hood dipped and the speedometer charged toward zero within a few feet from the light.
 I smiled, then reached over to Reese, my heart racing, and kissed her hard and long until the sound of a horn bellowed behind me.
 She shook her head and looked at me like a disappointed mother, "Someday you won't be able to stop in time."
 "Maybe, but I can't help it. When I feel down, speed seems to kill the gloomy shadows inside."
 Reese snuggled up to me as I drove a reserved thirty five miles an hour for several blocks. I parked on Route 309, a one-way street running past Five Point and opened her door.
 "Come on, we'll have to hustle. It seems like everyone is in town this Saturday."
 Both of us ran across the busy intersection trying to avoid traffic. Once inside, we walked to the back, our favorite place, where a kiss could be stolen without incurring the wrath of a grouchy waitress.
 A pot belly man with greasy white pants, T shirt and cook's hat tossed some menus on our table.
 "Stella here will take your order."
 The woman smacked gum between her teeth as she reached for a pad in her apron.
 "What'll ya have, kids?"
 I asked Reese, "You want the usual burger?"
 She approved with a nod.
 "I'll have a cheese burger with ketchup. She'll have a burger with pickles and mustard. Also, two orders of fries and two chocolate milkshakes." I pulled a dollar from my pocket. "And some quarters for the Wurlitzer."
 Slugging the juke box, six for a quarter, I picked ballads like, Love Me Tender and True Love Ways."
 As we ate our meals to the background of romantic music, I confided in Reese all the disturbing events of the morning.
 "In two more days, I'll be off to school and Pop will head back to the Colabs job. Mom will be alone all day."
 "What about Mrs. Delk? She only lives a few houses away. Couldn't she drop in during the day for a few minutes?"
 "Yeah. My mother likes her. I think Mrs. Delk would be willing to check in on Mom."
 "Why don't you see her tomorrow and explain what happened. Then find out if she can start Monday. I'll go with if you want. And if it doesn't work out, we'll try something else."
 I smiled at this beautiful, caring girl in my life and would have surrendered my soul if she asked.
 "Okay. Yeah, I'd like you to come with me."
 We finished lunch and walked downtown, hand in hand, gazing through the store windows. The shops displayed jewelry, furniture, fall clothing, toys. Just past Hunter Street we stopped in front of the Bridal Boutique while Reese stared at the fancy dresses inside.
 After several moments, she smiled at me but not in the usual way. Her cheeks seemed bright red. Reese never blushed at anything except maybe the time her father caught us kissing on the front porch.
 I felt at a loss for words because wedding dresses might as well have been aliens. Struggling to say something, I blurted, "What?"
 She pointed to a white gown with sparkles from top to bottom and a tail that seemed at least five feet long.
 "Do you think I'd look pretty in that dress?"
 "Hell. You'd be the most gorgeous lass, this coal town has ever seen."
 Reese brushed a lock of hair from her face, took a deep breath and closed her eyes.
 "I've dreamed of what it might be like to throw the biggest wedding ever and walk down the aisle at St. Joes in a beautiful gown."
 "How come you never mentioned it before?"
 "Vance, you have so much to deal with, the time just never seemed right."
 "I don't understand."
 "We never seem to get past your parent's troubles long enough to talk about us. I love you but sometimes I wonder if we have a future."
 My heart skipped a beat as her words crashed into me like a twister through Kansas. I felt stunned by the pure truth of it. Problems, always my problems, heaped up like old baggage onto her shoulders. Never leaving my troubles on Sharpe Mountain long enough to share or understand her world.
 "How long have you felt like this, Reese?"
 "Since you returned from Cumberland and told me about your mother's illness."
 Thoughts buzzed around in my mind like a dog chasing its tail.
 Have I really changed since last year? Reese says so but I don't want to believe her. Why can't I feel it, sense it?
 I clenched my fists, determined to find some sort of balance between her needs and mine.
 "Don't give up on me. Someday I'll be the groom at the other end of that aisle. I mean to keep that promise, Reese Arkov."

Chapter 29
 I drove Reese back to her house then headed to the valley even though the thought of what I might find there, scared me. Pulling in the driveway, I noticed a blue 1939 Ford in front of the house. A vehicle I had seen somewhere before.
 Parking my car, I walked to the front door and heard a man's voice, although muffled it seemed familiar, a deep baritone like Orson Wells. My curiosity ended when I heard, Amen."
 I grimaced, knowing Pop had called the preacher, Brother Hawkins, from Lewistown Valley. Although my father embraced atheism and told the parson so in very clear terms, he'd call the old man, when Mom asked for him. This man of God looked like a vagrant from the mountains with long white hair and a grizzly beard. He wore farmer bib overalls and a red flannel shirt, even in the middle of summer.
 He worried me with his talk of fire, brimstone and salvation but if bible thumping could bring my mother some kind of solace, so be it.
 Entering the house, I found Mom and the preacher sitting at the kitchen table. Pop stood at the sink, cleaning rhubarb, cutting it into pieces then throwing the chunks into a large pot.
 My father stopped for a moment and inquired, "Where you been, boy?"
 "I drove into town to see Reese."
 "Yeah? Next time, bring her for a visit. Your mother would enjoy her company right about now."
 "I'll ask her."
 "Well, you know Brother Hawkins here. He'll be joining us for dinner."
 I glanced at him, afraid to say anything, lest he start preaching at me. Mom, looking pale, glassy eyed, held onto her dog eared, Old Testament handbook while the parson paged through a large bible sitting on the table in front of him.
 "Will you join us, son? Your mother and I have gathered in God's name to discuss the good book. The Apostle in Acts 28:8-9 says prayer will bring healing and cleanse the soul."
 I wanted to tell the preacher to leave me alone, that he should go back to the mountains and bother someone else. But I didn't. Instead I smiled and walked over to my mother. She gazed at me and I saw desperation.
 Sadness welled up inside me and my heart said stay, for Mom's sake, but I also thought of Reese. I wouldn't let myself drown in a tempest of emotion.
 "No Sir. I have to get ready for school on Monday but thank you for asking. Please, continue with your reading.
 "Mom, if you need me, I'll be upstairs."
 She gripped the handbook tight to her chest and nodded. A flick of my eyes to the right showed Pop with his back toward me. When it came to the bible thumper, he didn't much care what I did, as long as nothing interfered with Mom and her redeemer.
 I headed to my room and sorted through the dresser for a new looking pair of jeans and shirt. My clothes, worn and faded from many washings, filled most of the drawers. On a bottom shelf, I found two pair of virgin Levis, each the unblemished color of sky blue. Rummaging through my closet revealed several good hand-me-down cotton shirts in brown, blue and grey. Not much of a wardrobe but at least a start.
 Spread across an end table lie a stack of college brochures from places like Penn State, Pitt U, Lehigh University. I never had the motivation to look at them before. For the first time, I felt compelled to think about tomorrow. My future.
 Plopping down on the bed, I grabbed several catalogs and paged through, looking at the black and white pictures of students engaged in college life. It all seemed so far away from the little, rural community, I called home.
 Several hours later, the sound of someone traipsing up the stairs interrupted my reading. As I put away the brochures, Pop appeared at the doorway, a towel slung over his shoulder.
 "They finished. You best come down now. I need help cooking dinner while your mother talks with the parson."
 Jumping to my feet, I rubbed my eyes then peered at the stairway.
 "Do you think he can help Mom?"
 "I don't know, but for certain she believes in religion and God. Hell, just maybe, Brother Hawkins will give her whatever she needs to find a way back."
 We walked down to the kitchen where various pots and pans sat on the stove. I smelled the aroma of pot roast simmering while steam wafted from string beans and boiled potatoes.
 "I just drained the water off those spuds. Mash em up, boy. I'll handle the rest."
 Down on one knee, I poked around several kitchen drawers looking for the masher. After pulling out half the contents, I found it under the cake pans.
 Busy churning the taters, I watched the preacher and Mom, who now sat on the old wicker couch, talking back and forth. She seemed more animated, involved, than before while he appeared to be listening, hands folded together, eyes closed, nodding his head from time to time then responding.
 "Boy, scoop the spuds into this bowl and put em on the table."
 Pop slid a large white tureen in front of me then picked up the pot roast along with string beans and set them between plates on the table.
 My father interrupted, "Soon as you can break away, Brother Hawkins, we'll be serving dinner."
 Several minutes later, the preacher and Mom joined us. Before I could dig into the vittles, Brother Hawkins announced he'd say a prayer.
 "Lord, bless the food and family gathered around this table. Deliver Mrs. Remke from the power of Satan and the foul stench of his domain. Protect her soul from darkness. Make your light a shining beacon, so strong, all will know, this woman walks in the path of God. Amen."
 Pop, looking solemn, commended, "Thank you for those mighty fine words, reverend. Let's dig in."
 The table came alive with the passing of dishes and clinking of silverware. A strong taste of herbs from the garden permeated the pot roast while the green beans and taters needed salt. Pop set out two quart bottles of whole milk, delivered from Heisler's Dairy that morning.
 "You put on quite a meal, Mr. Remke. Where did you learn to cook?"
 "I led a bachelor's life back in 1910 during my apprenticeship in Palm. You can't eat out every day so you pick it up."
 For most of dinner the kitchen filled with the light banter of community gossip, politics and the weather. When the conversation ran dry, the preacher started treading on old ground. He failed so many times to win the battle of God against Atheism, yet he never gave up and neither did Pop.
 "Mrs. Remke needs the healing support of faith from her loved ones. If you accept God now, she will have a better chance to rally."
 "Reverend, I respect what you do for Bernice but save the lecture for your flock. You haven't heard me trying to convert you to Atheism."
 "But what about your soul, Mr. Remke? Don't you want to enter the kingdom of heaven with your wife?"
 "Listen Padre, you should worry more about meeting the test of your own faith. I can die with a smile and at peace because I've always lived true to my beliefs. Now, if you'll excuse me, I need to start cleaning dishes."
 I grabbed my plate and followed Pop to the sink, trying to stay busy cleaning up so Brother Hawkins wouldn't take his soul saving out on me. He helped Mom up from the table and walked with her, back to the couch but didn't sit down. Instead he grabbed his jacket, talked with my mother for a few minutes then stepped toward Pop.
 "Thank you for the delicious supper, Mr. Remke. I have a sermon to prepare for tomorrow so I must get back. By the way, for the record, you represent one of my greatest challenges."
 "Likewise, Brother Hawkins. Glad you enjoyed dinner. The Mrs. always looks forward to your visits."

Chapter 30
 I washed a sink full of dishes while Mom sat on the couch reading her bible. Pop emptied half a bag of sugar over the pot of rhubarb before setting it on the stove to simmer. Later, we finished the evening watching Bonanza and Milton Berle. It seemed so peaceful, the three of us in the same room enjoying television even as I fought off a sense of impending mayhem.
 Around nine, Mom trudged upstairs to her room while Pop stayed long enough to see the news. He refrigerated the pot of rhubarb before calling it a night and I watched wrestling for an hour then turned in.
 Exhausted, I welcomed sleep as an escape from all the events of that day. However, when I dozed off, dreams, horrible visions of being eaten alive by shadowy, vicious beings, consumed me. Thrashing about in a grey mist, I tried to scream, watching the flesh from my body being ripped away.
 Bolting upright and gasping for breath, I felt sweat all over my body. Reaching for the light, I squinted at the alarm clock.
 "Damn. Only a little after midnight."
 Too upset for sleep, I walked on tip toe in the dark, down to the kitchen for a glass of milk. Opening the refrigerator door, I heard something moving behind me.
 "You can't sleep either?"
 I found Mom sitting in the old rocking chair, her raw hands perched on the armrests.
 "What? Yeah. I had a night-mare and couldn't get back to sleep. How about you?"
 "I've been thinking about what happened yesterday morning, dear."
 I poured a glass of cold milk and sat next to her on the foot stool.
 "Do you recollect what took place after breakfast, Mom?"
 "Yes. I remember everything. You asked about Agnes and I flew off the handle. Sorry, you didn't deserve that. I'll try to reveal everything you want to know.
 "After Walter and I married, we moved into the first floor room of Agnes' boarding house. Life seemed better for a while. I found work at the knitting mill and your father continued to lay brick for the new prison.
 "At night I helped Agnes in the kitchen. I didn't want any part of her boarding house. But your grandfather, Harry, couldn't stand on his artificial leg anymore. At least not for long periods.
 "He used to prepare food and wash dishes for all the meals but his strength left him. No longer a railroad man, dad lost the will to fight. He sat in the living room by the front window. Invisible. I tried to help him but he just gave up.
 "Before your grandfather lost his leg to a boxcar, he raised Gene, Cleona and me, as best he could. When he married Agnes, she tended us along with her bastard child, Georgia.
 "Georgia's father, an alcoholic, spent most of his days at Murphy's Bar in the Irish quarter. He died from psoriasis of the liver and ended up buried by the side of the road near Siler's farm outside Cumberland. His fair weather friends wouldn't even take the time to bury him in potter's field.
 "She had two skills, running a boarding house and manipulating people for her own twisted purposes. Agnes used both to influence your father. She'd wait until Walter took a smoke on the back porch after supper and then joined him to work her evil. Agnes knew I hated cigarette smoke and wouldn't interfere with their little twosome.
 "I tolerated it for a while but my suspicions waved red flags when Walter started talking about considering an investment in the boarding house. He knew how I felt about anything to do with Agnes and her damn house so we argued about it all the time.
 "One night after supper, instead of starting dishes, I crawled on the floor, from our kitchen to the back door. Down on all fours, I pressed my ear to the wall and struggled to understand their voices, reduced to little more than mumbling.
 "I heard the shrew going on and on about raising room rents, kicking out tenants who couldn't pay, hiring a cook. She talked a great story but I knew better. Cumberland attracted poor people from the mountains who came looking for menial jobs. You had to go all the way to Baltimore for professionals with real money to spend.
 "They started to come in so I scurried to the sink and made noise, pretending to be in the middle of work. Your father walked toward the living room. I imagine he joined the rest of the boarders listening to the radio. Agnes stopped in the kitchen for a moment with an arrogant smirk on her face. She didn't know that I aimed to take it off, some day."
 "Mom, why didn't you and Pop just leave?"
 "In the beginning, I wanted to but then it turned into a personal vendetta. All my life, she offended me in every way possible. But when Agnes inserted herself amidst your father and me, I filled with anger beyond comprehension and prayed for the strength to commit murder.
 "My daydreams ran over and over like a film, as I wrapped my hands tight around her throat and squeezed with every muscle in my body. Her smirk replaced by shock, I could see the life drain from those evil eyes and feel her body go limp. In my mind, Agnes' fate had been chosen, and I welcomed my role as her executioner.
 "Your father never knew I eavesdropped on their private conversation. Our relationship had rubbed itself raw because he couldn't see through Agnes' veneer of sweet ooze to the foul ogress underneath. I lived in a world of compromise, holding a rising tide of rage at bay and being a dutiful wife.
 "Several weeks later, after a long day of weaving cotton at the mill, I came home to find several men installing a new gas heater in the basement. Cornering one of the installers, I asked who ordered the equipment. A sense of dread ran through me when he explained that Agnes paid cash for it. She always complained that the boarding house never made a profit. Deep down, I knew, your father ignored my pleas to stay away from her schemes. He had taken sides with my enemy."
 "Why would he do such a thing after you warned him about that despicable woman?"
 "At the time, I didn't care about answers, only a showdown. My emotions at the boiling point, I waited in our room, pacing back and forth like a caged animal until he came home. When Walter appeared at the door, I screamed at him and threw a book, striking his face. I punched him over and over until he grabbed my wrists then I kicked his shins. He yelled for an explanation and I screeched curses back.
 "A loud banging on the door interrupted our fight as voices in the hall demanded an explanation for the noise. Deadlocked, your father threw me backward, sending my body to the floor. He walked out, leaving me to face the boarders. I explained that we had an argument but it ended and everyone could go back to their own business. Soon afterward, I crawled into bed and cried myself to sleep.
 "The next morning, I dressed for work then looked for your father in the living room. I thought he might have slept on one of the couches but couldn't find him anywhere. Agnes sat in the kitchen, drinking coffee and chain smoking cigarettes. I bit my tongue and asked if she knew where Walter had gone. I figured she already knew about the fight but not why.
 "Agnes tried to upset me by saying my marriage failed. I ignored every word coming out of her mouth and started to leave. Before reaching the door, she told me he left for good because I had the smell of weakness, just like my dad. Refusing to show even the slightest reaction to her verbal bile, I turned around and walked to within inches of her face.
 "Fueled by hot, white coals of hate, I reached out and pinched the meaty part of her arm and told the hag to stay away from my husband. Looking annoyed, she pulled away from my clamped fingers, saying they had a business arrangement and I couldn't do anything about it. Poker faced, I warned Agnes that Walter would not tolerate her conniving for long.
 "As I stood there, staring at her, all I could think about was the milk white skin covering her Adam's apple and how easily it might be crushed. But not now, I whispered to myself."
"Mom? My whole life, you've never hurt anyone. I can't believe you could just, kill her."
 "Her death became a consuming obsession. However, finding your father and talking to him presented a much larger challenge. I called the mill from a phone in the lobby and told them not to expect me. Walter never missed work so I knew he'd show up there.
 "Blisters formed on the heels of my feet, walking seven miles from Laing Street to the prison. Few women drove cars back then so I had no choice. I reached the front gate around nine and asked the guard to see my husband. He gave me an escort to the far end of the property and pointed at an empty shell of a building devoid of windows and bars.
 "I waited while the escort went inside to find him. Several minutes later your father appeared, dressed in the same clothes he wore the previous day. When I saw his lip, red and swollen, I felt so bad, knowing my book throwing must have caused it.
 "Tears rolled down my face as I reached out to touch him. At first he stared at the ground then his eyes met mine. I wanted to say so much, explain my violent actions and beg his forgiveness. All that came out of my trembling lips sounded like a feeble attempt to ask if we could talk.
 "Your father told me everything about their bargain. In exchange for fifty percent ownership, he'd finance improvements in the boarding house. I shuddered, listening to him talk about the pact. A good investment, he called it.
 "I asked if he put their understanding in writing. He reached for his wallet and pulled out a folded piece of paper. As soon as he opened it, I recognized Agnes' scribbled handwriting. Without a doubt, Walter had signed a contract with the devil."
 Mom stopped talking and heaved a long sigh. I stood up and stretched, staring at the wall clock. It showed quarter after one and except for several lights on the horizon through the kitchen window, the world outside remained pitch black. My glass empty, I refilled the milk and pushed my footstool closer.
 "In spite of everything you told him about Agnes, why would Pop go through with such a venture?"
 "He had an urge that drove him to take risks. At first I thought your father hurt me on purpose but later found he couldn't control it. Long before you came into this world, Walter had started a chicken ranch and even manufactured socks during the late '20s. In the '30s, he formed a construction company that built homes. The boarding house became the first of his many obsessions.
 "We made up that morning but had an uneasy relationship over the days that followed. Although he shared the details of their agreement, how could I ever trust him again? I wanted to be involved with every improvement planned for the boarding house. The projects worried me because they seemed to cost a lot and included new carpeting, painted walls, more lighting and a new menu every week.
 "As for Agnes, on a chilly October morning in her basement office, I put the shrew on notice. I dealt myself in as part of the arrangement. No more kitchen duty and a say in all the upgrades.
 "She tried to intimidate me, sitting there with her arms folded and a repugnant look on her face. Her brown hair, pulled back tight, sat in a bun perched on top like a school nanny. Although she stood taller than me, I thought of how her thin, wiry body would surrender itself when I collected my due.
 "I chuckled to myself how this woman kept me in the grip of fear for most of my life. She hadn't changed over the years, still mean, petty and dangerous. At that point, I represented little more than an aggravation to her. No doubt, she wouldn't hesitate to turn Walter against me or worse, if I threatened to derail her scheme. But I didn't care anymore.
 "We stared at each other, looking for any sign of weakness. The only thought consuming me, remained the fact she had crossed the line, involving my husband. Agnes never acknowledged the declaration of my intent to be her watchdog. However, she threatened me, saying my dad might not be able to keep up his medicine if the improvements fell through. So, the game had begun, Agnes and I locked in a battle of wits with Walter and my dad as pawns.
 "The next few weeks turned into an unending string of hard days at the mill followed by afternoons confronting an array of installers, painters and a short-order cook. Although Agnes had full rein over those people during the day, I made sure everyone knew my husband paid their wages."
 As I listened to Mom recount those grim events, I saw anger, no, more like matter-of-fact conviction, spread across her face. Pride filled me. Despite her mental illness, she survived a terrible life filled with fear and hate. I needed to know everything, no matter the outcome.
 "Weeks slipped by and one by one, the workers completed their tasks. The boarding house had never looked so new and clean. Soon after, Agnes raised rents by fifty cents a month. Enough to make a nice profit but not so high it would drive the tenants away. The boarders groused at first but in the end, paid up.
 "With the boarding house starting to return our investment, December became a wonderful time, buying presents for Christmas and spending many hours visiting my sister Cleona nearby. I let myself get caught up in the rush of excitement; believing everything had worked out for the best.
 "After the holidays, when alone, disturbing thoughts rippled through the wake of my mind. I felt Agnes slithering in the shadows like a viper, waiting to ruin everything. Day and night, a sense of danger stabbed at my last shred of happiness, destroying it.
 "Although I struggled with the demons in my head, life went on. The winter of '22 pounded Cumberland with one snow storm after another. During the bad weather, most bricklayers filed for unemployment but your father had the good fortune to work inside. All the cell walls of the prison lie six inches thick, set with reinforced brick and the new wing had hundreds of them.
 "Bitter cold held the town captive almost until the end of March but the boarding house stayed warm because of Walter's investment in the new gas heater. God, I hated that furnace because it bound us to Agnes.
 "Spring came late in April with trees budding and grass standing tall on the lawns of houses bordering the entire distance of Laing Street.
 "The payments from our investment came on the first of every month, without fail, creating some doubt about my skepticism. But everything changed one evening when a boarder complained about his rent being raised twice in six months. I felt no surprise. Without a doubt, Agnes had surrendered to her greed, thinking we would never find out. My feelings had been vindicated but offered little satisfaction. I felt compelled to break into her office and find out if she took advantage of any others.
 "Finding a hammer and chisel from your father's tools, I broke the knob off her door and pushed it open. Light from the hallway streamed into her work space as I reached for a pull chain. The clicking sound of a light switch accompanied the harsh glare from a ceiling bulb over her desk.
 "The work table had four drawers but only one with a lock. I rammed a chisel behind the catch and hit it hard, sending splinters everywhere. My heart raced, hoping no one heard the noise. As I pulled on the compartment, stacks of dollar bills fell to the floor. I kneeled down and started to pick them up when something hit my head. Then nothingness."
 Without warning, silence filled the room. Mom's eyes welled up with tears. She seemed so sad that I reached out and hugged her. We just sat there, listening to the ticking of the wall clock.
 "Mom, you don't have to talk about it anymore."
 "No, dear. I must tell you. You should know and I need to be free of this heavy burden.
 "I awoke, on my back, feeling cold and my head throbbed. At first it seemed like a hallucination until I tried to move my legs. Struggling to sit up, I looked around and discovered loose soil covered me from the waist down. Then panic invaded, as my eyes adjusted to the dark. I lay in a shallow pit, inside a root cellar, at the far end of the basement.
 "Swallowing the urge to scream, I started to work free while scraping sounds echoed from a corner of the vault. Then, for a moment, silence, followed by a shuffling of feet toward me. I lay flat with my eyes closed, praying for God to protect me. A spray of rock and dirt hit my stomach while I held my breath.
 "As the sound of walking, moved away, I squinted long enough to see the back of a thin woman with brown hair. Agnes, dressed in her night gown, carried a spade to the back of the room. She scooped at the dirt floor and headed back toward me.
 "Adrenaline rushed through my body as each second ticked by, listening to her approach. Sensing when she poised to throw the black grit, I opened my eyes, reached up and grabbed the metal spade.
 "Agnes froze, long enough for me to wretch the shovel from her hands. I swung the handle in a long arc smashing into her knee. She fell like a handful of pick-up sticks.
 "I fought to pull myself from the musty earth as Agnes reached out for the shovel. I slammed my fist into her mouth, sending saliva and blood everywhere.
 "She lurched backward, rolling on her side. I stood up and walked toward Agnes, cautious of the evil laying in front of me. Pulling her over, I looked for a sign of life. Nothing, or so I thought.
 "Before I could react, she clutched my hair, pulling me down on top of her. Agnes threw a handful of dirt at my face and while I tried to clear my eyes, she hit me, breaking my nose. Even though she seemed like a hazy blur, I saw well enough to bite her hand. She emitted a guttural cry of pain, clutching her fingers and giving me time to get on my feet.
 "The furnace room had tools, something I could use to defend myself. I limped to the doorway, looking behind long enough to see Agnes struggling to get up."
 Mom's voice trailed off and her eyes closed. I felt relieved to see my mother fall asleep after recounting such violence. The time approached two as I wrapped a quilt around her. Huddling next to the rocking chair, I also claimed sanctuary from the world.
                                                            ****
 My slumber came to an end with a constant nudging at my shoulder.
 "Vance. Wake up, dear. I brewed a pot of coffee."
 Mom shoved a steaming mug of java into my hand. I stretched my legs and arms, trying to shake the fog from my head.
 "How long have I been asleep, Mom?"
 "About an hour."
 "Did you make it to the furnace room?"
 "Yes. A small bulb provided the only light as I plowed into an array of garden rakes, hoes and hand-trimmers. A clamor of noise rang through the cellar as I thrashed about. In an instant, I gripped a metal fence post then held my breath as someone moved behind me.
 "Agnes swung her shovel at my head, but missed, hitting a gas pipe instead. A rush of noxious gas filled the room, catching her off guard. I ran headlong into the unholy woman, knocking her to the ground. Raising the metal post high over my head, I imagined the feel of her skull crushing under the force of my swing.
 "Looking down at her face, I saw stark fear. I commanded all the forces of heaven and hell to sentence her to death by my hand. With arm muscles so taut they ached, I slammed the rod down.
 "I couldn't kill her. The pole rested inches from her head. All my dreams of revenge, unfulfilled.
 "Seconds later, I felt a burning in my lungs and struggled with every breath to stay conscious and keep my balance. The gas. Propane hung heavy in the air. If it reached the first floor, the pilot light in the kitchen stove would unleash the force of a thousand sticks of dynamite.
 "Agnes struggled to get up and managed to walk a few steps toward the door, clutching at her mouth. My only thought at that moment centered on the safety of the boarders. Fear pitted me in a race against time. I had to wake everyone and get them out of the house.
 "Somehow, I found the strength to get up the basement stairs, taking only a second to glance back and witness Agnes not far behind.
 "I screamed to get out while running through the hallways and banging on doors. Lights turned on. My Dad and other boarders came into the vestibule. I kept yelling until I reached our room. Pulling the covers off Walter, I shook, pinched and slapped your father until he woke up.
 "Wearing only his night clothes, he followed me to the front door where the foul smell of propane had already started to accumulate. Seconds, we only had seconds to get down the front porch. We never made it. I remember hearing a powerful, hollow sound. As if the world had burst into millions of pieces.
 "The force of the blast threw us onto the front lawn, rolling, bouncing through the grass and into our tulip bed. Several boarders helped us to our feet as we checked for broken arms or legs. Everything seemed okay except some minor burns and bruises. In the panic, I also forgot about dad, not knowing if he made it out.
 "Agnes' two-story, prairie house, lay in a smoldering heap in the middle of the lot. Chunks of roof, wall, window frame, even chimney sat there like some odd, violent, mural.
 "The wailing of sirens and bright lights coming down our avenue consumed my attention. Several huge, thundering, fire trucks with hordes of firemen soon covered the area, pulling up bits of debris, listening for anyone still alive.
 "Walter and I yelled into the crowd, looking for my dad, hoping he escaped in time. Pushing through the on-lookers, we found him sitting on the curb, without his artificial leg. He must have crawled on hands and knees, down the porch and out to the street. I hugged him, relieved, until he asked about Agnes.
 "Without warning, all the horrible events of the previous hours came back. Then it dawned on me, no one had the slightest inkling what occurred in our basement. I explained everything to your father including the fact Agnes may not have made it out alive.
 "The night passed like a slow cargo ship through the locks of Panama until the morning sun found its way to Laing Street. Heavy machinery equipped with thick gripping claws removed debris and loaded it on a truck. When they found a body, everyone stopped while medical attendants brought the poor soul from the wreckage. I demanded to look at the corpse and recognized the kind old gentleman from 2B, upstairs.
 "Later, your father and I, covered with blankets, sat on the running board of his 1921 Ford. We waited for Agnes. Since no one had seen her since the explosion, we assumed her body had to be in there, somewhere. Just before nine, we heard workmen yelling. Someone had been found alive. I prayed it would not be her.
 "We ran to the stretcher and tried to recognize a face covered in black silt. The figure, not even human looking, had no hair, burned to the scalp. I couldn't even look at it much less know if Agnes possessed that decimated carcass.
 "To my surprise, it struggled to speak. Not able to understand the inaudible ramblings, I leaned over and held my ear next to its mouth. Ripples of anger, repulsion and closure ran through my mind as I listened to Agnes' feeble threat to kill me.
 "Without hesitation, I grated back that she would soon wish she had died in the explosion. And that her days of killing anyone have come to an end.
 "I sneered at the place where her face used to be, spit in its eye and walked away, never looking back.
 "Walter nodded to the attendants and they took her to an awaiting van. I never saw Agnes again, except in my mind.
 "My sister, Cleona, took us in until the police and fire department examined the site. Poor, loyal dad stayed by Agnes' side at the hospital, even after they charged her with attempted murder.
 "How odd, she tried to kill me and in the process, blew up the house. Too bad, but she would never see the inside of a court room. No, Agnes didn't die but the woman's severe burns left her a crippled shell.
 "Days after the explosion, neighbors found dollar bills in their shrubs, trees, everywhere. We managed to salvage some pictures and an old brass clock. Everything else burned in the explosion. It didn't matter though, because something beautiful came out of all the ugliness. I carried a new life inside me, your brother Buck.
 "Your father and I wanted a fresh start for our baby, somewhere far away from Cumberland. Walter decided on his boy-hood town, Schnecksville, about sixty miles north of Philadelphia.
 "On a hot day in July, we packed a few possessions into the Ford, said our goodbyes to Aunt Cleona and dad then headed out of town. Traveling down Laing Avenue, one last time, I cried as we passed empty lot 306 with burnt grass, deep tire marks from all the fire equipment and exposed basement, sitting there like an open sore. After all the years of cruelty I endured at the hands of Agnes, God saw fit to return the same to her.
 "You know the rest, dear."
 I looked out the window at the orange glow of morning sun creeping over Sharpe Mountain and noticed the time on our kitchen clock, approaching five.
 Mom spent most of the night talking about a way of life which could have driven anyone mad. I no longer felt sorrow for her. She had the determination to survive the horrors of that fateful night. The woman sitting next to me on the old rocking chair became more than my mother. She fought with courage, like a warrior, and beat Agnes in every way possible.

Chapter 31
 Sunday morning, I felt so tired but wouldn't let myself sleep. So many things to do. Pick up Reese at eight and see Mom's friend, Mrs. Delk. Prepare for school on Monday and visit with the Michek family. I hoped Kooba and I could mine coal at least until the snows set in. His daughter remained a sore spot but it didn't matter. I planned to deal with her when and if the time came.
 We heard Pop stirring upstairs so Mom pulled eggs from the refrigerator and started frying potatoes for breakfast while I headed to the shower. As the hot spray washed over me, I reflected on my newfound knowledge.
 Nothing about our family had changed but now I understood. The two people who brought me into this world each carried the weight of having lived through an apocalypse. Gut wrenching, fight or die stuff. My thoughts of them seemed so different on that clear September day. I clenched my teeth and resolved to get on with the things important to me. Like they would.
 As I slipped out of the shower, Mom yelled, "How many eggs, dear?"
 "Just a couple, scrambled. Nothing else."
 I ate breakfast when Pop came downstairs but finished in double time, so I could get to Tamaqua and pick up Reese.
 The sky spread blue from the valley and beyond as I drove to town. Pulling up to the Arkov house, I knocked on the door and waited by the front porch as the Mrs. yelled for Reese. Her mother, dressed in a knee length, black skirt and starched white blouse, always looked so formal. It made me uncomfortable. Even more so, when she started asking questions. 
 "So Vance, what do you think about the new school season?"
