 "Well, I hope for a better year. Last session, one of my friends died and another killed his father. I've given up trying to make sense why those things happen. I just want to have fun and graduate."
 "You know, Reese won't finish for two more years. What do you plan to do after high school?"
 "I don't know. It seemed clear last fall. My father expects me to enlist in the service to get GI benefits. But now, going straight to college looks better even though my Pop would have a fit."
 About the time Mrs. Arkov started to ask another question, Reese swung the screen door open and stepped toward me. She put her arm on my shoulder and hugged me briefly. All the while, her mother looked us over, serious, but never offered a word.
 We said goodbye and climbed into my car. As I started the engine, an aroma of intoxicating perfume invaded my nostrils.
 "You smell good this morning, Reese."
 "Thanks. I wore it just for you, valley boy."
 Grabbing the gear shift, I pulled into first and headed down the hill. With Reese snuggling up to me, I put my arm around her and drove back to the valley. Turning left onto the frontage road just past Albert and Emma Johnson's place, I parked in a clearing near the Delk house. After walking down a dusty path, I knocked on her old, weathered, screen door. Reese stayed close, holding my hand.
 The door opened slow, rubbing against a warped board on the porch floor. Mrs. Delk appeared, wearing a bathrobe and looking sleepy until she saw me.
 "Good Morning, Ma'am."
 "Vance Remke, what brings you and the pretty girl here on a Sunday?"
 "Mrs. Delk, meet my girlfriend, Reese. We came to talk about my Mom."
 She nodded and ushered us into her living room, a small but clean place with white walls and uneven plank floor. A black and white television sat in one corner with a worn brown couch and recliner near the door.
 "Make yourself at ease while I fix up some mint tea."
 Mrs. Delk disappeared into another room where the sounds of metal and china clinking together overwhelmed any silence.
 We sat on the couch and looked at pictures hanging from her wall. Old shots of bearded men and stern looking women, frozen in time.
 The frail woman emerged, carrying three cups of tea. I lurched forward and grabbed two of them. She sat on the recliner and pulled several small bags out of her pocket and put them on the end table next to us.
 "Help yourself to the sugar, kids. Sorry, but I ran out of fresh milk."
 "Sugar will be fine, Ma'am. I noticed the stills on the wall, over by the television. They look like prints from the 1900s."
 She walked to the black framed pictures and straightened one, then stepped back, gazing at them with a look of warmth across her face.
 "Yeah. 1907 to be exact. They remind me I had a family once. My mother passed from old age, brother died in World War I, and lost a sister in childbirth at Coaldale Hospital. I have my husband yet. He works for the railroad on the Baltimore run.
 "I miss my father the most. He hanged himself at our farm out in the valley. My sister found him swinging from the barn rafters in the winter of 1920. My mother said he languished for several years, depressed over my brother's death in the war. I don't remember much. I couldn't have been more than five or six.
 "But enough about me. Has something bad happened to your Mama? You wouldn't be here otherwise. What did he do to her?"
 "This has nothing to do with my Pop. I came to ask for your help. Mom has been unsettled, long as I can remember. For years, just little things. Now, she wanders off and even injured herself last week while having a spell.
 "I start school on Monday and Pop will be working at the Pennfield Industrial Park until winter sets in. She rambles around our house all day without a soul to talk with. If you could visit with Mom once in a while during the week to check on her, I'd be at your service."
 Mrs. Delk looked at me with tired, old eyes.
 "I always felt this day would come, Vance."
 Tears rolled down her wrinkled face as she reached out and grabbed my hand.
 "Over the last twenty years, your Mama told me lots of stories. Some crazy, some not, about your Papa, neighbors and strangers. Most of the time, she considered everyone her enemy. Except you, your brothers and me. I never judged her, even when she rambled on about people trying to kill her. I must admit though, she scared the Hades out of me. Someday, I expected to read about her murder in the newspaper. Listen, your Mama needed someone to talk with and we enjoyed each others company."
 "I didn't know, Ma'am. Mom never talked about her visits. Thank you for being a good friend."
 "Well, we've been close since before you came into this world. I don't think anyone can help what ails your Mama but tell her to expect me in the mornings for a bit, after my husband leaves for work."
 Mrs. Delk reached into her pocket and pulled out a red flannel handkerchief. This kind lady dabbed at her eyes while I fought to control my own feelings. Sadness choked at my throat and knotted my stomach. Reese nuzzled her head against my arm and wiped at her cheeks.
 "How can I ever repay you, Ma'am?"
 "Just take care of your mother. She would do anything in the world for you."
 I nodded in agreement then stood up, feeling we had bothered her enough.
 "We should go now. Thanks for the tea."
 Reese and I headed for the door with Mrs. Delk behind us.
 As we walked onto the porch, I hugged the old woman and whispered, "God bless you, ma'am."

Chapter 32
 A fragile sense of triumph prevailed on the drive back to Tamaqua. Mom now had someone to look in on her. I just hoped it would be enough.
 But in the end, my thoughts pulled me to Mr. Michek and Pop. My father didn't approve of Kooba, unskilled, without a trade or union. Yet I intended to visit him one last time to discuss working in a black hole under St. Mary's Mountain.
 Bootlegging tugged at me like an addiction. I felt part of an exclusive club, knowing only a few would risk their lives just for independence, beholding to no one.
 "Reese, I'll see you tomorrow at school. Okay?" This afternoon I want to visit Mr. Michek about working the Tuscarora mine.
 "Please, not again. Haven't you had enough of that filthy work? You need to focus on school not some stupid job."
 "We've gone through this before. I don't know how to do anything else except maybe sling cement. One more season, then I'll call it quits."
 "Just shut up and drive me home."
 Reese didn't speak a word on the way back. When we arrived, she opened the car door and started to get out.
 "Wait a minute. Can we at least talk about it?"
 "No. No. You do whatever you want."
 She walked toward her house, never looking back. I sensed something very wrong. Reese had never been so upset before.
 Running after her, I yelled, "What the hell do you want from me?"
 With tears in her eyes, she wailed, "Everything about you fills me with pain. I need time to think, about us."
 I reached out to her but she backed up and shook her head, rebuffing any further discussion.
 "Just leave me alone."
 She stepped to the porch stairs and disappeared behind the front door.
 Numb with denial, I pulled myself together and headed to Lewistown Valley. I couldn't stop thinking about Reese, trying to understand what had just happened. Our relationship felt like a handful of sand sifting through my fingers. Helpless to stop it.
                                                            ****
 Warm air rushed through the car's wing window and flapped against my face as I pulled into Kooba's driveway. The farmhouse seemed deserted without his truck parked on the side and his youngest daughters running out to greet me.
 I debated whether to knock on the door or leave. Except for the prospect of confronting Anna, I wanted to see if anyone remained in the house.
 The sting of my argument with Reese, still fresh, pushed me to get out of the car. Knocking produced silence in return. As I started to leave, the sound of a motor hummed somewhere behind the house.
 Walking through high grass to the back yard, I found Kooba loading apples into a cider press. The smell of fresh Mackintosh squeezing filled the air. After finishing the batch, he turned off the machine and wiped his hands.
 He dressed in farmer overalls, flannel shirt and an old straw hat. Not the coal dust smeared clothes I remembered from last spring.
 "Good Morning, Kooba. I tried the front door but no one seems to be home."
 "Morning, Vance. Yeah. Sent the family off to church and breakfast at the Hometown Diner. It gives me a chance to make some Jack and talk with God right here, surrounded by his mountains.
 I felt self conscious, embarrassed by his words. Pop cursed Sunday as unholy. A day for the weak and confused. But I've learned to bury those thorny feelings and smooth them over with zombie, glaze-eyed nothingness.
 "You need to go back up there, into those mountains. Don't you, Vance? My family doesn't understand how the calling drives someone. But I know."
 His words struck me like a bolt of lightning. Reese just doesn't get it. My feelings, still chaffed and bruised, now turned to anger. I'll do what pleases me, not her.
 "Yea, Kooba. I want to mine some coal this fall. Do you?"
 "A part of me says yes but since last year I've had a bad feeling. Nothing ever bothered this old bootlegger before. I just can't shake it."
 "You don't want to work the mine anymore?"
 "Not so. I have to go back in spite of my gut acting up. We'll need some time to check the equipment. Two weeks okay?"
 "Yeah. I've don't have any obligations. Not anymore."
 One last time, Kooba and I shook hands, sealing our agreement to dig coal and sell it on the streets of Tamaqua.
 I faced the balance of the day fighting off a sense of emptiness. My world felt dead, flat, without Reese. And even though I wanted to see her, to reason things out, I had to concentrate on school.
 The early morning light of Monday found me dressed and sipping coffee at the kitchen table. Pop headed to Pottsville about an hour earlier and Mom started laundry. Anxiety filled me on this first day of school, something I had felt before, yet different, changed from last year.
 Waiting until the last possible moment, I satisfied my need to know Mom would be okay then headed to town.
 Instead of driving past all the students in front of school, I found a parking place several blocks away and pushed through the crowded entrance. Once inside, I searched the hallway for Reese but never found her. Walking upstairs seemed strange, it took three years to reach the top floor. Now my time had arrived.
 I pushed through a sea of classmates passing me down the hall. Some familiar, others not. Reaching home room, door 214, fellow students stood just inside, joking with some girls. But silence reigned when they saw me.
 "Hey, Vance. Anna Michek came through here looking for you. She seemed angry."
 "Yeah? It doesn't surprise me since we sort of have this bond. She hates me and I don't give a damn. Her father and I plan to work the St Mary's mine and the whole thing just doesn't agree with her."
 The first morning bell rang and I pushed any thought of Anna out of my consciousness. The day filled with excitement, experiencing new teachers and subjects. I refreshed old friendships, forged new ones and during lunch, looked for Reese. One of her girlfriends snitched that she had been avoiding me. I felt angry but didn't show it because she couldn't evade me forever.
 As the afternoon came to a close, I headed through the back alleys to my car. Turning the corner at Base Street, I saw some one sitting on the fender. A teenage girl, thin with black hair, wearing tight denim pants and a plain sleeveless blouse.
 I didn't have to see her mug to know who violated my space. Anna, of course. A reckoning had to happen some time so it might as well be now. She leaned back on one elbow, puffed on a cigarette then slid to the ground. She sauntered several steps toward me, took a long drag then blew smoke at me. My patience, grated thin, had come to an end.
 "You want to stay off my property."
 "Hmmph. You mean the junker behind me."
 She started to take another puff but never finished. I grabbed her wrist, clawed the butt out of her hand and threw it to the ground.
 Anna appeared to look startled or maybe just outraged. Before I took another breath she reached out with both hands and pushed me back, her sour puss staring at me. I couldn't hit a girl but it tempted me.
 "You have something on your mind, Anna?"
 Without warning, she started swinging at my face. I blocked her punches until she appeared to quit in frustration.
 "Not a fair fight, huh? You should have sent your boyfriend, Ace."
 "You mean that jerk ex-boyfriend? The son of a bitch cheated on me. Besides, I don't need him to fight my battles."
 "Too bad," I chuckled.
 "Don't change the subject, you bastard!" she yelled, "I told you to leave my father alone. He can't go back to bootlegging."
 "Look. Talk to him. Leave me out of this. I can't help you."
 "Oh no you don't, Vance Remke. This sits on your shoulders. No one else."
 "Don't try to blame me. He has a mind of his own."
 "Damn you! I'll make it easy to understand. His only son went off to Philly to get away from these mountains. You replaced him. Now get the hell out of his life."
 I never thought of our relationship in those terms. A good friend and business partner, yes. A father figure? Maybe I should have seen it. I just didn't know.
 But no matter how much she complicated things, it changed nothing. I gave my word to Kooba.
 "This can't be settled with fists, Anna. Go home."
 "Yeah? Maybe so. But I'll square you away somehow. I swear it. Don't get too comfortable. Anything can happen in those mines."
 "You try to threaten me? Get the hell out of here. From now on, I don't want to see you anywhere near me. Ever."
 As I shoved past her to my car, she scoffed, "Give my best to your girlfriend, Reese. Oh, I forgot. She broke up with you. Seems she doesn't like coal crackers either. My mistake. Ha, ha, ha."
 Ignoring her cynical comment, I jammed the gear shift into low and laid a patch of rubber. The screaming tires kicked up stones and dirt leaving her in a swirl of dust.
 Seething, on the ride home, I vowed to embed myself in school and illegal coal peddling. My mind had been set and just like Pop, stubborn about changing it.
 The days flowed like a fast stream on South Mountain, immersed in the daily study of Algebra, English, German and other academic subjects. But could I survive in this narrow corridor of pursuit once we went back into the coal business? I hoped time would prove me victorious.
 Mid September continued to be warm, delaying the change of leaves and fetching perfect weather for shoveling anthracite. Early on a Saturday morning, Kooba called and suggested we meet at the site.
 I felt excited yet apprehensive. My desire would soon be fulfilled but at the price of standing up to Pop. He hated the mines because too many good men died the same predictable way. A vein of coal would break free, killing anyone working underneath those tons of raw ore.
 The Tamaqua Courier bore witness with articles and pictures of each disaster. And somewhere in those columns, a sentence or two of the families left to mourn. But such a calamity would never befall us. We inspected everything, hoist, cable, cross beams, leaving chance or fate for others.
 Hanging up the phone, I noticed my father, in his favorite lounge chair, watching news on television. Forced to this moment in time, I would have preferred not to confront Pop but my path stood clear.
 At the coal stove, I poured a cup of coffee from our old aluminum percolator and sipped at the hot brew. My thoughts turned to just a few years earlier when I had feared him so. A child, filled with uncertainty and so many questions about my life in this family. Now, I prepared to talk, son to father.
 Finding my way to the couch, I sat on the edge, leaning forward like a cat, resting but capable of springing at any moment. Pop must have sensed my nervous tension. He interrupted watching the news, a curious look on his face.
 "Seems sort of early for you to be talking with Reese?"
 "She didn't call, Pop, not now or anytime soon. We had an argument and broke up."
 "Hmmph. Too bad. Your mother liked her."
 "Yeah. I know."
 "So who called?"
 "Mr. Michek. We aim to open the mine at St. Mary's again. Yes. I know how you feel about bootlegging but I've made up my mind."
 Without warning, I had his full attention as he stood up. Coming to my feet, his eyes riveted on mine.
 "You have it all figured out. Don't you?"
 "I don't want to fight, Pop."
 "God damn you. Those poor stinking souls, including your Mr. Michek, don't have a choice. Coal grubbing. Hand to mouth living and dying. You wallow in their misery long enough, it'll rub off, boy."
 "You don't understand. This will be my last year. Kooba knows it. Besides, he taught me everything about safety."
 "You got your head on sideways, boy. I saw a young man pulled out of the Run Hill mine in 1941. I worked right outside laying brick on their new electric sieve. You ever see a crushed body up close? They don't look human anymore. No chin or nose, just three holes about where you'd expect eyes and a mouth. And the rest of his corpse like a wrinkled balloon with the air sucked out.
 "Big-time company owned the mine and ran it. Hired the best diggers, had the best equipment. Yet there he laid, dead. Couldn't even show his body at the funeral.
 "Keep going back and the odds will catch up with you, boy. Nothing can guarantee you won't end up like him."
 "We just don't look at life the same way, Pop. I can't see us budging an inch from either side. But for sure, you and I share the identical stubborn will. Only one thing seems true, I made a commitment and have to follow it through. I believe you would do exactly the same."
 Pop stared at me for several seconds.
 "The danger rests on you, boy. Your mother would never survive if anything ever happened. Don't let her down."
 Filled with nervous exhaustion and unable to respond with words, I nodded my head in agreement.
 On the drive to Tuscarora, thoughts raced back and forth in my head. Pop and I reached some sort of fragile understanding. Would it last? And what about Reese? She pulled at all my emotions; love, anger and sadness while I struggled with the idea of opening the mine.
 Arriving at the base of St. Mary's mountain, I noticed Kooba's old truck parked in an open field, overgrown with weeds. He climbed out as I pulled up next to his vehicle.
 Nodding, he welcomed, "Vance."
 I responded, "Kooba."
 "Well, young man, you ready?"
 Looking up the steep incline, filled with trees and coal shale, I declared, "Yeah. I need to do this more than you know."
 Kooba reached into the bed of his truck and grabbed a tool box.
 "Vance, could you grab my gas can?"
 Stretching over the tailgate, I swung it to my shoulder and prodded, "Okay. Let's go."
 We paced ourselves, slow but constant steps with a break halfway. Resting, I sat next to Kooba as he puffed on a cigarette. The forest lay quiet, like a cemetery at midnight, except for an occasional chirp from the birds overhead.
 With time to reflect, my thoughts spilled over. Should I bring up the feud with his daughter? Yes, he deserved to know.
 "Kooba. I have to tell you about Anna."
 "Shhhh."
 He stood up and turned an ear toward the top of the mountain.
 "Can you hear it?"
 I closed my eyes and strained to listen. But for what?
 As the seconds ticked by, struggling to detect a single vibration in the ether, I sensed it. A motor, labored and intermittent.
 "God damn it, Vance. We've got claim jumpers. Follow me and try not to make any loud noises."
 He stepped forward, his pace energized but cautious and hunched forward like a cat on the prowl. As we approached the crest of the mountain, close to camp, Kooba motioned to stop.
 He whispered, "They might have a lookout so keep you eyes open."
 Kooba reached for his tool box and pulled out a tray. Stowed in the bottom, a dirty towel, smelling of motor oil, wrapped around something oblong. As he unfolded the rag, I gazed on a jet black handle, then blue stained hammer, cylinder and barrel. A pistol, with flawless finish, like it had rarely been used. He raised it up to eye level turning the cylinder, click by click, looking at each shell casing and its gray metal slug.
 The sight of his gun and the way Kooba handled it sent alarms clanging in my head. Somehow, we changed from simple bootleggers to vigilantes. A sense of panic filled me as he tucked the firearm under his belt.
 "Come on," he directed, as we circled in a wide arc around the mine.
 An intermittent roar of the hoist engine hid the sound of our steps as we took care to stay in the woods, just outside of camp. Unexpected, he grabbed the front of my shirt, forcing me to stop in mid stride. He crossed his lips with an index finger and pointed just ahead.
 A skinny lad, maybe early 20s with greasy hair, sat in front of a large oak tree. A shotgun rested on his lap and his head nodded forward every few seconds. He appeared to be falling asleep.
 In concert with the hoist, we approached him from behind. Kooba waited for silence then aimed his pistol at the lookout's head. The homeless looking fellow appeared startled and reached for his shotgun.
 Kooba pulled the hammer back on his pistol and the lookout stopped cold, surrendering his weapon. I grabbed the double barrel and pointed it straight at our claim jumper.
 We walked our hostage to the rear of the old Ford which had been converted to a hoist and Kooba knocked on the side window pointing his firearm direct at the driver's head.
 "Bring it up! Bring it up, you son of a bitch!" Kooba screamed.
 The operator, who couldn't have been more than sixteen, looked like a frightened mouse. First he put his hands in the air then at the urging of Kooba waving his pistol upward, pulled the bucket to the surface.
 Nothing could have prepared us for the digger riding our ore car to the top.
 "Ace Borman?" I exclaimed.
 He squinted at Kooba then smiled at me as he lit a cigarette.
 "Well, look at you, Remke. Big man with a shot gun. Hey, I've been sober for a whole week. Maybe this time, I'll kick your ass."
 Kooba shouted, "Shut the hell up and get off our equipment! You got a smart mouth for someone stealing our coal!"
 Borman edged off the bucket and stretched his arms as if on a country stroll.
 "Listen, old man, Anna doesn't even want you here. Yeah, I know her. In fact, we used to go steady. Now run home before you get hurt."
 Kooba pointed his gun at Borman's face, "Never mention my daughter's name again."
 "Okay. Okay. You win. How about letting us go?"
 Borman nodded at his bungling accomplices standing in front of me with their hands in the air. What would I do if they bolted for freedom? With the safety off and my finger on the trigger, I tried not to think about it.
 Whatever happened next hinged on Kooba. He appeared to be deliberating what to do with the claim jumper. But Borman started to become fidgety, with sweaty face and eyes darting from side to side.
 Like a clap of thunder out of nowhere, Borman yelled, "God damn it old man! Call it! Don't just stand there like a fool."
 As Kooba started to reply, the claim jumper snapped a hand inside his coat and pulled what seemed like a small silver pistol. The sound of a single gunshot rang through the forest.
 My two prisoners started to run but before they reached the edge of camp, I fired a round over their heads. They stopped and this time I made them lay on the ground.
 A light puff of smoke trailed from Kooba's pistol as Borman lay on the ground holding his arm. The color red oozed through a bullet hole in his jacket.
 To my surprise, Kooba drew a bead faster than summer lightning. While my heart beat like a steam locomotive at full speed, I felt a new-found admiration for my mentor.
 "Let's see what you got there," Kooba demanded as he reached for the tiny pistol and started to examine it. "You like sporting a Derringer? I always fancied it the pistol of choice by low lifes, card sharks and women. Guess we know where you fit in."
 "What should we do with these claim jumpers, Kooba? Turn them over to the police?"
 "The law can't arrest them unless the landowner files trespassing charges. Since the State of Pennsylvania owns this property, they'd take us all in. Bootleggers settle their own grievances. Once word gets around about these boys, they won't be welcome anywhere."
 "Give me your belt. Now," Kooba snarled as he walked to the youngest teenager. Grabbing the leather band, he kneeled over Ace and strapped it tight around his arm.
 "At least you won't bleed to death on my watch. Now get the hell out of here and don't even think about taking your bags of coal. They belong to us now."
 I watched the three misfits traipse out of camp like jackals denied their prey. My gut told me Borman would either seek revenge or move on to jump some other bootlegger's claim. Perhaps next time he won't be lucky enough to walk away.
 "Vance, I think you should have this toy. Maybe you can give it a better reputation than its former owner."
 He placed the Derringer in my hand. I stared at the gun, puzzled by everything that happened. This world of coal mining now seemed more threatening than cave-ins or Methane gas poisoning. I never thought of bad men looting god-fearing miners, maybe even killing them for a bag of coal. I didn't choose this but it'll take more than Ace to make me back off.
 "Kooba, do you think they might come back?"
 "I don't think so. But we need to keep this place safe. I'll bring my daughters up to handle lookout."
 "Uh, I've been meaning to tell you about Anna. We had a run-in last week."
 "She told me about the argument. You, me and the mine. Anna can be like her mother; head strong, protective of the family. But I don't blame you, she picked the fight. Look, we each have our own reasons for being here. Let's try to put it behind us. Okay?"
 "Sure. I just wanted to be straight up with you. If you need Anna to guard the camp, I'll just have to live with it."
 "Ha. Anna can't handle a piece. She hates guns. I wouldn't trust her. She'd shoot her damn foot off. I plan on asking the twins."
 "But your girls, I'll bet a shotgun weighs as much as them."
 "Yeah, but I taught my babies to shoot quail and pheasants since they could hold a gun without tipping over. Good shots, both of them."
 From that moment forward, I could feel a new reality chafing at me like black grit. Kooba and I went about setting up and inspecting the equipment like we always do. The sacks of coal Borman swiped sat near the entrance to the mine. We decided to load the bags into my trunk and sell them in town. Peddling from door to door in the same old neighborhoods as last year, I found familiar faces greeting me like a lost son.
 I had become a bootlegger again and it felt scary yet exciting at the same time. Time raced by as weeks turned into months and winter stripped the forest of its green, leaving the mountains a cold gray.
 Finding a life of my choosing, I adjusted to the pull and push of school, home and the mine.
                                                            ****
 Noon became a ritual of eating my bag lunch downtown at the 5 Point Diner then hanging out with friends in front of the school. On an uneventful day, my peace became interrupted when I heard someone yelling, "Help! Help me!"
 I saw a girl struggling to get out of a beat up 1947 Ford parked across the street. Also, a shadowy figure remained behind the steering wheel.
 At first I thought a couple might have started an argument and decided to let off some steam. But as the teenager limped across the street, I recognized Cassie Hanks.
 Her hair looked savaged like caught in a wind storm and blouse, ripped from buttons to armpit, flapped to the ground. She didn't even have a coat.
 Cassie stumbled toward me and collapsed in my arms. I could feel her shivering from the cold. Up close, I saw her bloody nose and both eyes, a puffy bluish black. I took off my jacket and wrapped it around her.
 "My God, what happened, Cassie?"
 She couldn't stop crying and managed to eek out the words, "He raped me."
 "Who did this?"
 "Ace, Ace Borman."
 Anger welled up inside me as I shouted at the driver, "Get out of the car, you god damn coward!"
 The Ford started up and pulled into the street. With the engine still running, Borman exited the vehicle and walked toward me. He sported a look of arrogance.
 "The bitch wanted twenty bucks for a piece of ass. Nobody charges me for something I get free. All she had to do is spread her legs for ten minutes. The little whore brought it on herself. Now, you want to do something about it, Remke?"
 "You bastard. You destroy people's lives like a plague, chewing up everything in your path. Somebody needs to put an end to you."
 "And you think you can do that?"
 "Maybe I will."
 Ace stepped forward and I pulled Cassie behind me. He stopped when my friends from school joined me. Borman looked at them, as if debating whether he wanted to fight all of us.
 "We'll meet again, Remke. You always seem to have some advantage going on, first Michek and now your buddies. Next time, maybe you won't be so lucky."
 "I don't think so but you know where to find me, Ace."
 He walked back to his car and peeled out, almost hitting a car. His muffler belched a deep bellows of sound that rattled out for blocks.
 "Thanks guys, for backing me up. Cassie, you need to see the school nurse and then the cops."
 "No. I just want to go home."
 "Listen, you need to get back at that bastard. He needs to be stopped before he does it to someone else."
 "Don't make me do this. No one wants to help me. I'm not worth saving."
 "Jesus Christ. You're a human being, not some lost animal. Please, let me help you."
 She closed her eyes, took a deep breath and whispered, "Okay, let's just get it over with."
 As we turned to head toward the nurse's office, we confronted the entire student body surrounding us. Word must have spread about what happened. I could feel her pull back as we faced them.
 "Just follow me, Cassie."
 After a few steps, she grabbed my arm, almost falling down. I picked Cassie up, her head lying against my shoulder and forced my way through the gathering of classmates. Their grim faces passed me, one by one as I walked up the marble steps to the lobby.
 "Once inside, more students held quiet like solemn observers. My eyes focused on the Nurses Station at the far end of the hall. Just when I thought this day had reached its worst moment, Reese appeared at the door to Sophomore Algebra.
 I tried not to watch but my heart wanted to soak in her vision. She glanced at me, a somber look across her face. Our time would come to talk, but not now.
 The door to the nurse's office stood propped open. As I lowered Cassie to one of the chairs, the attendant rushed to her, asking questions and tending to her wounds.
 Cassie begged, "Please stay?"
 "I'll just be in the way. Listen, about Ace, I promise, he will pay for what he did to you. Someway, the score will be settled."
 Forcing a smile, I backed up to the door and closed it behind me.
 She never showed up in class the next day or the weeks that followed. Rumor had it, she dropped out of school.
 When I turned to the hallway, a few students milled about waiting for the afternoon bell. Before reaching the stairs, I heard Reese's beautiful, honey coated voice behind me.
 "The whole school knows what you did today, standing up for her against a brute like Ace. Even someone like Cassie doesn't deserve to be raped. I just wanted you to know."
 Facing her, I fell in love all over again, yearning for her touch. Before I could say a word though, a student walked up and put his hand on her shoulder. I wanted to punch him in the mouth but instead blamed myself. I caused our break up.
 "We have to go. Class will start any minute now," uttered the kid who dared to put his fingers on my girl.
 "Ignoring the student, I probed, "How long until you forgive me, Reese?"
 She stared at me with troubled eyes, "Has anything changed?"
 "Yes. You avoid me instead of talking things out. What happened to us?"
 "Stop it. Don't make this about me. I've asked you not to go up in those mountains but you went anyway. I must not be important enough to you."
 Before I could respond, the bell spewed its raspy clanging sound throughout the school. Without a word, Reese walked past me to the west hallway. I watched her until she entered one of the classroom doors. Out of sight and no longer a part of my life.
 A few students ran down the corridor, trying to make it to class on time. But I felt no such urge as silence fell over the hallways. It didn't matter if I arrived late or ever, to my next class.

Chapter 33
 It seemed strange, surveying the woods around camp and waving at Kooba's two girls, each toting shotguns. One sat on a large boulder by the North gulley and the other perched on an oak limb near the South rim.
 "Don't worry, Vance, they can handle any trouble coming through those trees."
 "I believe you, Kooba, but everything seems so different now. Instead of just mining coal, we've become an armed camp. Three years ago, bootlegging came so easy. We dug up ore, chipped and bagged the black rock then headed for Tamaqua to sell it."
 "Times change, Vance. You should have seen the 1940s. Big time mines lay off workers by the hundreds. Everyone combed the mountains from Tamaqua to Pottsville looking to strike a claim. The desperate ones formed into gangs and jumped camps by force. Either run the owners off or killed them and then dumped the bodies at the bottom of a strip mine."
 We labored all that Saturday until late in the afternoon, taking our cue from the sun hanging heavy on the horizon. Kooba and I loaded the truck so it would be ready to go by first light on Sunday morning.
 "My truck needs gas for tomorrow's trip, Vance. Why don't you follow me over to the gas station in Mary-D? Then we can both head back to the valley from there."
 "Yeah. I should fill up, too. Meet you at the station."
 We headed out the dirt lane to Route 209 and traveled west for about a mile. The old station, dirty but efficient; provided gas, car repairs and local gossip for anybody who wanted to listen.
 As I pulled in, a familiar gonging sound, alerting an elderly attendant that he had customers. He limped out to the pump and started to fill our tanks.
 Kooba and I walked inside the station while the girls stayed in the truck. The old man finished and rang us up. Handing back some change, the attendant asked, "You hear about the suicide?"
 "No," I responded.
 "They found a kid over at Sutter's Park. The cops gassed up a few minutes before you and headed over there to check it out."
 "Any idea who?"
 "One of the deputies said they found a driver's license on the body. It sounded like Darryl Grainger."
 I stopped counting my change and looked at the old man in disbelief.
 "Darryl Grainger? My God, I go to school with him. Look, uh, Kooba, I have to go over there. I'll meet you tomorrow, first thing in the morning. Okay?"
 "Sure, Vance. I understand. Be at the farm by seven."
 Everybody in those mountains knew Sutter's Park. On warm, sunny days, you could see picnic tables or blankets spread along the tree rows. Families relaxed in the shade or lovers pitched woo in some remote corner. I never thought of it as a place to die.
 On a back road out of Mary-D, I saw the park ahead and came to a stop at the shoulder near the entrance. Two cop cars, an ambulance and a tow truck parked in the small lot out front.
 The police and what looked like a medical attendant clustered around a '50 or '51, green Buick. The hood and trunk had been popped up plus both doors swung wide open. I couldn't see anyone inside.
 I walked past the vehicles and headed toward an old man standing next to the tow truck. His face sported day old stubble and he smelled like sour cheese.
 The grizzled fellow nodded, "You related to the boy?"
 "No sir, but I knew him. We both went to Tamaqua High."
 "Well, no doubt, they won't let you over there. Maybe family and the newspaper people, nobody else."
 "Do you know what happened?"
 "According to Mary-D's police chief, the teenager just made bail on a murder rap yesterday. But he must have thought about killing himself for some time. See that hose jammed into the exhaust and run up to the driver's side window. The cops found a piece of plywood with a hole in it fitted to the opening.
 "He also had to be liquored up because they found a half bottle of Seagrams in his hand. Autopsy should confirm it. Looks like the boy didn't leave himself any way to back out of the deal at the last minute."
 Ignoring the tow truck driver's advice, I stepped toward the activity, hoping to get a glimpse of Darryl.
 All of it seemed so unreal. Perhaps seeing his body slumped over the wheel, I could accept his death.
 My fears became truth as I gazed through the open doorway and recognized his blond hair and lifeless face. I felt grief start to well up inside. Fighting to control the sadness, I asked myself why Darryl ended his life and in this place. Neither God nor man had an answer for me on that day.
 I stood there for a long time, unnoticed by the cops while they huddled over the car, taking pictures, wiping, dabbing and taking notes. Before flickering remnants of sun faded over Saint Mary's Mountain, the medical attendant and one of the cops walked to the ambulance, pulled a gurney and retrieved Darryl's limp remains.
 The other policeman, appearing to be finished, noticed me standing about ten feet away. He walked over, introduced himself and asked some questions about Darryl.
 As we talked, the other men took a second gurney to the passenger's side of Darryl's Buick, raising my curiosity. I watched as one of them leaned on the front seat and tugged at something. After struggling for a time, they covered the gurney with a blanket and headed toward the ambulance.
 Unable to hold my silence, I asked, "Who's on that gurney?"
 "A girl. We found her lying on the front seat."
 "Huh? Who?"
 "Can't say right now. She doesn't have any identification on her. But if you knew Darryl Grainger from school, we could save a lot of time if you recognize the girl's body too."
 Shock recoiled through me. Apprehension. Denial of what might be. I couldn't breathe and my legs refused to move.
 Forcing myself to walk toward their ambulance with the cop, I stood with the others, hovering over the blanket clad body.
 He pulled the shroud back, slow and steady, as brown hair emerged and framed a face that looked at peace with the world. Her eyes stared straight at the sky, unmoving.
 Feeling a rush from my stomach, throat and mouth, I staggered to the grass on all fours and threw up. Raspy, guttural, heaving sounds disguised my sobs as I mourned the death of a friend. A girl, lost to the ugliness of this place.
 "You all right, son?" one of the cops asked.
 "Yeah, yeah. I just need a minute."
 Struggling to my feet, I stepped back to the gurney and put my hand on her shoulder. So many words ran through my head. Something to express my sadness but my voice failed me. After a few moments of silence, I tried again.
 "Why? Why? You could have made it, Cassie. Now you'll never leave these mountains."

Chapter 34
 My life became a merry-go-round of bootlegging and school. Sometimes I didn't know where one started and the other ended. Kooba and I worked the mine after school then sold our bags on the weekend. Every morning, I woke before daybreak, started coffee and talked with Mom.
 If I had homework, it became a struggle to complete in the few hours before school or out of desperation, ended up stuffed in my textbook, undone. A sense of my life, unraveling, out of control nipped at the edges of my subconscious.
 At school, I wandered from class to class, unable to focus on any subject. My need for sleep and just not caring anymore became overwhelming.
 By afternoon, I fought to stay awake and often lost that battle. My head nodded back and forth or collapsed on the desk.
 How long could I keep pushing myself? The seeds of my fate had already started to show their ugly heads.
 Early November signaled the start of the first marking period. A two month summary of how well students performed. A time I did not want to face.
 I met the devil of my torment at the end of a cold, overcast day as students filed out of German class. As I walked past Mrs. Gruber's desk, she asked me to stay.
 "Vance, we need to discuss your progress."
 "Why? Have I failed the class?"
 "No, but If you don't pick up your grade, you won't pass for certain. I know you've been working at one of those wildcat mines after school. My father worked the number six colliery until 1952. He died in Coaldale Hospital last year of black lung. Why you want to risk your life bewilders me but I think it may be affecting your school work.
 "Well, thanks for the warning. Can I go now?"
 "Not yet. I taught your brothers German and they all passed. Bet you didn't know that.
 I sensed Mrs. Gruber about to lecture me on the need for a good education. The same talk our principal gave during a rally at the beginning of the semester.
 "Walter Jr. seemed quiet but had good manners and treated me with respect. He always tried his best. When the Army Recruiters showed up in January of 1942, he volunteered without hesitation to join the war effort. It seemed odd but after he signed on, your brother appeared eager to go and in fact, his grade improved by the time he graduated."
 "I didn't know," I responded and glanced at my watch.
 Folding her arms, I watched my teacher's eyes turn into slits of no-nonsense.
 "Vance, whether you like it or not, I'm going to have my say. Your bootlegging can wait. Understand?"
 Looking at the floor, I dragged a yeah out of my mouth.
 "I know the head of the class reunion committee of 1947. He tells me your brother Miles moved to Japan to work on a secret project for the government.
 "His classmates considered him one of the most popular students in school. He never seemed to let his celebrity get in the way of completing the course. Miles passed which means he cared enough to study his homework."
 By now, anything I might say would be useless. I felt glued to that spot until she let me go. Even though my brothers never discussed their school experience and everything she conveyed, sounded new to me, I didn't care.
 "Your brother Paul earned the nick name Senator. He had a natural talent for promoting the latest public opinion whether debating our involvement in Korea or criticizing the principal for eliminating our music program. He took this course, studied hard and passed with ease. His old classmates tell me he's stationed at an Air Force base near Frankfort."
 She stared at me, arms still folded, but not saying anything further. Silence flooded the room, making me nervous. I felt that she wanted some enlightened response like I had seen the light, I'm cured. Hallelujah.
 "What do you want from me? They passed, so what?"
 She stared at me with her lips pursed and eyebrows arched high. I sensed her anger.
 "When you start failing this class, it becomes my business. I suggest you think about what's more important, graduating from high school or working in a mine. That's all I have to say. You can go now."
 Her words echoed in my head as I left the room and walked down the hallway. Students prepared to go home, heading to their buses, while I struggled with questions invading my every thought.
 How did my life get to this point? Why couldn't I save Cassie, help my mother or get along with my father? And Reese, my only island of sanity. How could she turn her back on me with such ease? Answers evaded me like a baby chasing butterflies on a spring day.
                                                            ****
 The mine became a refuge from all the stings, bites and hurt in those mountains. I wore the grimy coal dust on my hands, face and in every fiber of my clothes like a badge of failure. I deserved to be there.
 While I struggled to survive, one day at a time, word traveled to other poor people that we had cheap coal. Although reluctant, we decided to split our efforts. I chose the south side because South Mountain with its treacherous incline had to be crossed into New Ringold. My car's trunk and seats didn't carry a lot of coal like Kooba's truck but the engine ran fine.
 On a Saturday morning, I headed out from home before daybreak. My Chevy labored in second gear, up the mountain's narrow and winding trail, straining under the load. I felt abandoned, isolated, looking at nothing but tree after tree for miles. Coming down the other side, I could smell brake pads smoldering as I struggled to keep the car from careening down an embankment.
 Once on the other side, I sold my bags of coal to farms that dotted the countryside. Everyone in Schuylkill County knew how those croppers struggled. They barely made a living off the red clay. A sorry excuse for top soil. The Chevy emptied fast and after selling the last bag, I headed back to Tamaqua to help Kooba.
 Soon, every week end became a ritual of ascending South Mountain. Not only to satisfy my life but for the needy souls on the other side.
 November passed and gave way to winter with its cold and snow. Unable to get through six foot drifts on St. Mary's Mountain, Kooba and I closed the mine until early spring.
 Just before Easter, we started bootlegging again. The mountains on my southern route became less scary and offered solitude, time to think. I traveled east on Route 895 toward 309 like so many times before with a full load of coal.
 But without warning, my peaceful journey came to an end. It started with the sound of metal crunching metal, my head snapping backwards and the screeching of tires on black top. Someone behind rammed my bumper and started to push me off the road. Panic set in as I struggled to keep control.
 The car pulled around me at high speed, its front fender mashed.
 Screaming, "Son of a bitch!" I recognized Ace Borman's beat up Ford fish tailing down 895.
 My 1947 Chevy spun across the road of oncoming traffic toward a steep ditch. As if in slow motion, I watched my car broadside the incline and start to list like a sinking ship.
 Rolling, rolling, upside down, right side up. Early on, my body soared through the driver's window, onto an embankment. Lying flat in the dirt, I endured the full weight of the Chevy twisting onto my back. I fought to breath but nothing moved, as if paralyzed. A second later, it continued, descending, turning over and over until it finally came to rest at the bottom.
 I looked around me, feeling my arms and legs for broken bones and breathed relief, except for some lacerations on my hands, everything seemed okay.
 My first thoughts made no rational sense for someone injured, lying at the bottom of a ditch. I'd have to bum a ride to town and find Kooba. He needed to pick up the load and make my rounds. Then I'll go looking for Ace.
 The reality of what happened set in when a car pulled to the shoulder of the road. A well dressed guy, maybe in his thirties, stood at the rim. He ran down the embankment and knelt next to me.
 "Anything broken?"
 I struggled to talk, "I don't think so."
 This Good Samaritan noticed the cuts on my hands.
 "You're bleeding."
 Reaching into his rear pocket, he pulled out a handkerchief and wrapped it around the worst gash.
 He ran back up to the shoulder, looked around and fixed his gaze on something in the distance.
 "One of those houses should have a phone. I'll call an ambulance. Try not to move."
 "Hey. I don't want a meat wagon. I have to get to Tamaqua. Can you give me a ride?"
 "What? Have you gone mad? You need to get to a hospital."
 He looked shocked for a moment then ran off.
 I had to move, even though spikes of pain ran through my body. A bloody trail followed me as I crawled up the incline to the road.
 Forcing myself to sit upright, I looked down at the hulk that had been my car. The roof appeared flattened and the doors sat open at odd angles. All my coal, twenty bags, seemed to be inside, untouched.
 My hands, wrapped in my shirt, had stopped bleeding and I managed to stand up by the time an ambulance arrived. Two guys, appearing to be in their twenties, sprang out of the meat wagon and started checking me over. When they brought out a stretcher, I held up my hand and waved it back.
 "Forget it, guys. Just patch me up."
 One of the attendants queried, "You sure you want to do that? You might have internal injuries that won't show up for days."
 "You don't understand. My car is loaded with coal. I have to get it picked up right away."
 "Okay, we'll put it in our report. You refused to get checked out at the hospital."
 I walked with a limp and had bandages on both hands but I managed to talk a bystander into giving me a ride.
 Before we could leave, the sheriff arrived in a black and white, four-door Plymouth with its siren wailing and a gizmo on the roof flashing red.
 A thin, almost frail old man, swiveled out of the car with a clipboard in hand and marched to the rear of the ambulance. He talked to one of the attendants, looking at me every once in a while. After some time, they shook hands and the cop scribbled something on his note pad.
 I felt nervous as the officer approached me even though he didn't seem intimidating.
 "Looks like you've had a nasty accident, son."
 "You want Ace Borman. He ran me off the road."
 "Whoa, hold up. Let's start at the beginning. The medic tells me you refused to get treatment at the hospital. Any particular reason you don't want the docs to look you over?"
 Gazing down at my car, I lamented, "I don't have time."
 "Because of the coal?"
 "Yeah."
 "Did you mine it?"
 "All twenty bags."
 "You don't look old enough to be a real bootlegger. The old timers, they know the hardship. My father scavenged coal out of the Middleport mines during the 1940s when times hurt real bad."
 "Can I go now?"
 Peering at the ambulance, he confessed, "I've always looked the other way when someone's just trying to get by. But I have to file an accident report with the state. Unless you can show a certificate of origin and tax stamp, I'll have to take you in."
 Letting out a long sigh, I reflected, "I don't have anything except the sweat it took to get that anthracite out of the ground. What happens to my coal?"
 "They'll impound it over in Pottsville. Every month, the state troopers auction off confiscated goods to the highest bidder. My guess? A coal dealer would buy your load."
 "At least arrest that bastard, Ace Borman. He rammed me into the ditch and then took off. Put his ass in jail, too."
 "Borman? I know him. He's been in our lock-up more than once. It looks like he's created a whole mess of trouble for you. We'll see about arranging some accommodations for him up at county"
 "Thanks."

Chapter 35
 The officer took me to Coaldale Hospital for x-rays and then the town jail. Several hours later, sitting in a dark, gloomy cell, one of the cops let me know my father posted bail.
 As I picked up my wallet and keys, I saw Pop in the lobby talking with the old cop. They seemed to take forever so I waited by the front door until my father finished his conversation.
 Pop strode out of the police station with me following behind. He didn't say a word until we reached the car. Once inside, he lit up a Camel and blew the smoke out his window.
 "They towed your car to the South Tamaqua junk yard. The damn thing is beyond repair.
 "From what the arresting officer told me, you've got trouble, boy. Some of it not your fault. But just the same, do you remember what the hell I said about bootleggers? They run with bottom-feeders. Now you'll have a chance to wallow in their brand of misery."
 I didn't want to argue with Pop because a big part of me already felt crushed, defeated, humbled outside of words.
 Slouching in the front passenger seat, my memory replayed the moment Borman hit my car and then his Ford swerved around me. I imagined chasing him down and smashing a baseball bat into his head while he begged for his life.
 Kooba called my house when I didn't show up at Owrigsburg Street by four in the afternoon.
 "Vance, I can't believe Borman ran you off the road. The boy has to be crazy."
 "The cops issued a warrant for the bastard's arrest but I want to find him first."
 "Listen, let them handle it. You don't need any more grief. Do they know the location of our mine?"
 "No. They only seemed concerned about their tax money."
 "Good. Tell them you bootlegged from the old McCabe dig over by Pottsville. Everybody has almost picked the place clean over the years and the cops know it.
 "They'll give you a smack on the hand, maybe tender a fine and send you home. In the meantime, I'll shut down the mine until this mess passes."
 Monday morning, it felt odd, standing out by the road, waiting for the school bus. As I climbed aboard and walked down the aisle, some familiar faces greeted me. Betty, sweet as a peach out of my father's orchard, greeted me with her beautiful smile. The guys from Lewistown Valley nodded then resumed their horse play.
 Arriving at school, I entered home room and started the day's classes but my life seemed different. A void followed me, the usual sense of urgency and overwhelming exhaustion, gone. In its place, a long breath of strange relief.
 As the next few weeks passed, I caught up on school work and watched in amazement as my grades improved. Mrs. Gruber smiled as she saw me enter class. Reese and I even started to talk again.
 But it didn't last. As I studied government in Civics class, a secretary called me to the principal's office. Once there, she opened his door and ushered me inside. He looked serious.
 "Sit down, uh, Vance.
 I expected he wanted to lecture or expel me for the trouble with bootlegging. But my misery was about to reach a new level.
 "Son, the Drivers Ed teacher will run you home right away. Take whatever time you need."
 "What? Why?"
 "Your mother called. The New England Fire Company responded to an alarm about thirty minutes ago at your house."
 My feelings went numb as I struggled to cope with the words he spit at me. Thoughts rattled back and forth without meaning as I stared at the floor.
 "Vance, Vance? Do you understand?"
 "Yeah, yeah. I better go."
 I ran through the halls to the back of school in the faculty parking lot. The driving teacher, Mr. Seger pulled up by me and reached over to open the passenger's door.
 "Hop in, Vance. Sorry to hear about your house."
 "Yeah. Thanks for giving me a ride."
 He crept through the back streets until we reached the town limits then everything came unglued as we whizzed up the hill over 100 mph. A loud rushing sound filled the car all the way to New England Valley.
 In minutes, we came up to the flat just past Millers barn. Ladder trucks, water trucks and an ambulance sat parked in my father's driveway while firemen scurried about.
 Mr. Seger pulled close to the fire equipment and stopped.
 "If you need help, let me know," he consoled, before I could grab the door handle. I nodded and jumped out of his car.
 As I sprinted up the driveway past noisy water pumps, cold spray hit my face, bringing me to a grim reality. My father's house, the place we lived, had caught fire and I couldn't stop it.
 Yellow flames licked inside the second floor window while hot burnt air invaded my nostrils. I jumped over several hoses, ran to the front door and tried to look inside but saw nothing but thick black smoke.
 A wet hand grabbed my shoulder and pulled me back. A burly fireman, I knew as Motto, yelled, "Get the hell out of here. If we get a back draft, it'll kill you, boy."
 Pulling away from him, I shouted back, "Have you seen my mother?"
 "Some of the guys took her over to the old house."
 "She okay?"
 He shook his head, "You better get over there."
 I ran to our unused rental home on the west end of Pop's land. Aged by time, the roof had started to sag and the doors wouldn't close unless given a firm kick. Just inside, Mrs. Kostner, from a few doors down, stood next to Mom.
 The neighbor, voice filled with strain, groaned, "Thank God, you've come. She can't stop crying."
 Grabbing an old wood chair, I sat down and wrapped my arm around her while a hodgepodge of people ran back and forth outside fighting the fire.
 I tried to sound composed, "We'll get through this, Mom."
 My forced calm turned to shock when I observed thick smears of black char on her face and hands. The bottom of her house dress framed with gnarled chunks of burned material.
 "I tried to stop it but the flames had me. My dress, then everything."
 "Mom? What started the fire?"
 She bowed her head and sobbed, "I don't know. Something smelled like burning rags. In the laundry room, I found a basket of towels on fire with flames high as the ceiling."
 We stayed in the old house, several neighbors, Mom and me. Listening to the sounds of chaos just outside. At least until Pop arrived.
 He stomped over the transom like an invading titan. His jaw squarely set, Pop grabbed his hat and threw it to the floor.
 My father barked, "What the hell have you done, woman?"
 She stood up and tried to yell through her sobs, "Kill me! Just kill me, you bastard!"
 "You burned the house down! Admit it!"
 I walked between them, "Mom says she's not sure what set the blaze."
 He looked at me with hardened eyes, "We'll find out soon enough."
 Pop walked out and started to talk with the firemen. He had a way about him that demanded attention, respect. It appeared, no matter how frantic the moment, each took time to explain their activity.
 Several hours later, the raging sound of fire equipment and crackling of burning wood came to an end. In its place, dripping water and the aroma of foul charcoal.
 Firefighters busied themselves rolling up hoses and checking equipment. One of the older firemen poked at some of the wood framing and electrical wiring. Pop stood near the front door watching the inspector until he finished then talked with him, pointing to different parts of the house.
 In the meantime, Salvation Army trucks pulled by the road, carrying boxes to the old house. It hit me like a fist to the gut. We had nothing. No clothes, bed, food, television. All gone.
 I waited until Pop finished talking with the fireman to approach him. He stood just inside the front door looking up through the burned out ceiling to the roof.
 After a long silence, my father spit on the wet mangle of charred floor.
 "The inspector can't be certain what started the fire. He'll bring in an expert from Harrisburg tomorrow.
 "Your brother Buck and I built this house from the ground up. The cinder block came from Allentown and bricks from Pottsville. It took us over a year, working in our spare time."
 "You can rebuild it. Can't you? The walls and roof seem okay."
 He laughed, "Look at me, boy. I'll reach sixty five in July. My days of building houses on weekends will never happen again.
 "We have some insurance but not enough to hire a contractor. I'll board the damn thing up and think on it for a while.
 "Take a good look at the old wood frame place we've been renting out for years. All we have left sets right over there. So help me, if she started this fire, I'll commit her to the state."
 I couldn't believe what he had just said about Mom. So casual, like it happened every day. Maybe he only wanted to blow off some anger. With everything happening around us, I tried not to think about it.
 Looking at the faded old house, I recalled a middle-age couple had rented the place about ten years ago. But when they left, it festered, empty. A coal stove sat in the living room, rusted from top to bottom. A few pieces of furniture sat scattered throughout the house. Old, unwanted remnants from a time long past.
 I watched the men finish their chores and drive the fire equipment back to the station. As we tried to find some way to busy ourselves, a stream of neighbors, Pop's co-workers and strangers dropped by with hot food and clothes.
 When the day came to an end, Pop lit several oil lamps given to us by a family friend. Mom and I sat on a mattress in the living room, talking through the terrible events of that day.
 "What happened after Pop and I left this morning?"
 "I, I washed up the breakfast dishes just like any other day. Started a load of laundry. Mrs. Delk came to visit around nine."
 "The laundry room. Do you remember anything out of place?"
 Mom looked peeved, "Dear, I know one thing for sure. My washing machine, laundry tub, iron, everything looked the same as it's always been."
 Swallowing hard, I prepared to ask, forced myself to mouth the words.
 "Can you remember everything about today?"
 Puzzled, she asked, "What do you mean?"
 "Well, sometimes you forget, stuff."
 "No. No. I remember everything. Even those strange noises inside the house."
 All my senses came to attention, stomach knotted, throat numbed.
 "Uh, what did the noises sound like?"
 "The floor in our laundry room creaked like someone walking on it. I looked but never saw anything."
 Mom grabbed my arm and rasped, "It had to be real. I swear."
 Gazing at my father across the room, reading a newspaper, I whispered, "Mom, please don't tell Pop about the noises. He has enough on his mind figuring out what to do tomorrow."
 "Okay, dear, for you."
 Thoughts of the state asylum stabbed at my brain, shredding all rational feelings, leaving me with a throbbing, assaulting headache. Even if Mom set the fire, how could Pop even consider sending her there?
 Next morning, I stayed with Mom, helping to make our aged rental house livable. Newspaper served as window curtains, a card table for meals and several weather beaten chairs filled out the living room.
 A large metal basin served as the sink for washing dishes. I brought water from a hand pump in the front yard.
 Pop drove to the hardware store in town, looking for sheets of plywood. He returned with a flat bed truck following behind. Two men, sporting dirty work clothes and day old stubble, unloaded the wood.
 We started to cut sheets to fit over broken windows. Pop, cigarette hanging off his lip, hissing some unrecognizable tune, measured the length and width then marked it off with a carpenter's pencil. I used one of Pop's saws he kept in the rear of the station-wagon with the rest of his tools.
 As the morning sun grew warm, a red Plymouth pulled into the driveway, interrupting our work. Large white letters on the door read Pennsylvania Fire Inspector.
 A young guy, looking not much older than me, stepped out of his car and unlocked the trunk. He gripped two black suitcases and clipboard then headed toward us.
 "Good Morning," he greeted, while trying to flip through several pages on his board. "I'm looking for, Mr. Remke."
 "You found him but people around here call me Walter."
 They shook hands while he responded, "I go by the name of Denis."
 "You the state arson specialist?"
 "Yes, among other things, uh, Walter."
 "Well, you'll find what used to be the front door just past the garage. I'll walk you over there."
 As the inspector prepared to enter the burned out hulk, I overheard Pop confide, "I need to know what happened in there, Denis. Can you do that for me?"
 "If someone started this fire, I'll be able to tell you where and how."
 My father sanctioned, "Well then, I'll let you get to it."
 We returned to the plywood and continued cutting our sheets.
 During a break, I confronted Pop, "I hope he doesn't find anything wrong but what if Mom started the fire? Did you mean it, about sending her to an asylum?"
 "What choice would I have? A rational person doesn't burn down their own house. It crosses the line between crazy and outright dangerous."
 I wanted to plead with him not to do it. Hearing Pop call my mother dangerous just didn't fit. Mom couldn't threaten anyone or anything.
 As we continued our work, Pop kept looking at his watch and at the burnt out door. The sun floated straight overhead when the inspector emerged, dirty and appearing immersed in note taking. After several minutes, he walked toward us, shaking his head and a grim look on his face.
 "I have bad news, Walter. It appears someone might have used an accelerant in the laundry room. The state police will want to start an investigation."
 Pop retorted, "What started the fire?"
 "Looks like cigarette lighter fluid. The stuff can be hard to trace. It has almost no smell and burns clean. Did you store lighter fluid in the house?"
 My father grimaced, "Yeah, I kept a can in the hall cabinet, next to the laundry room."
 "I'll put it in my report and advise the state police. They need to find that can or what's left of it."
 The inspector stowed his cases, talked with Pop for several minutes then drove away. My father stood in the driveway, watching his car until it disappeared over the horizon.
 I waited for him to return to the plywood, neatly stacked on top of our saw horses. As Pop approached me, I could see his face filled with anger.
 "You best go spend some time with your mother now."
 "What about the plywood? We could have all of it hung by the end of the day."
 His head reared, "Did you hear me? Get out of here, boy."
 "You can't do it, Pop. Please don't throw her away like a worn out pair of shoes. She deserves better from us."
 "Shut up. Just shut the hell up."
 He stepped past me, walking through the backyard garden. I watched as he disappeared into the tree line just below our mountain.
 From my earliest memory, it towered above our home like a mute sentinel. I wondered. Could it feel the misery in our lives?
 I avoided my father when he returned and Mom seemed quiet, moody, since the inspector left. What if she heard me arguing with Pop and put the pieces together?
 In the afternoon, with few words spoken between us, my father and I nailed up plywood over windows but left the door open for police. When we finished, a cold chill ran through me. Our home no longer existed.
 After we finished, my father washed up and drove into town. He wanted to spend some time at the West End Fire Company bar. No doubt, to drink beer with his bricklayer buddies and forget everything that happened over the last few days.
 Meanwhile, as the last remnants of sun slipped over the mountain, Mom and I cleaned laundry in a donated wash tub. We struggled to hang wet clothing on a line strung between two trees out behind the house.
 We waited for Pop to come home. Holding off supper while the hours dragged by. He never showed. Tired and hungry, Mom warmed tomato soup over a camp stove and made peanut butter sandwiches. We sat at the card table and ate by the light of an oil lamp.
 Later, as Mom washed dinner dishes and I dried them, she asked, "When do you plan on telling me about what happened today?"
 Then I knew. She had overheard everything. My eyes welled up as I tried to respond.
 "He wants to put you in a mental institution."
 Her mouth tightened, "Because of the fire?"
 "The inspector found traces of lighter fluid in the laundry room."
 "I, I remember smelling something like that just before the fire."
 "Mom, did you start it?"
 "No. Damn it. I don't know. Do you think I burned our house down?"
 Struggling with all the emotions inside me, I tried to give an answer from my heart.
 "You couldn't have done it. You walked twenty miles through desolate countryside in the rain and I watched you beat your hands until they bled. You only hurt yourself, Mom."
 I reached out and held her hand while we sat in the silence of that tired, old house. A place where my three older brothers and I ate, played, slept, and lived over fifteen years ago. Long before the new house had been built.
 As the night pushed beyond ten, Mon and I set up bedding on the floor of the living room. We gave up on Pop and decided not to wait for him. It became obvious he didn't want to come home. We turned off the lamps and settled in.
 I couldn't sleep though, thinking Pop might still be sitting at the bar getting drunk or worse. What if he ran off the road or hit someone? During the night though, I heard him stumble through the front door and collapse somewhere in the front bedroom. Relieved, knowing he made it home, I dosed off.

Chapter 36
 Morning light filtered through my squinted eyes as the damn mattress wiggled back and forth. I noticed Pop, kicking at it and pointing his finger toward the front door. Mom, still asleep, lay with the sun at her back. Grabbing my pants and shoes, I dressed while tip toeing outside.
 Pop whispered, "Your last semester wraps up in a month. I appreciate the help around here but you need to get back in school. I can take care of everything from now on."
 "Forget it. I know why you want me out of here. You'll pack up Mom and haul her off to the Harrisburg nut house."
 Pop curled his hands into fists and backed up.
 "Listen to me, boy, you've been off almost a week. Get the hell on the bus this morning and quit giving me grief. As for your mother, I'll make the decision, one way or the other, not you."
 My father demanded I go back to school although my gut said to stay home. Something told me, nothing short of a fist fight would have changed his mind.
 "She didn't set the fire. Just leave her be."
 Pop seemed matter-of fact, without emotion, "It depends on the docs. We'll talk when you get back."
                                                            ****
 I went to school but this time anger drove my every move. My mind focused on getting through one day to the next. In the meantime, I needed to get back to bootlegging. Not just for the money but to keep my mind off the fire and Mom.
 I asked around if anyone had a car for sale. Two days later, Mike Leotti, a fellow student, told me his father had a 1946 Chevy. Mr. Leotti could bring the car by the Five Point Diner in the afternoon.
 After school, instead of catching the bus home, I walked to the diner. Entering the front room, with its red and white, vinyl seats, I thought of Reese. So many times, we sat in one of the back booths, laughing, talking and enjoying each other's company. My heart couldn't deny it, I missed her.
 Pushing those bitter sweet thoughts out of my mind, I looked around and spotted Mike and a guy appearing to be, maybe, in his forties.
 "Hey, Vance. Over here. Meet my Dad."
 The adult thrust out his hand and pointed to the other side of the booth.
 In a measured, baritone voice, he welcomed me, "Vance, please, sit, relax."
 "Thank you, uh, Mr. Leotti."
 He sighed, "Such terrible times we live in. It broke my heart to read in the Tamaqua Courier about your place burning down. If your family has a need for anything, it would be my privilege to help."
 "Thanks, we put up in our old rental. It seems to be okay for now."
 "Good. It concerns me, about your father. We met about nine years ago. In the winter of '51, he built a fireplace in my living room.
 "Except, he did more than that. Walter restored my faith in people.
 "I needed the hearthside finished before Christmas. My two brothers planned to drive in from New York for the holidays.
 "You must be familiar with the mess of construction. I wanted everything wrapped up, spic and span before they arrived.
 "Your father came in two hours early every morning. He laid the last bricks in place a day before everyone arrived. Nothing could have humbled me more than when he refused to accept a penny for all his overtime. Walter told me to consider it a Christmas present.
 "When I have guests over, many times they talk about how elegant it looks. And I tell them about Walter. You must be quite proud of him."
 I tried to force a smile and forget the anger stirred up inside me from morning.
 "Yeah, everyone admires him."
 "My boy says you've had a run of bad luck. Rolled a '47 Chevy? And need a new car."
 As he talked, I noticed his nice clothes, clean shave and combed hair, unlike most of the men in town.
 "Yeah. I don't care about the body and interior but the engine and tires have to be in good shape."
 "Then I may have the vehicle for you."
 We left the restaurant and walked to the parking lot on Main Street. I paced around his black, fast back sedan. It looked used but well cared for. Climbing inside, I test drove the Chevy through town. Unlike my first car, I lacked any passion to inspect it further. The vehicle appeared to run well and would serve my need to haul bags of coal. I didn't even want to argue about the price.
 "I'll take it, Mr. Leotti. How much?"
 "That depends. Do you still own the '47 Chevy?"
 "Yeah, I have the title. But the damn thing sits in the South Tamaqua junk yard. It can't be fixed."
 "Does the engine still run?"
 "It should. All the damage seemed to be on the body."
 "Vance, I restore cars as a hobby and could use a '47 Chevy engine for my next project. I'll sell you this car in exchange for the engine and thirty dollars. How does that sound?"
 "More than generous, you have a deal. I have to be honest though, you could get a couple hundred for it, easy."
 Listen, Vance, in my line of work, I sometimes pass along a favor here and there to good people. Maybe someday you can show me the same consideration."
 "You have my word on it, Mr. Leotti."
 I felt torn between gratitude for the car and resentment that Pop's good will stole my sense of self reliance. My feelings melted down and swirled into an unrecognizable brew of guilt, love, hate and respect.
 After I pulled back into the parking lot, we discussed the details of our agreement. He planned to remove my engine on Saturday and I could pick up his car on Sunday.
 Before he left, we shook hands. I wondered, as they drove away, what line of work would let him restore cars as a hobby? I knew several guys who tinkered with the body or engine but they drove those autos every day. It puzzled me, how different Mr. Liotti seemed, from most everyone in my life.

Chapter 37
 I walked to the outskirts of town on Hunter Street and thumbed a ride home with one of my neighbors. Squeezing out of their Packard, I started running up the driveway, looking for Pop's station-wagon. Scurrying back and forth, I soon realized, this place, my world, stood silent, empty as the inside of our old rental. All the while, my mind raced with one question. What happened to Mom? I feared to know and just sat on the steps, staring at the mountain until the sun went down.
 Moving inside, I kneeled in front of an oil lamp and ate cold Spam in an attempt to quiet my growling stomach. Thoughts wandered to the outer boundaries of anxiety. If Pop and Mom didn't come home soon, I'd call the state police from my neighbor's house.
 After a sparse dinner, I lay on the floor, staring at peeled whitewash on the ceiling. It lulled me into a half sleep until flickering lights through the front window roused me. The mechanical churning of a car engine, idled and then went silent.
 The front door creaked open and Pop stood half way through the threshold for a moment, as if taking inventory of me before entering.
 Anxiety seeped into every crevice of my soul as I looked for Mom somewhere in the darkness behind him.
 He sighed then closed the door behind him.
 "The shrinks want to hold her for a few weeks to run some tests."
 I turned away from him and continued to stare at the chips of paint curled up from the drywall.
 He yelped, "Yeah. Yeah. I know how you feel. Get off it. Just look at the burned down house every time you want to blame me."
 "What if they decide to keep her there forever?"
 "I've don't want her in Harrisburg Refuge any more than you do. But she can't be trusted anymore. What if your mother burns this place down in our sleep?"
 "You won't even consider someone or something else started the fire."
 "Who? What? I haven't seen any evidence to the contrary."
 Ignoring his question, I turned the oil lamp higher and poured us some coffee from a hot plate.
 "I bought a car today."
 Pop forged a skeptical look.
 "You didn't waste any time. Where'd you find an auto?"
 "Mike Leotti's old man sold me a '46 Chevy."
 My father bolted upright, set his cup of mud on the table and pulled his chair facing mine. His brows pulled up tight, framing eyes bulged wide.
 "What did you give him for the car?"
 "The engine out of my '47 Chevy and thirty dollars."
 "What else?"
 "Nothing. Except I thought he could get a lot more."
 "You did it this time, boy. Vito Leotti runs gambling for most of eastern Pennsylvania. His brothers own all of the Brooklyn boroughs when it comes to prostitution."
 "How the hell could I have known?"
 "Just shut up and listen to me."
 "Finish the deal as agreed, to the letter. No changes unless he requests it and on time. What day?"
 "Sunday."
 "When?"
 "I don't know."
 "Jesus Christ. Call him and find out. Not Vito. The son, Mike."
 "Okay. Okay. I get it. Don't cross the man."
 "Boy, do not screw this up. He can hurt you. And decline any offer to work for him. Tell him you want to become a bricklayer, like me. Vito can understand the concept since he wants Mike to follow in his footsteps.
 "I gave him a pained expression and mumbled, "Fine."
 "Look, I consider Vito a friend. We don't judge each other. But he believes in a different moral code than us. He has killed men who crossed him. Believe me. You do not want to enter his world. Buy his car, but end it and walk away. You understand me, boy."
 "Yeah. End it and walk away."
 On Sunday, I met with Mr. Leotti and his son at their house just west of town. It sat back from the road, hidden by a curtain of trees and brush. They showed me the 1947 Chevy engine, pulled from my wrecked car, dangling from a hook on the back of their tow truck. A man dressed in greasy overalls, maybe their mechanic, had already started to strip it apart.
 Nervous, I squeezed, over and over again, the three ten dollar bills in my pocket. Afraid if I didn't, they would disappear and Mr Leotti might shoot me when it came time to pay.
 We walked into his garage, huge compared to my father's and sat around a black wrought iron table. He shuffled through some papers, looking at each one in detail. After some time, he signed one and placed it in front of me.
 "Okay, Vance. That takes care of the title. Any second thoughts? You seem quiet today."
 "No., No. I like the car."
 I handed Mr. Leotti the limp and sweaty money from my pocket.
 He grabbed one of the bills, "Let's just make it twenty for the car and use this ten to transfer the title."
 "Thanks, Mr. Leotti."
 "Hey, it makes me happy to do a favor for Walter's boy." He leaned over and confided, "If you ever need anything, I hope you would come to me."
 I remembered Pop's warning not to get involved. "You've been very generous to me, Mr Leotti. I'll never forget it."
 We walked to the driveway where my new car sat with all the windows rolled down. Mr. Leotti opened the driver's door and I slid inside.
 "I had one of my cogs clean it up for you, Vance."
 "You didn't have to do that."
 "Nonsense, I take care of my friends."
 I adjusted the rearview mirror and fidgeted with the radio. Before I started the car, Mr Leotti leaned in through the window.
 "My boy says you have a gripe with Ace Borman. He ran you off the road and you can't find him. I happen to know Ace works on an assembly line at one of the Ford plants in Detroit. Borman stays with his parents at 56 Elm on weekends. He lays low while in town because the cops have a warrant out for him."
 "How, how did you know where to find him?"
