 "One of my friends knows his family. It came up in conversation when he paid them a special visit."
 "I don't know what to say."
 "No need. I hope this will be useful to you."
 "Yes. I have a score to settle with him."
 "Be careful. That sorry excuse for a human being believes in nothing. He has no honor."
 "I will, sir."
 "Well, Vance. I won't keep you any longer. Aarivederci, amico."
 "Goodbye, Mr. Leotti."
 As I drove onto the highway, revenge seemed within my grasp. Yet a nagging thought wouldn't let me alone, now I owed Mr. Leotti a favor.

Chapter 38
 My self respect seemed restored as I drove to school on Monday. But having another car brought no joy to me. I parked on Owrigsburg Street, far away from Tamaqua High, so no one would see it.
 Life deprived me of Mom, Reese and even my home, everything I valued. Anger and pain sat in the pit of my stomach like too many green apples.
 At school, classmates occupied their time with giddy talk of our graduation ceremony, asking a date to prom and road trips to New York.
 My thoughts floundered with visions of grimy bootlegging, revenge and emptiness. I felt like hitting someone.
 Later that day, I drove past Ace Fowler's house. A pugnacious appearing woman, middle aged, with salt and pepper hair, stood on the front porch. She wore faded Levis, tie-dye T-shirt and puffed on a cigarette like an out of control smoke stack. My desire for pay-back pushed me to go around the block and park in front of her.
 "Afternoon, Ma'am."
 "Can I help you with something?"
 "I have a message for Ace."
 "You know my boy?"
 "Yeah. Tell him Vance Remke dropped by. You let him know the cops will pick him up real soon and take his ass to jail."
 She looked puzzled for a moment then put one hand on her hip while stoking that ciggy butt like crazy. Then the woman broadcast the meanest expression I had ever seen.
 "Listen, you son of a bitch. I'll come over there and smash your face. No one threatens me or my family in front of my own house."
 "Yeah. Well, I don't give a damn. Ace can try and hide but he should give himself up. It'll go easier on him in the long run."
 As she jumped the porch railing, I shifted into low and pulled away, watching her in my rear view mirror. Even though it tempted me, I wouldn't fight a woman. Not even Ace's mother. She stood in the middle of the street and snapped her smoke toward the rear of my car. Her final act of hostility.
 Smiling, everything inside me sensed that Ace would know about my visit before I reached the police station.
 Stepping into the familiar lobby, I approached an athletic looking sergeant.
 "How can I help you, son?"
 "I have information on a fugitive."
 "Name?"
 "Ace Borman."
 "Okay. Let's just take a look at your Mr. Borman."
 He opened a drawer and thumbed through numerous files before pulling one out.
 "Yep. Open warrant. Flight to avoid prosecution. Hit and run.
 "Looks like he's been arrested before, drunk and disorderly, theft, domestic violence, assault with a deadly weapon, sexual assault. Whew. He has been busy.
 "Well, well, what have we here. Attempted murder, two years in juvey before he reached fifteen years old.
 "Son, you better avoid him like a bad case of polio. He appears to be a real nasty fellow."
 No surprise, shock or even fear. Everything I felt could be distilled into a massive brew of revenge.
 "So, what kind of information do you have about our Mr. Borman."
 "You can find him in Detroit at one of the Ford plants or at his parent's place here in Tamaqua on weekends."
 "Appreciate your help, son. We'll notify the Detroit Metro Police of his whereabouts over there."
 The sergeant took my name and a neighbor's telephone number so he could let me know when they arrested him.
                                                            ****
 I parked behind our rental house so my car couldn't be seen from the road. A strong dose of paranoia dominated my actions from then on. Before entering the old property, I looked for cars on the road and scanned the property line in all directions. Seeing nothing but a few neighbors next door, I went in and closed the door behind me.
 Walking to a small fridge donated by the fire company, I opened a bottle of coke and slouched onto my mattress with a pillow propped behind me. I grabbed my English text book and read through several chapters. My assignment for the next day.
 Not long after, my father arrived with hoagies for dinner and I made a pot of coffee. What a pair we had become without Mom. My diet consisted of sandwiches and java.
 As darkness started to creep over the valley, Pop settled in with his second cup of brew. He reached for his coat, pulled out several pieces of mail and tossed one to me.
 "This has your name on it, boy. From the return address, it looks like the Schuylkill County Court caught up with you."
 I stared at the official looking, brown envelope with Vance Remke typed in bold black letters. My heart raced as I ripped it open and read the letter inside.
 "It says I have to appear in front of Judge Adams on May tenth at nine in the morning."
 "Well, you better be there. Whatever punishment he gives you, take it like a man. You hear me?"
 "I'll be there but what about you? Can I expect you to show up?"
 "Don't worry about me, boy. I'll check in on the job site around six to make sure everything gets set up for the day then meet you there."
 The Pottsville courthouse looked old and depressing with Roman pillars and weathered concrete steps. I trudged up a long series of stairs to the huge oak front doors. Just inside, a fat, bald guard eyed me over as I scanned the hallway for courtroom number five.
 I pushed myself to walk a dark hallway, littered with seedy looking characters milling about, while following signs leading to a door marked Court of Schuylkill County. End of the line.
 In contrast to the corridor outside, I shaded my eyes, adjusting to the bright lights of the courtroom. And quiet. Every creak of the floor resonated throughout the room. I took a seat in the back, hoping to be unnoticed until my turn to face the judge.
 I combed my hair straight back instead of the usual Elvis look and wore a pair of my brother Miles' hand-me-down black slacks and white shirt.
 As the minutes ticked by, most of those unshaven, unwashed men, I had seen in the hall wandered in and found seats up front. They had an air of familiarity with the room and each other. I kept my head down and hoped they wouldn't notice me. All the while, I glanced at the door, expecting Pop to enter at any moment.
 At nine the bailiff entered, barked out an order to rise and announced the judge. A short young man sporting black hair and flapping robes walked with vigor to the bench. In a prompt manner, he called the court to order and picked from the top of his neatly sorted files.
 As the bailiff called the first man up, I panicked. What the hell happened to Pop? Sweat ran down my neck and into my shirt collar. The urge to vomit seemed so real even though I had no breakfast.
 These men, pathetic human beings, one by one, answered for an array of petty crimes - public drunkenness, petty theft. My brain pulsed. When? When would he call my name?
 I flinched, jerked and twitched like a crazy man. Looking. Looking over my shoulder for Pop.
 "Vance Remke. The State versus Vance Remke."
 It felt as though someone slapped me in the face. Struggling to my feet, I held onto the bench for a moment, waiting for my legs to catch up.
 Thinking my father had abandoned me, I stopped expecting him and walked toward the bench.
 "You have been charged with tax evasion and illegal possession of anthracite. How do you plead?" the judge asked.
 Before I could answer, a well dressed, older man, walked up next to me. He looked familiar. In the recesses of my mind, I fought to remember. Then it hit me, one of Pop's friends.
 How long had it been since the last time we met? At least ten years ago. He laid brick, just like my father but studied at night to become an attorney. I always wondered what happened to him.
 I turned around again, searching for my father but only strangers met my gaze.
 "Your honor, I represent Mr. Remke."
 "Mr. Horton, please be on time while in my court."
 "Yes your honor."
 "How does he plead?"
 "Nolo contendere, your honor. The boy has no prior convictions. He mined the coal from abandoned state land and intended to sell it to the underprivileged, below market value. He transported the bags within the state only, in his personal car, therefore not violating any interstate laws on conveying bulk bootleg coal."
 The judge skimmed some pages in his folder and motioned to the attorney.
 "Please approach the bench, Mr. Horton."
 I watched their heads bobbing and hands flicking, as if engaged in some sort of contest about who could be the most animated. At some point they stopped and the attorney stepped back.
 "Mr. Remke, on behalf of the State of Pennsylvania, I sentence you to one year probation and pay a fine not to exceed one hundred dollars. If at the end of that year you have not committed any other crimes, all charges will be expunged from your record. Dismissed."
 I stood there for a moment, my head swimming from what had just happened. I could have ended up in jail. No one ever anticipated this.
 Mr. Horton shook my hand as we walked out to the lobby. He motioned to a seat, pulled a tablet out of his brief case and started writing.
 Your father will not be happy when he gets my bill. I don't like to take on cases with only two hour's notice. Walter gave me your background over the phone. Lucky for you, I knew the judge and we worked it out.
 "Sir, what does probation involve?"
 "If you get into trouble for anything, speeding, jaywalking, spitting on the sidewalk, over the next year, you will go direct to jail. Understand?"
 "Yeah. I get it. What happened to my father? He promised to be here."
 "I don't know but he must have had a good reason."
 He tore a page out of his tablet and handed it to me.
 "I listed the address of the court and secretary. You owe the state eight dollars and thirty three cents on the first of every month for your fine. Don't be late. Well, good luck young man and give my best to Walter."
 "Uh, thank you for everything."
 He nodded, then stood up, walked to one of the other courtrooms and disappeared inside.
 I stuffed Mr. Horton's information in my pocket and stepped to the lobby where the same guard watched my every move with a critical eye.
                                                            ****
 At school, my afternoon seemed to creep by, like a barge on the Schuylkill River. I needed to confront Pop. What could have been so important that he failed to be there for me?
 So empty, lonely, angry. Those painful feelings washed over me as I parked the Chevy at home and waited for Pop.
 I sat in one of the old chairs and faced the front door, listening for his car. When Pop motored into the driveway, I stood up, waiting for him to come across the transom.
 "Where the hell have you been?"
 "Simmer down, boy. I just got home."
 "You left me there. No doubt, I've been a lousy son. In fact, a complete disappointment and don't deserve your support. But at least tell me up front. Don't send a lawyer in your place."
 Pop took off his hat, sat down without saying a word and stared out the dirty front window. I felt bewildered, expecting a stinging response. But instead, he seemed withdrawn, lacking the energy or desire to argue with me.
 Awkward. Uncomfortable. I stood there without purpose. My role as his adversary ripped away. Now, I wanted to make excuses for my outburst. Apologize somehow without sounding stupid.
 "Mr. Horton saved me from jail. If he hadn't been there, I might be in the county lock up right now."
 For the first time in my life, I noticed tears in his eyes. My brain screamed, noooo! Something wrong, ungodly, must have happened. I felt afraid to know and stepped back. My world tilted, swinging back and forth, out of balance while sirens wailed and bells clanged. Bur only in my head.
 "Your mother. Uh. The doc called me. So I drove to Harrisburg Refuge this morning. Oh God. She needs help. Uh. More than I thought."
 A long period of uneasy silence passed before I questioned, "Did she have a spell?"
 He exhaled a sigh and lamented, "She, uh, tried to kill herself. Your mother punched through a window and cut her wrists with the glass. She lost so much blood before they found her. A few more minutes and she'd have been gone.

Chapter 39
 The smell of disinfectant filled the lobby as Pop and I followed a prim looking nurse to the hallway. She led us past an array of somber men in white jackets escorting what looked like patients in gray uniforms. Some perched on wheelchairs while others shuffled like war wounded.
 Our guide directed us to a room, looking like all the others until I saw my mother. They only allowed one visitor at a time so Pop stayed in the hall. I walked to the bed, grabbed Mom's hand and noticed her wrists bound to the side rails by thick straps. Tubes ran to different parts of her body and bags of liquid hung from an assembly of hooks.
 I kissed Mom on the cheek and sat next to her. She looked so pale. We stared at each other for a moment then she struggled to talk. I leaned over and tried to understand her whispers.
 "Help me. Every day. Drugs, needles, crazy people. I can't be here No more, please."
 I tried to control it. But hot, wet tears rolled down my face without permission.
 "Mom. We need you. I, uh, I'll find a way to get you out of here."
 My head filled with visions of breaking in and rescuing her.
 She tried to smile and shook her head.
 I wiped my tears before anyone noticed.
 "Pop wants to see you next. Just hold on, Mom. Please, don't give up."
 I hugged my frail mother goodbye and backed up to the door. My promise seemed overwhelming. How will I ever make good on it?
 Driving home, neither Pop nor I seemed to have any passion for conversation. Once my father set a course, he never changed. Asking him to get Mom out of that loony bin would be useless. But I had to try.
 Breaking our silence, I exclaimed, "She can't stay there."
 Pop announced so calmly, it scared me, "They haven't finished looking her over yet. The doc told me his nurses will take precautions so it can't happen again."
 "What kind of precautions?"
 "Hell, I don't know. Maybe keep a nurse with her all the time. Something like that."
 "They had her tied up."
 "Yeah?"
 "You should do something. She deserves better. Animals get tied up, not human beings."
 "I don't like it any more than you but the docs know best. Face it, boy, Harrisburg Refuge might be the end of the line for her. But I hope, maybe, they can help her in the long run."
 "Not from what I've seen"
                                                            ****
 Arriving home, we saw two Pennsylvania State Police cars parked in front of the burned out shell. Several men in coveralls emerged through the open doorway with some sort of equipment in their hands. Pop walked over and started talking with them.
 I hoped their efforts would find the truth, whatever it might be. But most of all, I wanted them to prove my mother innocent.
 Standing by Pop's station-wagon, I watched the stateys fire questions at him. They seemed so intent on interrogating my father, my presence appeared to be irrelevant to them.
 It didn't last though. The police turned toward me and Pop motioned to come forward. I felt myself flinch, caught in the spotlight of attention.
 "Come here, boy. These gentlemen want to talk with you."
 One of the older stateys divulged, "The school principal confirmed your presence at Civics class the morning of the fire. We need to look at this case from every angle. Do you have any enemies? Someone who has a grudge and might want to torch the house?"
 I felt so steamed up. Hell yes, I had enemies. But Pop couldn't see beyond his damn obsession with Mom as the arsonist.
 "Yeah. I know two people who would be happy to see our house go up in flames.
 "Anna Michek threatened me at the beginning of the school year. She promised to get revenge somehow for working with her father, uh, up on St. Mary's Mountain. Yeah, I know, it doesn't make any sense. But her craziness about the whole thing leads me to believe she might be delusional enough to do it.
 "Also, Ace Borman, the bastard who ran me off the road several weeks ago. Before that, in front of my friends at school, he promised to put a hurt on me. The guy has no conscience."
 It came easy. Explaining all the details of my antagonists. Did they burn our house down? Maybe the police could find an answer before I extracted my own revenge.
 But most of all, I wanted Borman put away, to pay for all the grief he caused in these mountains.
 The officers took finger prints from areas that escaped incineration and informed us they planned to run them through the state files. I hoped they might identify someone, anyone, who didn't belong in that house.
 After they finished, the stateys packed up their equipment and bags of evidence. Pop talked with them for a few more minutes, shook their hands and watched as the entourage rolled out onto the highway.

Chapter 40
 My father and I tried to resume our lives or what seemed to be left of our pitiful existence. Pop left for work early and came home late, sometimes stopping by the VFW until after midnight. I concentrated on school and yearned to start bootlegging again.
 The threat of going to jail for selling illegal coal loomed in the back of my mind like a dark, ominous cloud. But I pushed it deep into the recesses of my thoughts as if it didn't exist. I kept telling myself it could never happen again.
 With only a few more weeks until graduation, I hadn't even thought about taking someone to Prom. At least not until one of the girls in my class asked me. I had known Shelly, a cute brunette, since ninth grade.
 I could never tell her about my bondage to another human being. Reese may have turned her back on me but I had never let go. I'd become a lost soul, unable to move on.
 Trying to let Shelly down easy, I explained how mining would consume all my time. On Prom night, I expected to be loading coal into my car for the next trip over South Mountain. A lot of poor families in New Ringold Valley depended on the anthracite I delivered.
 She managed a smile over her disappointment. "Oh, I thought since you and Reese broke up, well, you know. So, you don't want to go at all? Not with anyone?"
 "Yeah. I just want to keep things simple in my life right now. Listen, you won't have a problem finding someone else for Prom. And I know you'll have a great time."
 I felt chafed with regret. Life strutted by, thumbing its nose at me. Beyond my control.
                                                            ****
 On Saturday morning, I drove the familiar, old route to Kooba's farm. Green mountains dominated the horizon on both sides of the valley and an early summer had started to warm the air.
 His house looked the same with its faded white siding and gray slate roof. Grass had overgrown ruts in the front yard, the only clue that a driveway existed there.
 I pulled in and knocked on the front door, not knowing if friend or enemy would answer. Floorboards creaking inside worked their way toward the front door.
 After a moment of silence, I heard a latch turning and saw a shadowy figure in the entrance. As someone stepped forward, the morning sunlight illuminated Kooba's smiling face.
 "Good Morning, Vance. You look good for someone who's been through hell."
 "Thanks, Kooba. I never thought of it that way. But maybe I do."
 "The Tamaqua Courier had an article about you going before the judge. I apologize for not coming to the court but it would have gone bad for you if they knew we worked together.
 "Back in '56, I spent thirty days in the county jail for trucking bootleg coal to New Jersey. The law does not like us crossing state lines."
 "It doesn't matter. The court let me off easy. They just want me to stay out of trouble for a."
 A loud shot rang out, interrupting my conversation. And an adrenaline rush overwhelmed me as I looked for a safe place.
 Kooba and I both fell to the porch floor and looked back and forth, searching for the direction of gun-fire. My eyes focused on the enemy I had been trying to avoid, coming toward me.
 "Get the hell off our land, you son of a bitch!"
 Anna, red faced and yelling, poked the front end of a double barrel at me like a broom stick. She favored her right arm. A sign, for sure, Anna didn't know anything about shooting the gun. They give quite a kick.
 Kooba leaped off the porch toward her, "Anna, have you lost your mind?"
 "I told him to stay away, Papa. Make him leave."
 Grabbing the shotgun from her, he commanded, "Go to your room, right now."
 She yelled past him, "I warned you, Remke. I warned you."
 After venting her poison, she scurried away, disappearing behind the house.
 He walked toward me, carrying the gun and shaking his head.
 "I think she has become obsessed with you. Please accept my apology."
 "Don't blame yourself, Kooba. I should have called instead of just showing up."
 "Well, we both know why you've come. But so much has changed, Vance. What with claim jumpers and your run-in with the law. And what about school? Won't you graduate soon?"
 "All of that just doesn't matter right now. Bootlegging coal and selling it seem the only things that won't let me down."
 Kooba rubbed his chin, appearing deep in thought. A serious expression on his face.
 "We can check out the mine on Sunday morning while the girls go to church."
 "Okay. I'll see you there by eight."
 It felt so strange, as I headed back home. Anna had tried to shoot me. I should have been afraid but all I felt inside remained this nagging urge to go back up the mountain.
                                                            ****
 Pulling up my driveway, I saw Pop on the front lawn holding his bricklayer's tape measure. Three wooden stakes had been driven into the ground and after careful measuring he started to pound in the fourth with a sledge hammer.
 Walking up behind him, I questioned, "What goes in there?"
 "A trailer. I can't wait any longer. Our damn insurance won't settle until the police submit their findings. And if they prove your mother, well, it'll come out of savings.
 "An outfit down in Allentown promised to deliver a twenty-footer in the next two weeks. At least we'll have hot running water, toilets and a. furnace before winter."
 "What do you plan on doing about the house?"
 "Nothing. It can sit there until forever. I'll let someone else worry about fixing up the hulk."
 I helped him shovel dirt from the rectangular area then he checked it with a level. When he felt satisfied it looked true, I raked the surface of rocks, clods and tamped it firm.
 My forehead glistened under the mid day sun when I finished. We broke for lunch, making baloney and cheese sandwiches. Pop had a beer and I drank soda.
 I had to tell my father about Sunday, so I waited until he downed his meal and lit a cigarette. He pushed back on his chair and inhaled deep, then propelled the smoke high in the air.
 "I'll be checking out the mine tomorrow. If everything goes okay, I plan to start up next week."
 Pop took another drag. After a long silence, he stood up and mashed his smoke in an ash tray.
 "I've tried to be reasonable but this bootlegging thing has a strangle-hold on you. Your mother kept me from saying a lot of things on my mind. But she can't stop me now.
 "You graduate in a few more weeks. Come that time, you have two choices, go to college or join the service. Hear me, boy. In June, you will pick one. If not, I promise to pack your clothes and throw them out on the road.
 "And if you screw up probation, you're on your own."
 Shocked, my senses ground to a halt. I had been drowning in bitterness for so long that my world looked like scorched landscape without hope or future.
 Soon, my life would change. I accepted it.
 "Yeah. I'll make a decision. But not because of you."

Chapter 41
 Route 209 seemed abandoned on Sunday morning as I left Tamaqua and headed for St. Mary's Mountain. To my thinking, most people had already gone to church or slept in.
 I tuned the radio until one of the up-state New York stations came in. Gene Pitney filled the air with a mellow ballad.
 Without warning, a Ford pulled up behind me and rode my bumper. I strained to see who sat behind the wheel but couldn't make anyone out.
 Reaching behind my seat, I grabbed a tire iron and set it next to me. If Ace Borman had the gall to show up, I'd make him sorry.
 I slowed down to forty around the curves and held it there on the straight away. Both hands on the wheel, I looked for a place to pull over.
 The car shot around me like a bullet and continued to speed down the road until it disappeared from sight.
 Could it have been Ace? Maybe, playing a game of hunter and hunted. Until I knew for sure, the tire iron stayed on the front seat.
 As the roadway crept by, my eyes darted left and right, looking for that Ford. I imagined Borman parked in some secluded spot ready to smash into my car, trying to kill me.
 Fifteen minutes later, the Mary-D gas station came into sight. Exhaling a long sigh, I started to think, perhaps, my fears drove me to the brink of paranoia.
 My fuel gauge sat near empty so I pulled up to one of the pumps and filled the tank. Pulling five dollars from my wallet, I headed inside where the attendant operated his cash register.
 "That will be four fifty, young man."
 Handing him my money, I asked, "Have you seen a black '49 or '50 Ford coupe come through here?"
 "Hmmm. Can't say for sure. A lot of cars have been out this morning."
 "Well, I'd sure be interested if you ever did."
 Ringing up my gas, he pushed two quarters across the dirty, top glass of his display case. I pushed them back.
 "Keep the change," I suggested.
 The old man responded, "Check with me next time you come in. If it stops here, I'll let you know."
 "Thanks."
 I started walking to the door.
 "Hey. Young man. I almost forgot. Kooba's old pickup passed by in a hurry for the mountain road about thirty minutes ago. I swear, he almost took the corner out front, on two wheels."
 I left the station and started up the same road. At first, nothing seemed wrong. But as my thoughts wandered, it seemed odd. Kooba favored his beat up truck from the first day we met. He never speeded.
 Following the macadam as it wound through the hills of slag, I parked in the usual place, at the base of the mountain. Kooba must have driven further down the road because I couldn't see his pickup anywhere.
 Ignoring an odd feeling in the pit of my stomach, I slipped on my jacket, grimy with coal silt and grabbed the tool box. Slogging up the worn path we made from our many treks to the mine, memories clicked in my head like pages of a photo album. Most of them good recollections, the first load of coal mined with my own hands, customers who became friends, the wisdom I gained from Kooba about bootlegging and life.
 As I reached the top of St. Marys, something seemed different. The sky, unblemished, filled in a solid blue. Birds chirped and a slight breeze tasseled leaves about, just like they should, on a good day.
 But something in the air, almost unnoticeable, smelled sweet. I must have been downwind of it. Deer had a musky odor and roamed the mountain but not so much in the summer. This scent didn't come from an animal.
 I moved with slow, light steps, looking in every direction. When the diggings came into sight, I scanned the horizon for Kooba. He should have been there, waiting for me. But the camp appeared abandoned.
 The old hoist sat motionless. Fresh rust had started to form on the cable, threading its way down the shaft. A layer of coal dust still covered the ground. A reminder of the three years we cracked anthracite into small pieces with nothing more than a pick hammer.
 Setting my tool box on the ground, I knelt down to open it. As I sorted through the wrenches, a voice behind me, young and angry, sliced the air.
 "Stand up, Vance."
 I obliged the command and turned to face the hostile owner of those words.
 "Ewa?"
 "No. Zofia."
 "Hmmph. I never could tell you two apart."
 "It doesn't make any difference."
 She held a shotgun, muzzle toward the ground, like an extension of her arm.
 "What the hell, Zofia. Do you plan on shooting me?"
 "You had to know this day would come. Anna told you, plenty of times, to back off but you kept dragging Papa back here. She sounds tough but doesn't have the guts to stop you for good."
 "Listen. Listen. You need to think this out. Your father will be here any minute. He'll take a switch to your hide for this."
 "Hah. My father drove Anna to Coaldale Hospital. She came down with, well, a convenient illness. And such good timing on her part, just before Papa planned to meet with you. Mama and Ewa don't know and it'll stay that way.
 "I'll bet he won't come here until tomorrow. By then you'll be sleeping forever at the bottom of the strip mine just over that ridge line. It'll be like you never existed."
 "It won't work, Zofia. The old man at the gas station saw your father's truck this morning. He told me just before I came up here."
 "Yeah? I should have parked my father's truck in Tuscarora and walked through the woods. Thanks for telling me about the guy at Mary-D station. I'll see him on the way out."
 "Jesus Christ. Kill me, then him? You just turned, what, fourteen? I don't believe a word. Now give me that gun."
 As I stepped forward she snapped the safety off and pointed that smoke wagon at my face. Looking at both barrels from the wrong end, an ice cold chill coursed through my body and breathe evaded me.
 "I can bring a pheasant down before it gets two feet off the ground. If you come any closer, I will execute you. Then the surprise will be ruined."
 Staring into her eyes, my voice grated," What? Don't play games with me, girl."
 "Consider this a farewell present. I planned on breaking in and trashing your place, last month, but never got further than the laundry room. Somebody out front started yelling, crazy stuff about ghosts. By accident, I discovered some lighter fluid, torched the laundry and got out of there. It felt good, setting your house on fire. Bet you didn't have the smarts to fig."
 I screamed, at the moment of my insanity. Lunging at her to extract revenge, gripped my brain like a vice.
 My ears filled with a loud explosion, like standing close to a cherry bomb on the Fourth of July.
 It felt weird. When the haze cleared, I saw sky and Zofia, looking down at me. My senses came into focus when I raised my head but couldn't get up. Then all attention turned to the pain coming from my left side. A big circle of wet red seeped through my jacket, heaving up and down with each breath.
 "You lucky son of a bitch, Remke. Look's like it'll take more than a chest full of buckshot to finish you off. I give you credit though, it showed some spunk, trying to take the gun away from me."
 I struggled to reach a hanky from my jacket pocket in a desperate attempt to stop the bleeding. Pushing my hand inside, I found salvation, not from God, but my enemy.
 Zofia looked calm as she raised the stock of the shotgun to her cheek and took aim at my head.
 "Any last words, Remke."
 I struggled to whisper, "Stop. Last chance."
 She chuckled, "I thought you'd beg for mercy. Guess I figured wrong. Goodbye."
 How odd, the trigger had almost no resistance and the shot coming from the muzzle sounded anemic. Not like a real pistol.
 As a small waft of smoke floated skyward, Zofia dropped her shotgun, a startled, unbelieving look on her face. My hand, holding Ace Borman's Derringer, pointed up toward her chest. His pistol, forgotten until now, saved my life.
 She fell, the full weight of her body hitting the black grime with a thud. Zofia laid a few steps away, her face turned toward me. Her eyes open and staring into nothingness. I managed to pull myself within arms length and tried to shake her. Begging Zofia to wake up.
 Buckshot must have penetrated my lung because each breathe seemed more labored. Time lost its importance as visions of Mom, Pop and Reese filled my mind. So much undone yet so weary, a bone deep tired. I welcomed sleep.
                                                            ****
 Noise. Annoying shouting. Someone lifting, jostling me about. Disturbing my peace.
 "Boy. Hey boy. Look at me. Don't close your eyes. Stay awake."
 Trying to speak, I muttered, "Pop? How did you find me?"
 "Mr. Michek called. But we can talk later. Right now, I need to get you off this mountain."
 Grabbing my father's sleeve, I asked, "Zofia?"
 I observed several men kneeling over her limp figure. They seemed frantic, pressing on her chest, checking for a pulse. And Kooba, standing just behind them, a pained, lost expression on his face.
 Pop shook his head, "I don't think she made it."
 As tears slid down my cheek, I grieved, "Zofia set the house on fire, not Mom. Not Mom."
 Seconds later, drifting in and out of a mental haze, I sensed tree limbs brushing against me followed by the wailing of a siren. Somewhere in my nether world, I experienced the ultimate conflict. They carried me into a place with the brightness of a thousand suns. Yet after a nurse prepped me for surgery, everything went black.

Chapter 42
 Blinking, struggling to see; an illuminated white ceiling came into focus. A thick gauzy bandage covered my chest and the sterile backdrop of a hospital ward surrounded me.
 "Vance? Can you hear me?"
 "Mom?"
 With great satisfaction, I recognized my mother standing next to the bed.
 "Thank God. You've been unconscious for the past week."
 "How? How did you get here, Mom?"
 "Your father brought me. I've been at your side every day since the shooting.
 "He never questioned it, when you told him Zofia set the house on fire. He marched into Harrisburg Refuge that afternoon and told the doctors to release me.
 "Several days later, the police confirmed she committed the arson when they matched Zofia's prints to the ones taken off the back door."
 My mother looked haggard and bandages still covered her wrists. A reminder, Mom tried to commit suicide only a few weeks earlier.
 "And Pop? When can I see him?"
 "Oh, he went back to work today, after the doctors took you off the critical list.
 "That poor soul, Mr. Michek, wanted to drop by but they say you can't have visitors yet. He knows what happened, dear. When the hospital couldn't find anything wrong with Anna, he demanded answers.
 "She confessed to cooking up a vile plot with Zofia to get rid of you. Anna sits in Schuylkill County Jail. She's been charged with conspiracy to commit murder.
 "Mr. Michek doesn't blame anyone but himself. You acted in self defense. The police told him, from the angle of entry, you had to be lying on the ground when you shot Zofia."
 I spent the next several days struggling to make sense of it all. Choices made and their consequences. This self examination left me exhausted but determined to make peace with myself and move on. To where, I didn't know. Outside of these mountains? Maybe? But could I just leave everything behind?
 The next day, friends from school and neighbors came to visit. Kooba stopped by after Zofia's funeral with a copy of the church program. We cried together for her short life. No blame, anger or regret. Only sorrow. Before he left, we hugged and promised to keep in touch regarding Anna's trial.
 During the day, I slid out of bed and walked through the ward. My breathing seemed better and the wound started to itch. A sign that I could go home soon.
 Surprises have a way of being uncomfortable events. Nothing prepared me for the sight of Reese walking toward my bed. I felt cold and stiff as she leaned over and kissed me.
 "I don't know what will become of you, Vance Remke. One of your friends told me what happened. I came right away."
 She stroked my unruly brown hair, a wistful look on her face.
 "You look good, Reese."
 "Thanks. What about your wound? Will it heal okay?"
 "Yeah. The doctors say I'll be able to leave in a few more days.
 "Can we talk about us getting back together? Those mountains, I'll never go back. Not after what happened."
 Silence, followed by more silence. She stared at the floor as if deep in thought.
 "Our time has passed, Vance. I've been seeing someone."
 "Who?"
 "A guy from school. You wouldn't know him."
 "Listen, I don't blame you for dating other guys, after we broke up. Looking back, I acted like an idiot. But what about all that's happened between us? We have something special. Nothing will ever change that."
 She sat on the bed and looked at me, her gaze unyielding.
 "You won my heart a long time ago in spite of your restless yearning for danger.
 "And I know the boy in my life will never be you. But he just wants to be a carpenter when he graduates and stay in Tamaqua. I need someone predictable, safe. No matter how hard you might try, you will always be risking your life or future on something."
 I wrapped my arms around her, kissed those sweet lips and told Reese I loved her.
 She kissed me back, not a peck but the kind of intimacy between two people who share a soul.
 Wiping at the tears from her sad eyes, she whispered, "And I will always love you."
 She slid off the bed, held my hand for a moment and without another word, walked out of my life. It seemed like neither one of us had the will to say goodbye.
 I wanted to scream, plead, beg for her to come back but the harsh reality of her words came home to me.

Chapter 43
 Several days later, I walked out of Coaldale Hospital into the warm air and took a deep breath. Some pain still aggravated my lung but I forced it aside as my father pulled his station-wagon up to the curb.
 Opening the door, I glided into the passenger's seat, careful not to make any quick moves.
 "Your mother wanted to come but she decided to fix lunch for us instead."
 "That'll be nice, just the three of us. Like it used to be."
 Pop drove the short distance through city streets to Route 209 and turned left, headed for Tamaqua.
 For the first time, I scanned the horizon with different eyes. The slag heaps and coal pocked mountains no longer held any fascination for me. I felt lost with nothing connecting me to this place except those poor souls who bought my coal. They would always be in my heart.
 As I pondered all the events that brought me here, my father broke his silence.
 "Someone at the police station called. They said you might be interested to hear, your tip led to the arrest of Ace Borman. The cops found him hiding at his parent's house on a weekend, just like you said.
 "They booked him on hit and run charges as well as the rape of a school girl. Maybe you know her, Cassie Hanks?"
 I sighed, "Yeah. I do. Good. At least he won't hurt anyone else."
 "We need to discuss your friend, Mr. Michek. He and I had a long talk about what happened. You faced a terrible choice. Zofia died so you could live. Yet he holds you in high regard even after the pain of losing his daughter.
 "I've learned that a bootlegger can be an honorable, straight up man. He doesn't want you to fester over what happened and I agree with him."
 "Pop, how can I ever forget Zofia and the mine? Memories haunt me every waking moment. Just the sight of these mountains, remind me of all the pain I've seen over the last few years.
 "Every thought in my head says to leave this place so I've decided to join the Army. Can we stop by the recruiter's office on the way home?"
 He consoled, "Sure - son."
 As we entered the town limits, I looked at my father and one more time imagined him in his soldier's uniform. A handsome, young man with blond hair and a thirty eight pistol strapped to his hip.
                                                            ****
 Pop and I arrived at the old rental several hours later. Mom hugged me and kissed my cheek. It had been a long time since I noticed her so happy.
 We sat around the card table while Mom served her favorite, pot roast, boiled potatoes and green beans. Most times, she made the roast for dinner but not today. This lunch celebrated a coming home for all of us.
 Pop talked about the new trailer which would be delivered in a few days and Mom boasted of planting Geraniums in the front yard.
 After lunch, my father headed back to work. Mom and I washed dishes and cleaned the kitchen.
 Later, we stepped outside to the front yard and sat on the swing. As we rocked back and forth, in that quiet moment, I told her about my trip to the recruiter.
 She turned toward me and reached out to touch my cheek.
 "I knew you would leave someday, just like your brothers. How long do you have?"
 "They gave me a week to put my affairs in order. Pop wouldn't let me sign any papers. At least not until the recruiter straightened away concerns about my bootlegging probation.
 "I'll visit Mrs. Delk tomorrow and see if she can drop by in the mornings, just like before. You have Pop's phone number at work. Anything else you can think of?"
 "I'll wash and pack some clothes for you, dear."
 "Thanks, Mom."
 I scanned the horizon over South Mountain and lamented, "That mountain has been there as long as I can remember. It towers over the valley, unchanging, observing the good and bad in people's lives. I wonder if it knows how much my life has changed."
 She patted my shoulder, pushed the swing back and forth and proclaimed, "Let's go visit Aunt Cleona."
 I nodded, knowing she had just slipped away from me. Losing my mother again to a fantasy world of her own making. There would not be a trip to visit Aunt Cleona, except in her mind. I suspended my own reality to join her on the road to Cumberland and pushed the swing a little harder.
 "Okay. Okay. But I'll drive."
                                                            END
